THE RED PAGE
The Tin Wreath
A Plea for
Purtell.
What’s the matter with James Purtell ?
Nobody has mentioned him up to now ; yet
he has many, if not all, the
attributes put forward on be-
half of other claimants for the
tin chaplet. The only man
who runs him close is Grant—or should it
not be Grand ?—Hervey, with whom he has
many points of resemblance. ,Harrison
Owen demands the bauble for Hervey on
the grounds that he is a patriotic Australian,
a staunch democrat, and a number of other
estimable things. Jimmy Purtell is all
these and a few over. Moreover, he has
made his name solely by delivering the
poetic goods, unaided by any such adventi-
tious advertisements as magnificent tailoring
or injudicious and execrable shooting.
Purtell is a modest poet. Only once in his
long and illustrious career has he put off his
bays to appear before the public, and that
was when The Bulletin forced him by unjust
criticism to protest. On that occasion, James
did not enter into any long and elaborate
polemics to prove himself the real tiling.
He came straight to the point by telling The
Bulletin u Anyhow, I can get a living out of
poetry, which is more than any of your high-
toned poets who write about the birds and
the blossoms and the breezes can do.” The
rebuke was effective. The Bulletin, which is
willing to “ take on ” any political or social
giant, retired to its lair before the pregnant
logic of James Purtell. The poet was right.
He is the only one of his profession in
all Australia who lives by his verse. He is
truly the poet of the people. His moral
tone is high, his advocacy always cast on the
side of the weak. He reaches the great
heart of the public, just as Eugenie Duggan
does on the stage. You lay down your Pur-
tell feeling a better man. Moreover, his
poems are so phrased that he who runs may
read. Y r et his work is not without its deli-
cate imagery. Look at the dainty play upon
the metallic name of Mrs. Needle, the Mel-
bourne poisoner, when he wrote :
You horrible woman, oh ! how can you feel ?
Your brain is of iron and your heart is of steel.
But as a rule he gains his effects by broad,
impressionistic efforts, by the hyperbole
which has ever been the most valuable asset
of all the poets from Homer down. When
Martin Bourke, the cross-country jockey,
was killed in action, Purtell penned his
threnody :
The truest, bravest Irishman the world has ever
seen.
People*paid a penny for that poem and wept.
They knew well enough that the description
of the deceased jockey was not literally cor-
rect, but so direct was Purtell’s appeal to
the emotions that for the moment they over-
looked the exaggeration. They even forgot
that only a few months previously Purtell
had penned a similar requiem about Tommy
Corrigan—probably the greatest of all his
works in point of popularity. On the day
Corrigan’s funeral passed through the streets
of Melbourne, Purtell’s poem was sold by
runners on the fodtpaths, and for weeks the
demand at local newsagents caused the issue
of edition after edition. It is really question-
able whether any poem ever written in Aus-
tralia was so widely read, and when it is re-
membered that it was not purchased as part
of a mass of other literary wares in a news-
paper, but had to be got alone for good red
copper, the full greatness of the poet Purtell
becomes manifest. Purtell’s works are
healthy in tone and, unlike those of many
claimants for the Laureateship, may be in-
troduced into any household. Even when
he touches on a delicate subject he does so
without coarseness or suggestiveness. Take
his work on the Melbourne boot-trunk
tragedy, written on the arrest of Madame
Radalsky and young Tod. His was a
pathetic picture of the wreck of the dead
girl’s home, and the actions of the offenders ;
and four eloquent lines ran :
Just think of her parents, their awful position ;
Just think of that girl—in a certain condition—•
Just think of Radalsky and sweet little Toddy ;
Just think- of the way they disposed of the body.
Neither Maupassant nor the Greek trage-
dians turned out anything more simple or
direct in its pitiless insistence than
Just think of the way they disposed of the body.
Purtell’s sturdy democracy does not need
mention. All things considered, I claim
that I have made out a strong case for James
Purtell. The tin wreath should certainly go
to somebody with nice hair, and Purtell’s
curls a lot more prettily than even Hervey’s.
H. GLOBB.
The Dying
Gladiators.
Now that the Battle for the Bays is bang-
ing along, I put out my thumb over the edge
of my flower-hung box and
turn it down upon all of the
combatants. Upon all—ex-
cept Arthur Adams. Quinn
lies faint and calcareous, bleeding into the
sand, with one blue eye glued on the clouds
through a hole in his chaplet ; Bayldon
babbles of Bayldon on the point of his own
sword ; Jeflbott struggles and kicks, his
trident bent like a toasting-fork, and his
strong muscles swelling and squirming in a
net of inarticulation. Lawson is dead whde I
speak. Paterson runs hither and thither with
a pair of large blinkers hooked about his
temples ; he also has kneecaps on ; he foams
at the bit, but to no purpose ; he is the slave
of the redoubtable Skuthorpe. Brennan
booms like a buzz-hy with a pin through his
body, and Grant Hervey squats under a
hoarding blowing a brass trumpet while
Pegasus feeds on the straw in his flair.
Harry Sullivan, squashed by the camel of
mysticism, will soon be quietly rolled away
to a carpet emporium ; while O’Dowd,
patted on the back by Professor Tucker
and Murdoch, makes the air creak with the
noise of his choking. Brereton and Hebble-
thwaite die with their arms round
the neck of a mutual white lamb.
McCrae disappears down the throat
of a Lindsay illustration, and Brady
crawls on the top of a corned-beef cask, his
eyes thick with accumulations of salt as he
begs like a wooden-legged sailor for the
wreath that may never be his’ra.
Alone, alone, amid the red Aceldama,
exhilarated and pink, the Pale Page Poet
waves his rancid sword—where’er he sticks,
a victim falls. He heeds not their cry for
mercy, but, “at the centre, silent and full-
fed,” gathers their wreaths to be a bonfire
that he may warm his feet.
WILLIAM ANDERSON.
Legends of the Kelt
“In the old time,” writes Lady Gregory,
in her Book of Saints and Wonders (Murray),
‘ ‘ the people used to be looking at the moon
and at the sun and the rest of the stars,
travelling and ever-travelling through the
day, and at the flowing and ever-flowing of
the world’s wells and rivers, and at the sad-
ness of the earth, and the trance and the
sleep of it with the coming of the winter,
and the rising of the world again with the
coming of the summer. But it was all
like a head in a bag to them until
such time as Philip the Apostle told the
whole story of the making of Heaven and
earth at the great gathering in the east of
the world.” And in these tales “put down
according to the old writings and the memory
of the people of Ireland,” Lady Gregory
shows us the Celt still looking at the moon
and the sun and at the flowing and ever-
flowing of the world’s wells. The author is
doing great service to the Irish renaissance
in gathering up the material out of which
Synge and Yeats write their plays and their
poetry. The miracles herein recorded are
homely but useful, quaintly told. There is
much about Saint Brigit, the first “slavey ”
—whose glory has, unhappily, lately been
overshadowed by Saint Mary Ann. Of
Saint Brigit, Gregory tells :
“ W lien she was a poor girl she was mind-
ing her cow one time at the Curragh of
Life, and she had no place to feed it but the
side of the road. And a rich man that
owned the land came by and saw her, and he
said, ‘ How much land would it take to give
grass to the cow V ‘As much as my cloak
would cover,’ said she. ‘ I will give that,’
said the rich man. She laid down her cloak
then, and it was spreading out miles and
miles on every side. But there was a silly
old woman passing by, and she said, ‘lf that
cloak goes on spreading, all Ireland will be
free ’ ; and with that the cloak stopped and
spread no more. And Brigit held that land
through her lifetime, and it never had rent
on it since; but the English Govern-
ment have taken it now, and have put
barracks upon it.”
And here is another quaint tale :
“There was a beggar boy used to be in
Burren, that was very simple like and had
no health, and if he would walk as much as a
few perches it is likely he would fall on the
road. And he dreamed twice that he went
to Saint Brigit’s blessed well upon the cliffs
and that he found his health there. So he
set out to go to the well, and when he came
to it lie fell in and he was drowned. Very
simple he was and innocent and without sin.
It is likely it is in Heaven he is at this tim*.”
More in anger than in sorrow, T. P.
Deagan is requested to stop. The Rescue of
Victoria, the Beautiful Nihilist (Hearne .and
Co., Melbourne) —a compound of preposter-
ous language and equally preposterous adven-
ture—ends with the threat that the author
intends to follow in Australia the adventures
of his characters “in our next volume.”
He is hereby warned.
Wilde
QUITE the wrong sort of biography is
that of Oscar Wilde (Laurie),
written by Leonard Cresswell Ingle-
by. The biographer’s only claims
to his position are a personal friendship witli
Oscar Fingal O’Flahertie Wills Wilde, and a
painstaking study of his works. Against
those useful assets must be placed his com-
plete lack of critical ability, his dulness, his
tedious detail. The work may stand as a
sort of over-bulky primer of Wilde for the
kindergarten. Ingleby’s methods are in-
stanced by his careful compilation of contem-
porary skits upon Wilde, and his biblio-
graphy of all the references to him in that
dreary paper, Punch. He even inflicts
upon us Mrs. Wilde’s views of house
furnishing. His attitude is in turn pat-
ronising and grovelling ; and his lack of the
critical instinct is throughout shown by his
ludicrous dismissal of Shaw as a person of no
importance. And the critic is often sub-
merged beneath the religion of the critic.
But the book has its uses. Ingleby gives
an interesting account of Wilde’s life ; and
he subjects his plays, poems and prose to an
analysis whose minuteness of scrutiny may
be of use to those for whom the less-known
works of Wilde ate inaccessible. In his
slovenly style Ingleby tells a story about
Salome :—-
“Sarah Bernhardt had taken the play
back to Paris, promising to produce it at the
very first opportunity, a promise that was
never fulfilled. Moreover, when, a couple of
years later, Wilde, then a prisoner awaiting
his trial, finding himself penniless, sent a
friend to her to explain how he was circum-
stanced, and offering to sell her the play out-
right for a comparatively small sum of money
in order that he might be able to pay for his
defence, this incomparable poseuse was pro-
fuse in her expressions of sympathy and
admiration for ce grand artiste, and promised
to assist him to the best of her ability. She
had the cruelty to delude with false hopes a
man suffering a mental martyrdom, and after
buoying him up from day to day with pro-
mises Of financial assistance, the Jewess, not
considering the investment a remunerative
one, shut the • door on his emissary, and
failed to keep her word. Now that the foreign
royalties on play and opera amount to a
considerable sum of money annually, her
Hebrew heart must be consumed with rage
at having missed such ‘ a good thing.’ ”
v»>
Of Wilde’s other plays, excluding the four
inimitable comedies, little need be said.
The Duchess of Padua, and Vera, or The
Nihilists (a melodrama) seem, from Ingleby’s
plot analyses, imitative and immature.
Indeed, in poem and prose, Wilde seems
always curiously swayed by the style of
other writers. But his prose remains a
thing of beauty, though, like all beautiful
things, it is “precious.” A passage such as
this is delightful in its careful choice of
words, its balance of phrase :
“We are always apt to think that the
voices which sang at the dawn of poetry
were simpler, fresher, and more natural
than ours, and that the world which the
early poets looked at, and through which
they walked, had a kind of poetical quality
of its own, and could pass, almost without
changing, into song. The snow lies thick
now upon (fiympus, and its scarped sides are
bleak and barren, hub once, we fancy, the
white feet of the Muses brushed the dew
from the anemones in the morning, and at
evening came Apollo to sing to the shepherds
in the vale. But r this we are merely lend-
ing to other ages wfiac we desire, or think we
desire, for our own. Our historical sense is
at fault. Every century that produces
poetry is, so far, an artificial century, and
the work that seems to us the most natural
and simple product of its time is probably
the result of the most deliberate and self-
conscious effort. For Nature is always
behind the age. It takes a great artist to
be thoroughly modern.”
For the student of a complex genius,
whose faifie has triumphantly and swiftly
grown from the moment of its apparent
absolute extinction, this book is worth the
perusal. However, it is meant chiefly for
the kindergarten class. 83.
♦ — —-
W. H. Koehel, the author of an excellent
account of Modern Argentina, promises well
in his Maoriland yarn, The Anchorage
(Bell, per Dymock’s Library, Sydney). The
story is of the redemption of one of the many
English “ bad eggs ” shipped off to Maori-
land. On an up-country station this failure
finds anchorage, and in that haven he gets
his chance to regain his lost manhood. The
story is of the conventional ty,pe, but written
with a first-hand knowledge of local condi-
tions, and commendably devoid of sen-
sationalism. F 3.
The Simple Life
Five o'clock. Father has just got up. jr„>
never later than five.
Comrade John (Macmillan, New York), by
Merwin-Webster, the joint authors of the
crisp American business-novels, Calumet
K. and The Short-Line War, is as brisk, and
modern, and original as those earlier suc-
cesses. The yarn is built about a new re-
ligion ; and of new religions America has an
output almost equal to that of millionaires.
Hall Caine in real life is a little man,
whose blazing eyes and blazing hair suggest
a spluttering firework. The present writer
once saw him very sea-sick, and even then
lie looked like a damp green cracker. A
bust of him in the comic-opera Manx
House of Keys strongly suggests Shake-
speare’s physiognomy—a very diminutive
and spluttering Shakespeare. It is told of
Caine that on an admirer remarking to him
that he was “really very like Shakespeare,
the little red-hot man replied: “I have
been told there is also a physical resem-
blance.” And the critics wonder why he is
a “ best-seller ! ”
Mixed
Farming
“Jim!” A grunt.
“ Jim ! Time to get up.” Muffle
sounds of displeasure. Jim i s bis
hard to shift. Father passes on nd
, ... , ~, on.
Aliek !
“ Yes, pa ! ”
“ Five o'clock ! ”
“ Right, pa ! ” Alick rolls Over and snores
“ Peter ! ”
“ Orlright, father ! ” and Peter leaps out Pnu.
is the eldest.
“Bill!” No answer.
“Bill 1”
“What?”
“ Get up ! ”
“Orlright ! ”
“Bill !”
“ What ? ”
“ Get up and bring in those jolly cows. Smart
now ! ” ’
"Orlright, orlright, orlright! ” And Bill, still
half asleep, bounds out and commences with furv
to tear himself into his clothes.
By the time we walk blinking into the kitchen
father has the tea made and chunks of home-made
cake cut. Bill dashes down his tea, seizes some
cake, and goes forth for the cows. Father and
Peter discuss Hogan’s bull, and drink tea till t'hev
hear, “Bail up there, Strawberry. Come here
dog. Gome here you ! ” Then they go out
and start milking.
About an hour after Jim flounders into the
kitchen, drinks a couple of cups of tea in sips, and
stumbles sleepily out with his milkcan. There
are 40 cows to milk, and we work doggedly
Father and Peter maintain a volley of comments'
and Bill listens open-mouthed and contradicts
whenever possible. Jim is somewhat deaf and is
not often addressed; for the cows take loud talk to
apply to them alone and become restless.
Breakfast at 8. Pig’s cheek bacon, mild cured
tea, and the usual sundries. Mother is worn
thin in the service of the nine of us, and her bovs
are a great joy to her, but the girls are handy only
as grumbling-butts._ The three of them walk in
just as we are finishing, and receive a stormy
welcome from mother.
Mother reads a portion of Scripture while the
rest of us gaze vacantly at the ceiling and walls.
There is silence for a minute after the reading',
and then father rises, and on his way to the door
remarks, “ I reckon Joshua and those Israelites
were fair brutes.”
We cart cocky-chaff all day—sun heat some-
where about 170°. We don’t talk much, for a
peck of dust in the throat is the reward of ver-
bosity. Layer after layer of black chaff-dust is
sweated into grime, and there is a goodly deposit
of chaff inside our shirts round our middles.
Dinner is brought out at one—two-gallon tin of
tea, mutton and bread.
At four father knocks off and goes home to
spruce himself up for the township. Peter
watches him out of sight, and then proposes a dip
in the forbidden dam at the bottom ot the scrub.
Carried unanimously.
There is about two feet of water in the dam.
You can't sink above your knees in the black
mud, for at that depth old kerosene tins and mis-
cellaneous scraps make a firm if uncomfortable
bottom. After half an hour of awkward move-
ment we get out and scraps the mud off us, and
dry in the air.
At seven we are back at thecowsand the separator
—the eternal cows and the eternal separator. It
only puts through 12gallonsan hour, and we take till
10 to get done. Two or three neighbors come up
with their milk for separating.
Alick and Bill (the kids) feed the calves by
lantern-light in a new galvanised-iron shed. The
quality of the air in that shed is beyond descrip-
tion ; but they ride the big bull calf round and
round till father comes in and spanks them.
Tea at 10 p.m. We all go to sleep round the
table when it is over, and some of us snore. Father
wakes up suddenly and shies a piece of cake at
Bill, who is sleeping with his mouth open. Bill
howls, and the family awake, and make off to bed.
All except father, who goes out to try and get
the butter to come. ’Tis a stifling night, and the;
butter doesn’t arrive till 2 a.m. Then with a
sigh, father goes into the oven-like house for
three hours’ sleep. There is a light burning in
our room. Father looks in and sees Jim sleeping
on his knees. “ You're very devotional to-night,”
says father, giving him a clout fit to send him
through the bed. Jim rises and creeps silently into
bed. Father blows out the Randle.
It seems only a moment after when “Five
o'clock, Jim !” sounds, not as tunable as lark to
shepherd’s ear.

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Ut in Principic.
I wonder in what forgotten age, in the spring of
the daedal earth,
Ere man had come to his heritage, our souls first
found their birth.
Were the wild woods wet to our naked feet when
as boy and girl we strayed,
With the scent of the grasses rising sweet through
the steam of the tangled glade ?
Did I lie, stretched close, to secure the game
through the slow, hot afternoon,
Till the spear smote home like a shaft of flame, j
and the skin at your feet was strewn ?
Have you quite forgotten the day we passed in
the shade by the jungle pool,
And I turned on a sudden and held you fast, and
you gave me yourself in full ?
And you crouched at my side in my hut that night
with your sombre eyes afire,
And your limbs were flushed in the ember’s light,
and your mouth was a red desire.
And we fought when the hairy foemen broke in a
shouting, surging tide,
Till our strength was spent with the wounds and
smoke, and we kissed a last kiss, and died !
For the cycles change, and the world grows old,
and the white moons wax and wane ;
But the amarantli scatters her seeds of gold—and
love returns again.
If the whole wide world were melted down and
turned to a single gem,
And the burning sun to a golden crown, with the
stars for an anadem ;
If the fire of living were set in a cup like a draught
of dazzling wine,
And I knew I had only to drain it up to be one
with the gods divine ,
I would spurn the jewel, and scorn the wealth—
what use were it all to me ? —
And pour at thy feet the foaming spilth that I
could not drain with thee !
For Heaven is Heaven, and Hell is Hell, where the
swords of space divide,
But Heaven were torment unspeakable, with
memory at my side
Of thy oval face and thine eyes, dark brown,
where the love-lights gleam and grow.
Like fires of ocean that pass deep down on nights
when no winds blow ;
And thy soft small hands that clasp and meet, and
the kiss of thy curling hair —
I should leap at the last from my silken seat, it
were more than my soul could bear !
For Heaven is Heaven, and Hell is Hell, where
the pathless gulfs divide :
But I’d reckon my throne lost wisely and well, if
I gained in Hell thy side !
N.S.W.
F. S. BURNELL.
Ploughin’.
Cuttin’ furrows long an’ deep
(O an’ how the cool winds pass !)
While the skylarks skyward sweep
From the rustlin’ meadow-grass ;
Music notes without alloy—
Little gloom are birds allowin’ —
O, I tell you, it is joy
Ploughin’.
What care I for stuffy town,
Shiny pavements, roadways white !
Give me paddocks, hillocks brown ;
Give me fresh air—l’ll be right !
Perched upon me old machine
To no man on earth I’m bowin’;
Cares ? We dunno what they mean
Ploughin’.
It may rain—er course it may,
But we take it in good part;
If ther sun shone ev’ry day
Crops ’ud never get er start !
Seems to me some folk I know
With Dame Nature’s alius rowin’—
We don’t care if it should snow—
Ploughin’.
An’ when work fer day is done
There’s a lassie to be kissed—
As the stars shine, one by one,
They can see us at the tryst!
And when she becomes my wife
(Little laughin’ Bess McGowen) —
Why, I’ll simply sing thro’ life . . .
. . . Ploughin’.
MAOKILAND.
TE AROHA.
The Shadow and the Song.
I met a pleasant fellow
When the red was on the leaf,
And he sang in gallant measure
Of a world that knew no grief.
The road was lit and golden,
And the birds were in the trees,
And his voice—a silver bugle—
Sounded gaily down the breeze.
I watched him as he carolled,
And I said : “ His lines are drawn
To a golden-hearted sunset
From a crimson-hearted dawn.
“ He was born to many favors,
And his life-miles sure must wend
From a rich and rare beginning
To a rare and radiant end.”
But a sudden shadow gathered
'■*' As the noon to evening wore,
And he passed into the shadow,
And I heard his song no more.
N.S.W.
RODERIC QUINN.

changed since the

A ND we do not do
things now as we did
50 years ago. Science has
become domesticated and
is working all the time to take'
better care of the everyday wants of man. those things
we use most have been improved to the greatest extent.
Take, for instance, paints. A few years ago the best
paint was mixed by hand, by rule of thumb, llie material,
a paddle and a bucket, were all that was necessary. If
the materials were pure, a fairly good paint was the result.
But to-day we have a much better paint, It covers more
surface to the gallon, it looks better and lasts much longer,
because it is prepared on an exact scientific formula, the
result of careful laboratory and practical tests, and is
ground and mixed by machinery specially made for this
purpose.

improving its quality, and it is to-day as good as they know
how to make it —as perfect a house paint as it is possible
for human ingenuity to produce.

By sending direct to Tub Bulletin Offce Twenty-Six
Shillings sterling you can have. Tint Bulletin posted
regularly to you every week for a year.
Cheques, Drafts, etc., and all Business Communications
to be addressed to William Maclkod, Managing Director.
Hoiv to Address Betters.
Correspondents to The Bulletin would oblige the paper
(and, to an extent, themselves) by taking some care as to
methods of address.
Business letters (with regard to subscriptions, advertise-
ments, etc.) should in no case be addressed to the Editor,
but to the Manager. To send them to the Editor is to
invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters to
anyone by name. “ The Editor, Bulletin,” is the proper
superscription for “copy." Delay, and. even loss, is
sometimes caused by addressing to persons instead of
officials. The person may not be there ; the official
always is.
Special Entice.
Insufficiently stamped letters are liable to be refused.
The contents of this paper are copyright by the pub-
lishers. The right to reproduce or translate any part of
the contents of this number is hereby expressly reserved by
the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and, where practicable,
acknowledge in the “ Correspondence ” column all contri-
butions submitted, whether in the form of Political,
Social, or other Articles, Verse, Short 'Tales or Sketches
(those dealing with Australian subjects, and not exceeding
twc columns in length, or, say, 3000 words, are specially
acceptable), Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper Clippings.
All communications will be regarded as strictly confi-
dential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when the
address is specified and stamps are enclosed But no
MS. will be returned, or preserved, unless these conditions
are observed. When MS. is forwarded ivithout stamps
for return it will be understood that the copy is to be
destroyed if not acceptable. Personal applications for
return of MS. cannot receive attention. The Bulletin
desires, further, those sending MS. to distinctly under-
stand that it is not responsible for the preservation or
transmission of MS.
To Amateurs in Hlaclc. and II hite.
Anyone supplying to The Bulletin original humorous
or other topical drawings in line or wash, of sufficient
merit and interest and adapted for direct reproduction, or
any interesting photograph illustrative of a current event,
will be paid for same on acceptance. Any picture con-
taining a good idea, but unfitted for direct reproduction
— i.e., any picture ivhich has to be fe-drawn —ivill be paid
for as a suggestion only. The conditions under which
such artistic contributions ivill be received are those
applying to MS.

THURSDAY, MAY 7, 1908.
LABOR, AND THE AUSTRALIAN CAPI-
TAL, AND OTHER MATTERS.
concern. But it said nothing and threw no brick.
Passively it preferred to be a joint in the Wade -
Carruthers tail. And it has made considerable
sacrifices in order that it might be passively, if
not actively, provincial; that it might be a joint
in the Tory anti-Australian tail; that it might
avoid going against its bitter enemies, the Sydney
dailies; that it might avoid damaging the Tory
gang led by Carruthers and Wade, even if it
didn’t actively assist that gang; that it
might speak no word worth hearing or recording
in defence of Australian national sentiment aud
in support of the most democratic Parliament
the world at present possesses. It might have
got thousands of votes that never before came its
way if it had declared itself the pro-Federal party
in State politics. The result of the Australian
elections shows that there is a very strong anti-
provincial feeling in N.S.W., and though it has
hitherto had no chance to express itself in State
politics, because neither Wade nor McGowen

The attitude of the State Labor parties, especially
the Labor party of N.S.W., towards the Federal
capital question, and towards the State Frights
craze generally, is a bewildering feature of Aus-
tralian politics. The Labor party is the political
body in which, above all others, a strong Federal
feeling might bo looked for. It is continental as
no other party is, with its powerful representation
in the Australian Parliament, and its representa-
tives in all the State Legislatures, and its confer-
ences and leagues and other bonds of union, that
nominally keep the State and Federal Laborists
in line. Its policy is, on paper at least, essentially
the same all over the Commonwealth. There-
fore it was fondly imagined that Labor would
think in continents—that it would think
with a sort of unanimous think, and that its
thoughts would be large ones, and that it would be
an active force in the good cause of Nationalism, as
against Provincialism. The hope is still alive,
but it isn’t the hope it once was.
In N.S.W. especially, the attitude of the State
Labor party has been a meanly passive one. It
has professed to consider that such questions as
that of the Australian capital are purely Federal
matters, and are, therefore, none of its business
and wholly off its beat, just as if the Federation
was a foreign matter, in which N.S.W. had no
concern. The Tory party in the State Legisla-
ture has made every Federal question its business,
and has put an impudent and disreputable paw
into every Federal concern ; and it has harassed,
annoyed, and insulted the Australian Parlia-
ment and Government at every turn. The
State Labor party has made no serious
protest, and offered no assistance on the
other side. It might have taken up a
Federal attitude on the ground that the can-
tankerous, annoying and hostile manner of the
Carruthers-Wade crowd was a bitter insult
offered by State Toryism to the most democratic
Legislature in Australia ; or it might have thrown
at least one brick in the Federal and all-Australian
interest on the ground that the State Government
was interfering with things that were not of State
THE SNOWY RIVER ,
Below Dolgety, the Federal selection for the Capital of the Australian continent .
ing to be called for. The N. S. W. State Labor party
preferred to lose these votes rather than come de-
finitely out of the parochial rut, and break
with Wade and the Daily Telegraph, and
say a good word for Australia. And the
party might have come into power if McGowen
had moved a vote of censure on the Carruthers
Government over Joseph’s insane wire-netting
insurrection, but it, or its leaders, preferred to
keep out of office rather than be Federal and
honestly Australian.
* * * *
The general attitude in State politics is that it
is the business of the Tory party to denounce,
vilify, obstruct, and contemn the democratic
Federal Parliament; to harass it with silly little
insurrections and law proceedings and such-like ;
and to hire men at public expense to overhaul ali
proposed Federal laws in search of pegs upon
which local Toryism can hang bogus complaints
and groundless law proceedings. And somehow
it isn’t the business of the Labor party to
do anything at all on the other side
of the battle or combat or difficulty.
Federal legislation, and proposed legislation,
meets in a general way with the approval of the
N.S.W. State Labor members. They couldn’t do
other than approve of the White Australia policy ;
of the anti-Kanaka movement; of the New Pro-
tection, which seeks to secure for the worker
the tariff benefits which have hitherto been re-
served mostly for the employer; of the proposed
all-Australian old-age pensions; and of almost
every other act of the Australian Parliament,
down to the prohibition of the enormous grain-
bag which for so long has bowed the shoulders
or broken the backs of the State Labor party’s
own constituents. But while the State Labor
party approves of all these things individually, it
comes no nearer to being an enthusiastic general sup-
porter of the Australian Parliament, which does
these things—its supporter against the blatant
insolence and impudent encroachments of Tory
State politicians of the Wade stamp. Even when
Wade proposes to spend State money—some of
it the coppers of the wharf-lumper himself—in
trying to prevent the Australian Government
taking the murderous 3501 b. sack off that wharf-
lumper’s shoulders, the State Labor party seems
to get no nearer to being the Federal and anti-
Parochial party in the State Legislature. It still
remains the party that threw away almost the cer-
tainty of office—the office that it had been striving
after for 16 years —rather than vigorously denounce
the lawless ruffianism of a crowd of Tories, when
that lawless ruffianism had the merit of being an
insult thrown at United and Democratic Australia.
So it comes to pass that in the matter of the
Australian capital the State Labor party of
N.S.W. sits on a fence—the usual fence. The
capital question is the business or job or meddle-
someness of every State Tory politician and every
Tory paper, so the anti-Australian cause is well
represented in N.S.W. State politics. With
Carruthers and then Wade to consolidate all
the Tory influences in the State Legislature on
the old business of obstructing, denouncing and
humiliating the democratic Parliament of all Aus-
tralia, there is a fairly solid phalanx on one side
of the question. And the State Labor party,
which should be the other side of the question,
simply says that the matter is off its beat. Yet
if the party’s leaders were not blinded by the
narrowest kind of parish limitations it is difficult
to see how any question could be more dis-
tinctly on the Labor beat. The Australian
Parliament has shown a warm and generous
sympathy for the workers, such as most of the
State Legislatures were never even suspected of.
The Federal city and territory will be under in-
dustrial laws made by this Parliament, and it is
fairly certain that these will be the best industrial
laws in Australia. The Federal Territory will be
free from the curse of the ground landlord, for the
Commonwealth is going to be its own ground
landlord. It will almost certainly be run on in-
tensely sober lines, for the Australian Parliament
is almost a teetotal institution. Ardent Federal-
ists want to plant this Federal city where it will
become, by reason of its situation and its abun-
dance of cheap water-power, a great manufactur-
ing city, so that as many workers as possible
may live under the Federal Parliament’s
ideal industrial conditions, and so that
Australia may demonstrate, as clearly as
possible, that profitable industrialism doesn’t
necessarily mean slums, dirt, narrow streets, tene-
ment houses, bad smells, larrikinism and misery.
In other words, the true Federalists of Australia
want to justify the creed of Labor, and the
State Labor party in N.S.W. that
it doesn’t matter to the party whether the enance
to j ustify its creed is thrown away or not. Ardent
Federalists desire to have a Federal territory ot
fair size—sooo square miles would be a very
moderate area—so that there may be room to set
forth a noble and noteworthy example
elimination of the private ground landlord,
which is an essential feature of the Labor
creed. Out of N.S.W.’s 310,700 square miles
the excision of 5000 square miles wouldn't even
begin to be noticed ; it would only represent a
twelfth the area of England, and England could
be lost in a> corner of N.S.W. Yet when Toryism
howls against the suggested ‘‘sacrifice” of terri-
tory, the State Labor party, instead of taking up
the question with enthusiasm on the other side,
reckons that it doesn’t see how the question con-
cerns it. The State Tory party clamors for a little
dry capital which, by reason ot the scanty water
supply that would have to b® pumped over a
range of hills, and the total absence of water-
power, could never be an example of successful
industrialism run on good Labor principles; and
it howls for a small scrap of Federal territory,
where it really wouldn’t matter whether the
private landlord was eliminated or not, so trifling
would be the area affected. On this subject the
State Tory press fights in the thick of the battle.
But the State Labor party keeps out of the fray.
It prefers to sacrifice its principles rather than
sacrifice its parochialism. The affair isn’t on its
beat.
The various proposals that have been made for
a Federal city at Lyndhurst, Bathurst, Yass, Moss
Vale, or Canberra, or in the backyard of JOHNSON,
M.H.R., somewhere about Botany, were all
schemes for keeping the new capital under the
influence and control of the Wade crowd, of the
Sydney importers, of the Telegraph and Herald
—in fact, of all the influences that have always
been anti-Labor. Always it was to be a small
CANBERRA.
The site of N.S. W. State Premier Wade's proposed
Capital—the Capital of a whole continent.
capital, with no great influence or population or
distinctive life of its own, with no powerful press
of its own—a mere shadow of Wade and the
Daily Telegraph and the York-street com-
mercial gang. Against this was set the idea of a
city nearly half-way between Sydney and Mel-
bourne, far enough away from each to be
free from the evil influence of Wade and
the Telegraph on one side, and of Bent and the
Argus on the other—a place which would have a
chance to be great, which would be run under the
best industrial laws that Australia could devise,
and which would be the world’s first great modern
experiment in anti-landlordism. The State Tory
party and the State Tory press have turned over
every stone, and disturbed every insect under-
neath, to defeat this idea. The State Labor party
said the affair wasn’t its affair. A large section
of the State Labor party once awoke to vast
enthusiasm over the question of building a city
in Paraguay, in the land of Rue Australia ; but
it is very difficult to rouse it to any fervor over
the building of a city in its own country.
It is not suggested for a moment that the
somnolent McGowen and his flighty and fatally
eloquent evil genius, Holman, should be driven to
turn good Australians against their consciences.
If their parochialism is so vast and incurable that
they would conscientiously sacrifice the ideal—
because it is an all-Australian ideal—of a great
Federal oity, run on purely democratic principles
by the most democratic Parliament on earth, and
of aflourishing Federal territory from which private
landlordismhad been wholly eliminated, ratherthan
give their cordial support to a Parliament that
doesn’t belong wholly to N.S.W. and turn
definitely against the policy which has for its ex-
ponents the Telegraph and Bruce Smith, and
the nigger labor party, and Wade, the man who
is now spending the workers’ money in defence of
the 3501 b. corn bag—well, they must obey their
consciences. It is merely suggested that the
N.S.W. State Labor party should sack them re-
morselessly, and get new men with different con-
sciences. The Labor party has representatives in
every Legislature in the Commonwealth, and it
purports to have practically the same policy all
over the Commonwealth, and if its leaders in
N.S.W. can’t “ think Australian ’’ then they are
not fit to be in such a party. The N.S.W. Labor
men made one grievous mistake when they didn’t
fight to the last ditch against that gross act of
treachery by which the N.S.W. State Legis-
lature raised the minimum vote required
to carry Federation from 50,000 to 80,000,
and thus broke faith with the other
States, in which there were also Labor men with
whom it was worth while to keep faith. They
made another grievous mistake when they failed
to throw their whole weight into the scale against
George Reid, when he came back from the Pre-
miers’ Conference at Melbourne with the glad
news that he had swindled all the other Laborists
of Australia out of any chance of having the
Federal capital in their territory. They are
making their third gigantic mistake now, with the
theory that Federal matters are really of no con-
cern to the State Labor party of N.S.W., and
that, though N.S.W. is the greatest of the Aus-
tralian States, yet Australia is something apart
from N.S.W., and hardly worth its consideration.
The N.S.W. State Labor crowd has thrown Aus-
tralia overboard, and beyond that it is hardly
possible to go.
CORNSACKS, AND THE FOOLING AWAY
OF STATE MONEY, AND THE CAN
TANKEROUSNESS OF STATE PRE-
MIERS.
The Conference of State Premiers had scarcely
got together when it tackled the cornsack pro-
blem, and its members gave way to great excite-
ment (barring Premier Kidston, of Queensland,
who seems to have kept his head) over the matter.
This trouble is a complicated one. The gigantic
size and weight to which cornsacks had grown
was a public scandal. Lumpers died, or became
crippled for life, through struggling with horrors
that were more like pyramids than ordinary bags.
They appealed for assistance to the callous or
loafing State Governments, but for all the satis-
faction they got, they might as well have cried to
Baal and Ashtoreth. The State Governments
could have refused to carry the bloated monstrosi-
ties by train, and could have shut them off
their wharves, and prohibited their removal
in carts ; also they could have passed
an Act to fine any man £lO every
time he put more than 001 b. of wheat
in a bag. They had abundant power,
but they did nothing. Then the
humane and democratic Federal
authority took a hand. It has full
control over inter-State and foreign
commerce, and it did its little part by
prohibiting the import or export of
wheat bags calculated to hold more
than 2001 b. And in that it did a
kindly and noble action which should
stand to its credit on Judgment Day,
and which should make every decent
citizen rally to its support. But a
curious legal question has been raised
by certain cantankerous State Pre-
miers. They admit that the Austra-
lian Parliament can legally shut out
the bloated and murderous wheat
bag, by levying an impossible duty-
say 100,000 per cent.—on it, but they
profess to have doubts whether the
Federal authority can legally shut it
out by simply proclaiming it a pro-
hibited substance, though the statute
says distinctly that ‘"all goods, the
importation of which may be pro-
hibited by proclamation.” shall be
shut out. " But however clear the law
appears to be, there is always one
chance in 1000 that it may be ruled
out on a technicality or a side issue.
And on that one chance in 1000 the
State Premiers (or the majority ot
them) decided to spend good State
money in testing the legality of the
Commonwealth s action,
* *
It is necessary to consider the rela-
tion of the States to the wheat-bag
problem in order to appreciate the
meanness and cantankerousness an
childish insolence of this action, in
the first place, the State I rentiers
fully admit that the purpose which
the Federal authority had in vie\
was an honest, honorable an
humane purpose. Even the mos
callous of them can’t deny that 20010.
is a sufficiently heavy load forany 1
to carry, hour after hour, often on an upm
on a treacherous, springy board ; and tha
3001 b. or 3501 b. or 6101 b. cornsack (there
a 6101 b. cornsack) is just about plain mu •
The State Premiers can’t deny, either, that tn
Australian Government waited a fairly .
to see if they would act before it took a ha
the matter itself. Further, they don t pio
deny that the Australian Parliament P
sents the people just as much as ,
State Parliaments. Consequently, if the P,
tackle the bloated cornsack trouble in a
way, per medium of Deakin, Lyne and O - - ■
they are governing themselves just as m u , •
they tackled the same trouble in a State' j
per medium of Wade, Bent, Moore, E- - ~
co. And it is much easier to tackle '
Australian question in one act throug
authority, than to deal with it in six ms '
through six authorities. Also, as alrea y
tioned, these remarkable and multitudin
Premiers make no attempt to deny ,
Australian Parliament has power to
the monstrous wheat-bag by a P 1 im e
duty, though they allege, on what > ok
no evidence at aH, that it exceeded P
bv shutting out the same article y ,
ting it in the category of forbidden g


Yet, if it did exceed its powers by so doing,
it didn’t thereby annex any powers belong-
ing to the State authority, for even the maddest
State insect doesn’t profess that the States have
authority to shut out goods either by proclamation
or by tariff regulations. The case of the State in-
sects is based simply on the allegation that the
Australian Government, while attending strictly
to its own business, and doing so apparently with
the complete approval of the majority of the Aus-
tralian Parliament and people, did the thing one
way instead of doing it the other way ; and they
hold that it is the business of all good State in-
sects to watch for cases in which the Australian
Government can be annoyed or obstructed on
technical points, and to spend the public money
lavishly in proving that the Federal “i” wasn’t
properly dotted. If the Federal authority was
the same kind of soured and silly fool, and hired
an officer to watch for technical flaws in State
legislation, and scattered public money in testing
the legality of, say, the proposal to indemnify the
councillors of some Sydney suburban municipality
for borrowing money contrary to the Act, the
State insects would probably begin to see the size
of their own dirty little insolence. But the Aus-
tralian Government takes a man’s size in souls,
and doesn’t do things like that.
* * * •
The Premier or chief State insect of N.S.W. has
already spent a good deal of the people’s money in
getting legal opinions to prop up his theory that
the Federal Minister of Customs has done the
right thing the wrong way. Every adult in
N.3.W. has already paid a trifle towards the
cost of expensive legal opinions, obtained in
the hope that Wade might be able to gratify
his small animosity by proving that the Federal
authority has done the right thing the wrong way.
And it has been decided to spend more money,
and to wrest a copper or two from every adult in
Australia, in order to prove at law that the
Commonwealth, in attending to its own business,
which includes the regulation of foreign and
inter-State commerce, made a small technical
error. The chances are 1000 to 1 that the
attempt will fail, and if it succeeds, the
Federal authority will simply do the same
thing the other way, and shut out the
gigantic grainsaek by putting a prohibitive duty
on it instead of by putting it among prohibited
goods; or else it will pass an Act to make the
prohibition legal. The people, in their State
capacity, will pay Costs, thanks to Wade and co.,
to try and prove that in their Federal capacity
they exceeded their powers under the existing
Act. The people, in their Federal capacity, will
be compelled to pay Costs, thanks to Wade and
co., to prove that they didn’t exceed their
powers. At the most they will learn at great
expense, either (1) that they have more State
power and less Federal power than they thought
they had, or (2) that they have just as much State
and Federal power as they thought they had.
They gain no extra power either way. It
isn’t even professed that they will have the
3001 b. wheat bag restored, for Wade and his
friends promise that if, at great expense to the
people, they can abolish the Federal regulations
on the subject, and bring back that horror, they
will immediately take action to wipe it out them-
selves—the action they wouldn’t take before, when
Australia waited for them to do something, and
waited in vain. The only gain will be to the
microscopic vanity of Wade and co., who, if they
have quite unexpected luck, may bark rather more
loudly than usual at the Federal door. The people
will get less joy out of the business than they
would if the same money were spent in proving
that the dog regulations at Timbuctoo were ultra
vires. In that case Australia would only pay half
the Costs while the municipal authorities at Tim-
buctoo would have to meet the rest, while in
Wade’s case the Australian people have to pay
all the Costs—those of plaintiff and defendant
alike. And, in the case of the dog regulations at
Timbuctoo, the winning of the case would at least
give Australia the satisfaction of bowling out a
foreign nigger, and proving that he didn’t know
his own business. In the cornsack business
success will only mean that the Australian people
will have heaped mud at great expense on them-
selves by heaping it on the Parliament that they
elected and the Government that they created.
It is time that it was explained to these State
Premiers that the public funds of Australia were
not entrusted to them that they might use them
in the satisfaction of their wretched little grudges
against the representatives of Australia. If Wade
and co. think they have been slighted or dimin-
ished in any way, they should go to law about it
at their own expense.

Bent's Courtesy.

Again the Post Office.
The “disorganisation” which Federation has
brought to the Post Office is of the same general
character as the “ruin” which it has brought on
Sydney. You can see the “ ruin ” of Sydney at
almost every street-corner—the tumbling bricks of
old houses which are being demolished to build
new palaces. You can see the decay of
the Post Office in almost every page of the
Postal Guide, in the shape of a reduction of
rates or increase of facilities. Since Sydney is
the headquarters of the outcry against Federal
postal administration, compare the N.S.W. Postal
Guide for 1900 (State control) with the N.S.W.
Postal Guide for 1907 (Federal control). Begin
with British and foreign mails. (The anti-
Federalist is always particularly concerned with
British and foreign mails: they tell him of the
part of the world which concerns him most.) In
1900, the postage-rate to Great Britain was 2Jd.
per |oz ; in 1907 it is 2d. In 1900, letters could be
posted for England up to 4.30 p.m. on mail days ;
late-fee letters up to 5.30 p.m. (at the G.P.0.),
extra fee, 2£d. ; special late-fee letters (at the
railway station) up to 6.45 p.m., extra fee, sd. In
1907, British letters can be posted up to 5.30 p.m.
without late fee ; up to 6.15 p.m. for a late fee of
2d.; up to 7.30 P.m. (at the railway station) for
the same 2d. So the anti-Federalist, with his
letter to England, bewailing the misery of living
in the colonies—allowing that his tears keep him
to the latest posting times—fares this way :
Before Since
Federation. Federation.
Cost of Joz. letter to London,
special late fee .. .. 7£d. 4d.
And Federation allows him an extra three-quarters
of an hour for tears. On the same page of the
Postal Guide as gives this information will be
found, in 1907, the code of signals displayed at the
G.P.O. to announce storms on the coast. That is
lacking in 1900 ; it is one of the new iniquities of
Federation. The late fee on inter-State and
inland letters in 1900 was 2d. In 1907, under the
Federation, it is Id. Then, consider these special
facilities which the Post Office allows in 1907
under Federation, and which it did not allow in
1900 under State control. Postmen must collect
letters for the post from householders if asked.
The Post Office has an express delivery service, by
which articles can be delivered express at very
low rates. Letters may be marked “Express,”
and, if an extra 4d. in stamps is affixed, will be
delivered by telegraph messenger on reaching the
town to which they are posted. The sender may
further endorse his letter “ Wait reply,” add 2d.
postage, and the express messenger will wait on
the addressee 10 minutes for a reply. Firms may
arrange to have all their mail delivered to them
express on payment of small fees. Mail trains
with travelling mail officers will carry ex-
press letters to any railway station at which
the train is timed to stop. Thus the Sydney anti-
Federalist, after he has sent his lamentation to
England on the evil of living in the colonies, may
hie him to the railway-station post office, fill
pages there with the bitter insults of Victoria
until 7-50 p.m., and send the letter express to
some Melbourne citizen, confident that he will
get it within a few minutes of the arrival of the
train at the southern capital. Not to weary the
argument with too much detail proof: In 1900,
under State control, the Post Office in N.S.W.
was an institution which did its obvious work
with reasonable efficiency ; in 1907, under Federal
management, it is an up-to-date institution, with
largely reduced rates, which is always worrying
around to give the public some new facilities.

Are We Dissatisfied?
Every now and again this country is afflicted with
a fresh heap of Deakin-Wade correspondence
about the Federal capital. There is a terrible
sameness about this correspondence, and any one
sample is very much the same as any other two
dozen samples. The latest batch finds Gregory
Wade holding forth as follows :
Reading your last letter in conjunction with the earlier
comm unication of the 3rd March, the position is clear
that you are unable in this important question to speak
on behalf of the Commonwealth Parliament. In view of
the specific and clear nature of the questions put by me
on the 6th March, I am forced to conclude, by applying
the usual canons of interpretation to the terms of your
reply, that a definite offer by the Parliament of this State
on behalf of New South Wales of the site they prefer will
not be acceptable to your Government, or helpful to them
in their deliberations.
I need hardly say this was unexpected. It is, more-
over, very disappointing to me, who had hoped for co-
operation on your part ; and it will not tend to remove
the aggrieved feeling that now exists, unfortunately, be-
tween this State and the Commonwealth on the subject
of the capital site.
That brilliant statement suggests at least one help-
ful idea. How would it be if the Deakin Ministry
offered Wade a reward of £lO,OOO for the produc-
tion of any reasonable evidence that would show
that, say, one-tenth of the people of N.S.W.
have an aggrieved feeling, or care one battered
penny-piece about Wade’s silly Federal capital
row. By all appearances, that Wade-Carruthers
shindy is the most ridiculous confidence trick ever
played on a guileless and innocent people. Cer-
tain daily papers lash their tails wildly over the
matter, and strive to howl themselves and their
readers into fury, but nobody really seems to care.
There are no meetings, no petitions, no proces-
sions, no rising, no excitement whatever. After
the daily papers have howled in 25 leading
articles about the Wrongs of N.S.W. in this
matter, they sometimes evoke, and sometimes
don’t evoke, a letter from one correspondent—the
same old faithful correspondent —who urges
N.S.W. to secesh, but that sad, lonesome,
desolate person hardly amounts to a large popular
movement, or a boiling fury, or a fried devil, or
anything of the kind. There used to be about
six correspondents, but five of them were
misled by the habit the daily papers had
of telling them that the Constitution con-
tained all manner of provisions that it didn’t con-
tain, and that it never dreamt of containing.
When they learned how they had been deceived
they dropped out. Now one man, who writes to
Sydney Telegraph at very long intervals, repre-
sents all the visible “aggrieved feeling” that
exists outside the offices of the Sydney dailies.
And what really exists inside these offices isn't
so very apparent. Presumbly, the tremendous
and dignified proprietors, who never condescend
to go down into the highways and by-ways and
inquire into popular sentiment, think that there
is some large feeling of woe and injury; but the
members of their staffs, who are, as a rule, cheer-
ful, manly fellows, greet the delusion with a howl
of merriment when it is mentioned to them out-
side. In a casual way they seem to regard their
proprietors as a herd of amusing lunatics.

The Stinking Fish Party.
[For The Bulletin.]
Man, the Master, gifted rarely, from the most
unlikely things
Fashions forth the perfect image of his soul’s im-
aginings.
Out of cold implastic marble statues wonderful are
hewn ;
Simple notes of music mingled make the dear
dream-haunting tune ;
Fibred canvas, grey of surface, drab and unin-
spiring quite,
Which an Artist paints his soul on, may become
a world’s delight;
The rare Poet weaves an idyl, out of words of
every day,
That shall charm men’s souls for ages after his
has passed away ;
So the Engineer and Builder, so the Potter with
his bowl,
Body forth the secret yearnings that torment the
artist soul.
Mother Nature (who is Beauty) doth her alchemics
employ,
To transmute by wondrous process harshnesses to
things of joy.
Out of colorless beginnings—dawns unpromising
and cold —
She will fashion summer noontides, evening skies
of rose and gold ;
On the tortured storm clouds drifting, drifting so
dejectedly,
She limns forth her rainbow glories with supernal
artistry ;
Tiny seeds flung earthward harshly in sere
Autumn’s last sad hours
Blossom, when shy Spring returneth, into promise-
laden flowers.
On the modest rush-girt lakelet, crooning o’er its
tiny bars.
She imposes this fond mandate: “Mirror thou
the lonely stars ! ”
Mother Nature (who is Beauty) doth her alchemics
employ
To transmute by wondrous process harshnesses to
things of joy.
Man, the Master, gifted rarely out of most un-
likely things,
Fashions forth the perfect image of his soul’s
imaginings.
But, nor Man nor daedal Nature, though they
have the DIRT at hand,
Can make consciences for fellows who’d betray
their Native Land !
MAORILAND. SINC. S. CLARK.

“ Gimme Back Me Member!”
There is material for a new row in Federal circles
—in fact, it is a wonder this particular row didn’t
come earlier. When the Commonwealth opened
for business in 1901, N.S.W. had 26 members and
Victoria 23. According to law, the distribution
of members alters as the balance of population
alters, but then a question arose—was the mem-
bership to be adjusted every ten years, when the
reliable census figures were available, or was it to
bob to and fro, and up and down, on the mere
annual estimates —possibly unreliable estimates —
made by the State Statisticians ? After a few
years these estimates showed that N.S.W.’s popu-
lation had increased so much faster than that of
Victoria that N.S.W. was entitled to one extra
member at Victoria’s expense. So the N.S.W.
members declared that there must be no
waiting for the definite figures of the census,
and that the guesses of the respective Statis-
ticians were good enough. Tbe thing be-
came a State grievance, and- N.S.W. carried
its point. That left Victoria with 22 mem-
bers, as compared with N.S.W.’s 27. But
later on a Federal Statistician was appointed
(Knibbs, of Sydney), and for the first time the
population estimates of all the States were made
up by one man instead of by six men. Now,
Knibbs is understood to be of opinion that the
old estimates were shaky, and to flint that Vic-
toria lost its member under a misapprehension,
or a premature rush, or something ; and there is a
fair chance that Victoria will demand its lost
member back again. And, if the number of
electors on the roll is any fair guide to the number
of inhabitants, Victoria has a very remarkable-
looking case. These are the figures for the 1906
election, according to the Year Book op Aus-
tralia for 1908 (published in Sydney):—
Electors Electors to
State. Members, on Roll, each Member.
N. S. Wales .. 27 736,267 27,269
S. Australia .. 7 192,826 27,547
Westralia .. 5 145,473 29,095
Queensland .. 9 271,109 30,123
Victoria .. 22 680,863 30,948
Tasmania is left out of the calculation, for the
number of its members isn’t based on population,
but on the law which says that no State
shall have less than five Representatives.
The above figures led to a good deal of
grumbling even before the new estimates of popu-
lation came along. The figures alleged that
N.S.W. had more members in proportion to the
number of electors than any other mainland
State, while Victoria had less members than any
other. Even if, at last election, Victoria had
had the original 23 members and N.S.W. the
original 26, there would still, on these figures, have
been more members to a given number of electors
in N.S.W. than in Victoria. In fact, it is claimed
that, if the number of electors on the roll is any
criterion, South Australia and N.S.W. have got a
most remarkable advantage over Queensland and
Victoria. The following list—also from the Year
Book op Australia—shows where the dispro-
portionately big and the disproportionately small
electorates were situated in 1906 :
Disproportionately Large Constituencies.
Fawkner (Victoria) .. 37,366 electors
Brisbane (Queensland) .. 34,839 ~
Oxley ( „ ) .. 34,589
Bourke (Victoria) .. 33,975 ~
Maribyrnong ( ~ ) .. 33,891 „
Kooyong ( ~ ) .. 33,695 ~
Bendigo ( ~ ) .. 32,257 ~
Perth (VVestralia) .. 32,093 ~
Balaclava (Victoria) .. 32,049 ~
That means that, according to the Yeab-Book’s
figures for 1906, there were nine mainland con-
stituencies which, in a most conspicuous fashion,
had been done out of their fair share of representa-
tion, and of these, six were in Victoria, two in
Queensland, and one in Westralia. Then, accord-
ing to the same authority, this is the other side of
the case :
Disproportionately Small Constituencies.
Eden-Monaro (N. S. Wales) .. 23,564 electors.
Grey (S. Australia) .. 24,127 ~
Maranoa (Queensland) .. 24,698 ~
Kennedy ( ~ ) .. 24,843 „
Macquarie (N. S. Wales) .. 24,852 ~
Newcastle ( ~ ) .. 24,910 ~
Hume ( ~ ) .. 25,068
Riverina ( ~ ) .. 25,079 ~
Barrier ( ~ ) .. 25,294 ~
Werriwa ( ~ ) .. 25,835 ~
Richmond ( ~ ) .. 25,861 ~
Gwydir ( „ ) .. 25,887 „
That signifies that there were 12 mainland
constituencies which had, in 1906, a ridicu-
lously large amount of member in propor-
tion to the visible number of voters, and of
these constituencies S.A. had one, Queensland
two, and N. S. Wales no less than nine. Such
discrepancies certainly suggest that in future
there should only be a re-adjustment of represen-
tation once every ten years, when there are the
reliable census figures to go upon. But mean-
while Victoria, which says it has nearly all the
under-membered constituencies, and that N.S.W.
has almost all the over-membered ones, is de-
manding an inquiry to decide whether it lost a
Representative to N.S.W. fairly three or four
years ago, or whether there was an amazing
muddle somewhere. In effect it says : “ Gimme
back me member ! ” Of course, even if it lost its
member unfairly a few years ago, the state of the
population to-day may have tardily justified the
act. But it is claimed that the act was needlessly
sudden when it happened.

The Labor Conference Decides for a
Military Caste.
The above wasn’t exactly what the recent Sydney
Labor Conference said it did, and it almost cer-
tainly wasn’t what it thought it did, but, all the
same, it was precisely what it DID. The exact
reading of the matter appeared thus in one of the
Sydney dailies :
COMPULSORY MILITARY SERVICE.
Mr. E. Riley (Sydney Labor Council) moved :
“ That congress protest against the introduction of any
scheme of enforced military service.”
He contended that in every country where there was a
military caste, and where militarism predominated, the
workers had a very poor show of getting a fair deal.
And the proposition was carried by a fair ma-
jority-incredible as it may seem, for a more
upside-down motion was never put before
any assemblage. In the military business
there are just three alternatives: (1) A country
may do without any army at all—or at least
without any competent army. Then it gets
a military caste in the end, but it is a foreign one,
even as the Norman military caste came and sat
on the head of England, and the Muscovite one
on the skull of Poland, and the Ottoman one on
the Greek Empire, and the Jap one on Korea,
and just as every community that had an army
and felt like collecting “chout” came and stood
on the countenance of Bengal. Then (2) there is
the alternative of a voluntary army. It has to be
a long-service army, because, as a rule, only a small
portion of the able-bodied male population volun-
teers, and the country wants to keep its soldier
when it has got him. So the volunteer soldier
settles down to pass a fair portion of his
life in military quarters. He becomes a person
apart from the mass of the people. He loses
his knowdedge of them and his sympathy
with them. He grows to be a part, not of the
nation, but of the army. He gets to a point at
which, as a being permanently separated from the
rest of the community, he cheerfully shoots down
strikers or any other civilians when ordered to do
so. That is the military caste idea in all its
horror. And then there is (3) the alternative, as
practised in Switzerland, of training the whole
male population, but having no military caste,
either native or foreign. Or if the trained males
do form a military caste, it is a caste of the whole
male population; and a caste that includes
everybody is no caste at all, just as no
secret society can remain a secret society when
everybody is in it, and everybody knows all
about the passwords, and the symbols, and
the mystic billy-goat and the ritual of the
XXXIInd degree. That is the scheme'which
the Deakin Government proposes, and which
was denounced by a strangely-aberrated majority
in the Sydney Labor Conference. On the plea
that it didn’t want a military caste, it gave its
vote against the only scheme which can prevent
Australia having one. And the alternative,
which presumably the dunder-headed majority
favors, is the voluntary, long-service army which
is something severed from and foreign to the
nation —the sort of force that England has —
the one which cheerfully shot down the
workers at Peterloo, and many other places,
sometimes for merely complaining about
the high price of bread. The only modern
countries in which the Deakin Government’s
scheme has had a fair trial are Switzerland and
the Transvaal. Switzerland is commonly regarded
as a country where the interests of the workers
are looked after as they are in no other part of
Europe and nowhere in America. And in the
Transvaal, where every adult male Boer was a
trained soldier, with his gun and ammunition at
hand and his local field-cornet to lead him, and
where the only armed force was the worker
himself, it would have been a very suicidal tyrant
who tried to oppress that same worker. As a
method of providing military defence without
obnoxious military institutions, the Swiss and
Boer policy, which Prime Minister Deakin advo-
cates, appears unrivalled. Certainly it seems far
ahead of the British system by which is furnished
the armies which hold down Ireland and India,
and go annexing and marauding in four conti-
nents. Still, if the majority of the Labor Confer-
ence wants a military caste, it deserves to have
one. And it seems to want such a caste very
badly.

386 GEORGE STREET (near G.P.0., Sydney).
Branch : 93 Queen Street, Brisbane.
basis. It is when the statistician leaves his columns of
figures and his mathematics, and turns himself into a
descriptive writer, that trouble is apt to call in ; and it
calls in rather frequently in this Year-book. It may he
fairer—this being the first year of issue—to judge it by
its plan than by its text; and of its plan nothing but
good can be said. As to the text, inaccuracies to the
extent of a page-full were detected as the issue was going
through the printing-machines; and the page might
easily run to half-a-dozen.
Take the mining section, for instance : “ The export of
zinc spelter and concentrates has increased from 97 tons,
valued at £9BB, in 1889, to 3666 tons, valued at £292,806,
in 1906.” That would make “zinc spelter and concen-
trates ” worth about £BO a ton, whereas metallic spelter
was then worth only about £25. Following up the same
metal, the Year-book in another place remarks that
“in the early years of the history of the Broken Hill
mines the whole of the zinc contents of the ores was lost
in smelting operations ” —a statement which will strike
anybody who knows anything at all about metallurgy as
absurd. Of course, the zinc has to be eliminated by wet
concentration before the silver-lead ore is even sent to
the smelter; and, anyhow, “in the early years of the
history ” of Broken Hill there wasn’t any zinc in the ore.
Occasionally, too, where one might; expect something
like a glad sound of triumph, we get a very poor
quavering note. No Australian industry has made finer
progress than that of coke-making. Down to 15 years ago
the Australian article was pushed aside contemptuously,
and in 1891 76,542 tons were imported, while as late as
1892 only 8852 tons were made in Australia. In 1903-6,
however, 520,027 tons were made in Australia, and only
16,000 tons were imported. Yet, in spite of this fine
Australian achievement and this complete turning of the
tables, the Year-book of Australia tells us this about
coke:—
“ Notwithstanding the large deposits of excellent coal
in Australia, there is at the present time a fairly consider-
able amount of coke imported from abroad, the oversea
imports during the three years ended 1906 amounting to
about 16,000 tons. Various reasons were adduced to
account for the rejection of the local article, such as ex-
cessive friability in transport, lack of strength, excessive
amount of ash, etc. It is believed, however, that in a
great many instances these disabilities have been over-
come, so that succeeding years should see a more extensive
local production.”
Much the same sort of thing occurs in connection with
the reference to the manufacture of chemical fertilisers,
another industry in which Australia has made extraordi-
nary progress during the past half-dozen years or so. A
table shows that, in 1906, 134,225 tons of fertilisers were
imported ; but as to Australian production :
“Statistics relating to the fertiliser manufactories of
the Commonwealth, which in 1906 only numbered 14, are
not available ; but the output is, as yet, very small in
comparison with that which is imported.”
Now, Victoria alone used 60,871 tons in 1906, and
Westralia 16,127 tons ; and it is very certain that S.
Australia, N. S. Wales and Queensland among them used
about three times that quantity, which would make the
Australian consumption over 300,000 tons. If, therefore,
only 134,225 tons were imported, it is certain that a great
deal more than that was made in Australia. Yet the
Australian Year-book tells us that “ the output is, as
yet, very small in comparison with that which is im-
ported.” If a Year-book must err, this really isn’t the
side on which one might reasonably expect it to tip over
—at least, with such frequency.
Three other illustrations of the urgent need for a
stricter revision of the Year-book in future issues must
suffice. First, as to private railways. The published table
purports to cover a period no later than 1905. The text
says “ there are at present three private lines in N. S.
Wales” —the Deniliquin, the Silverton Co.’s, and the
Warwick Farm. This omits the East Greta, Aberdare,
and Stanford-Merthyr lines, which stand second in im-
portance to the Silverton Co.’s line. As to banking, the
Ballarat Banking Co. is omitted from a list which pur-
ports to be complete. And, finally, as to life insurance,
we are told that the Victorian, the Australian Alliance,
and the Citizens’are the only cos. which are partly pro-
prietary ; though, of the offices included in the Year-
book’s general list, the Standard and the Australian
Metropolitan are just as much proprietary as the other
three are. The Year-book, moreover, repeats this inac-
curacy on a later page.
And yet, in spite of these stumbles, the Year-book
is a valuable arsenal, though until it is revised it will
have to be drawn upon cautiously. Very likely
the statistical tables will be found to be com-
paratively free from error. Still, on p. 798, the S. Aus-
tralian electors enrolled for the House of Representatives
and entitled to vote in 1906 are set down at 42,065 males
and 38,578 females, or a total of 80,643, whereas the total
enrolment was, according to an official statement made
at the time, 192,826. In general, however, it is not easy to
check such figures, but where it has been possible to run
an effective measuring-rule over them, they have mostly
proved to be a satisfactory 12 inches to the foot. Out of
these tables rise a fine myriad of arguments for the
better confounding of the Stinking Fish person and the
State Frighter. To the man who curses at his telephone
service, and tells you that Federal mismanagement sticks
out all over the business, you can at least whisper that,
anyhow, the telephone connections in N. S. Wales alone
have sprung from 9864 in 1901 to 15,453 in 1906, an increase
of 56 per cent, in five years. To the State Treasurer who
shrieks about his loan repayments you can point to a
table on page 690, showing that not one single State re-
duced its debt during the past two years—N. S. Wales,
indeed, increased its load by £3,285,844. And, finally,
here you can get a comparison of the climatic conditions
of, say, Canberra, and the alleged “blizzard-swept” Dal-
gety, and see that the mean winter temperature of the
latter is less than 3 degrees lower than Canberra’s, while
it is 5 degrees higher than Amsterdam’s, 10 degrees higher
than Berlin’s, 5 degrees higher than Brussels’, 16 degrees
higher than Chicago’s, 11 degrees higher than Copen-
hagen’s, 23 degrees higher than New York’s, and 28 de-
grees higher than Quebec’s. It is even 1 degree higher
than Shanghai’s.

A New Outbreak of the “ Truck ” Disease.
The massive and magnificent Commonwealth Oil
Corporation, the concern which consists for the
most part of lordly English millionaires, proposes
to introduce something of the “ squire ” system to
the Wolgan Valley (N.S.W.), where its main
works are. Consequently application was made
recently for a private township site of close upon
a square mile. Local people heard of it, where-
upon there were ructions, and the Labor Con-
gress, which happened to be sitting in Sydney,
asked the State Government to receive a
deputation. To the Congress’s amazement, Chief-
Secretary and Mines Minister Wood refused ; to
its greater amazement, he gave these extraordinary
reasons—(l) the Government was opposed to
granting such leases ; (2) this particular lease had
already been practically granted. It hadn’t been
actually granted, it was admitted ; it had not
passed the Governor-in-Council. Yet, though
there was ample time to stop the lease, the
Minister declined to hear reasons why the Govern-
ment should not do what it declares that it is
opposed to doing. If Chief Secretary Wood had
said that there wasn’t any reason for the deputa-
tion because the lease was not going to be granted,
as the Government was opposed to such leases, the
thing would have been intelligible ; but as matters
stand, it looks as though the Government knew
it was doing wrong, yet insisted upon doing it,
and therefore was not game to place itself in a
position in which it would be expected to justify
its misconduct. Even if the Government were
not doing something which it knows to be wrong,
this was a subject upon which a body representa-
tive of the wage-earners had a particularly strong
claim to be heard. The daily papers talk glibly,
of course, of the case of Sunlight, in England,
where “a great manufacturing proprietary houses
its workpeople”in “a model township—model in
design, in comfort, and in sanitation.” There
is, however, no need to go England for
illustrations. Newcastle has coal companies
which also house their employees; and the
same thing happens at the Wallaroo and Moonta
copper mines in S. Australia. In neither of these
places is the design, comfort, or sanitation any-
thing to boast of; on the contrary, when the em-
ployer is also the landlord, the employee mostly
finds he has to put up with any old shack—it is
the price which he pays, and which his children
pay, for being kept on the pay-sheet. Then, if
the employer and employee disagree about wages
or conditions, the employer can use a two-edged
sword. As employer, he knows that no wages
are being earned ; as landlord, he can then step
in and insist upon the payment of the rent which
he knows, in most cases, can’t be paid, and if it
isn’t paid, out the tenants must bundle —or accept
the labor conditions that he offers ! Long ago
the evils of the “truck” system were made
so clear to the Parliament of N. S. Wales, that a
stringent Act was passed making payment in
anything but money illegal; it was so stringent
that, until a slight amendment was made, em-
ployers could not even deduct a small weekly
amount for the up-keep of a hospital. Yet,
though Parliament has thus expressed its opinion
about the “truck” system, this foolish, blunder-
ing State Government of N. S. Wales has appar-
ently made up its mind to give an absentee co. a
chance to introduce one of the very worst forms
of “truck” —a chance for this absentee co. to
compel a man to be its tenant before it accepts
him as its workman.
