The Drift of W. Kidston.
“ Itan ” submits his view :
Is Kidston drifting Tory wards ? True, there are rumors
of a Government-Opposition coalition. But supposing
the union does take place, it will be a case of Kidston
absorbing Philp and Co., unless Willif. is tempted to
become Agent-General. If Kidston returns, as he says he
will, the coalition will mean the extinction of the Philp
crowd as an organisation—swallowed up by Democracy.
(What a stomach Demos must have !)
Kidston has been literally forced to do something, for
the Bowman followers, with Lesina and Hardacre yap-
ping in front, have made politics intolerable ever since
Morgan left for the Chamber of Fossils. It was plain
that the Government could not go on as it has been
going, and it is much better that Kidston should lead
than that the awful set of Philp blunderers should get
office, backed up by a section of the Kidstonites, who
would probably take that course rather than join forces
with Bowman. Democrats must not forget that even
during the last session Kidston got through an amazing
lot of very advanced Democratic business, and inci-
dentally dragged out of the Tories considerable support.
There may be reason for fault-finding at the syndicate
railways, but even as to that the State has not got a bad
bargain. The terms are such that the Railway Depart-
ment will have control of the lines and the working
arrangements, which will prevent any repetition of
the Chillagoe extortion scandal. The construction of rail-
ways in the Lilydale country, as well as back from Clon-
curry, is largely a case of gambling with the risks of the
mining industry. Under the existing conditions, the
Government is practically getting those lines built at the
expense of the syndicates, which take all risks up to a
certain time, whereupon the lines become the property
of the people at a price calculated on the last preceding
five years’ earnings.
It is deplorable, no doubt, that Kidston and the Labor
Party could not work together. But the latter has shown
such an aggressive disposition that no self-respecting
Government could tolerate it. In the end, Democracy will
lose by that. The Labor Party, probably through exces-
sive zeal, has put the clock back. Many sympathisers, in
fact, fear it has disorganised the instrument alto-
gether through over-winding, or has broken the main-
spring, or done something else equally foolish.
The Cheap Gun Horror.
The S. M. Herald, which has ever barracked
violently for the dirt-cheap foreign-made article,
printed without comment (2/5/’08) the following
from the pen of one H. J. M. Mallard :
One has only to spend a few minutes at our rail-
way station on the eve of a holiday (such as Easter), and
he will wonder at the sight of hundreds of young fellows —
boys from the age of 13 and upwards—shooting bound.
But to me it is a marvel that not more come back on the
stretcher. The majority are equipped with a single- or
double-barrel breechloader. On investigation you will
find that it is the cheap 30s. to 50s. gun that
dominates (made in Belgium). I had the misfortune
of witnessing a gun accident on Good Friday,
at Douglas Park, the third inside of 12 months.
The unfortunate shooter was in an unrecognisable state.
He was in a pitiable plight, and it was a toss-up whether
he would lose his left eye or not. When inquiries were
made about the type of gun he was using, it turned out
he was another cheap-gun victim. How is it that if
a pea-rifle accident happens a hundred miles from
nowhere it finds its way into our daily papers, while gun-
bursting you never hear of, as it is rarely, if ever,
reported, unless it proves fatal ? Now my firm opinion is
that a pea-rifle is far less dangerous than a cheap gun, etc.
On each of the occasions when an “ Arms ” item
has come before the Australian Parliament the
Herald has pleaded strenuously for “cheap
arms ; ” and members of its way of thinking drew
painful word-pictures in the Federal House of the
destitute cocky with his ranch a mass of raven-
ing rabbits owing to his inability to afford the
ruinously expensive gun that would eventuate if a
high enough duty were imposed to bring
about the Australian - made fowling - piece.
Furthermore, the Herald and its afore-
said crowd suggested drearily that in any
case an Australian couldn’t make a fowling-piece
that would slay a rabbit. The collective Free-
trade voice clamoring passionately for “the
greatest good of the greatest number” suc-
ceeded in enacting that the duty on the cheap
30s. to 50s. gun (made in Belgium) should be low
enough to permit of its broadcast entry into the
Commonwealth. The result, as the Herald's
correspondent, Mallard, points out, is seen most
days in the year. Failing a prohibitive duty on
these deadly imports, the Australian Government
has two courses now open to it. It may (1) forbid the
importation of the “untested” g:un altogether, as
“ more than ordinarily perilous in its components
or application ” ; or (2) it may impose a gun tax
that will hit hard at the lunatic who uses the dirt-
cheap foreign-made article. Either of these will
tend considerably to preserve our fast vanishing
indigenous bird-life, and still more to preserve the
life of the indigenous human being.

At Her Majesty’s (Sydney) “Humpty
Dumpty ” still has a great fall eight times a week;
and eight times a week he rises from the stage
to plaintively ask who his mother was. The
Bulletin doesn’t know for certain ; but, judging
by the crowded state of the house and the per-
sistent smile on the face of the box-office, it
reckons that she was an oof-bird.
* * *
At Sydney Palace the Maskelyne mysteries
still magnetise much wealth from the pockets of
people who love to have tricks played on their
eyesight. For although this paper is quite unable
to explain how the illusions are worked, it is still
prepared to take its solemn affidavit that the lady
does not materialise out of space, and that
the monkey is never really in the box.
The air of absolute reality with which
these mysteries are carried out merely proves
what a remarkable man John Nevil Maskelyne is.
Possibly it is all based upon manipulation of the
mirror. Maskelyne’s father was an astronomer,
and used mirrors to explore the depths of space,
and, naturally, John Nevil uses them to explore
the depths of the public pocket. He was a Chelten-
ham artist in jewellery when he invented his mar-
vellous cabinet in 1863, and, great artist as he was
in metals, he never gave a finer finish to
any of his goldware than he does to his
illusions, which have stood the test of 45 years,
and are still practically unsolved by the public.
His great masterpiece was the Cleopatra’s N eedle
illusion. A shell obelisk stood on a pedestal, with
a sheet of paper between it and its support, appa-
rently blocking the only means of ingress and
egress. Yet out of this small object he produced
about half the population of ancient Egypt,
and left his audiences dismayed and staggered
with the idea that there was something in magic
after all. Had he chosen, he could have posed as
the great boss magician of the ages ; but from the
very first he smilingly declared there was nothing
supernatural about the business, and that it was
nothing but masterly fake. And thereby he made
greater glory for himself, for had he possessed
supernatural powers it had been easy; it was a
better proof of greatness to do the apparently
impossible.

On Friday night next the Sydney Criterion
“Beggar” takes a pull at his steed (which has
been running a fine and willing race for the past
fortnight), dismounts, weighs in, collects all bets,
and makes way for the next event on the Meynell
and Gunn programme—to wit, “Camille.” The
fact that objections will be inevitably and in-
stantly lodged on all sides to the above perform-
ance will not irritate, stultify, confound or other-
wise discommode this popular horseman one iota.
Madge Mclntosh will carry the syndicate’s colors
on Camille the following night; and Hareourt
Beatty has been secured at a big retaining-fee to
steer Armand.

Kubelik’s sponsors are anxious to remind
the public that He Is Coming. He will be
tethered in Sydney from May 19 till a later date.
Besides being in the front rank of violinists,
Kubelik deserves the gratitude of the public for
not bringing his four girl babies along with his
thousand-pound Strad.
He * He He
The melancholy Dane is what Max Schliiter
appears in his portraits on the hoardings—a very
sad Dane, looking down at the fiddle nestled on
his breast as if grieving over a broken string.
But he isn’t really melancholy. Last Monday he
appeared at Sydney Centenary Hall for the first
time after an absence of six years. He was received
by a fairly-filled house in glad raiment, and again
proved himself the brilliant Dane that Sydney
already knew. His programme of classic,
dramatic, and sentimental pieces was a star
achievement, and the little catastrophe of a string
breaking whilst he was bowing out a coquettish
mazurka only whetted the audience’s appetite.
The artist retired to repair the damaged catgut, and
he had put in a fresh strand of pussy-fibre, and
was hastening back to the neighborhood of the
potted palm elevaticm, when the new string
snapped. And he hadn’t a third. The faithful
and resourceful Southwell pulled a string from
his evening clothes, but the mercurial Dane
sprinted from the premises, and fled like
a hare in a hansom to the Australia to get
his own particular brand of meow rope
The audience, with that mazurka still quivering
along its nerves, waited unclamorously. For the
rest, Miss Sussmilch, and Mr. Laurence God-
frey Smith and Maestro Sykes helped to make the
recital a fine thing in its line. Schluter will
play again on Thursday, and close his season on
Saturday.
** * *
At Prince Alfred Park (Sydney) Wirth’s
gigantic circus and menagerie still draws with the
energy of a 40-h.p. mustard plaster, and the
packed audience of last Saturday night looked
eagerly forward to the christening of the giraffe
who was making his first public appearance in
Australia. For general out-of-the-wayness the
giraffe beats the kangaroo hollow. His neck is
longer than his backbone, and his hind legs are
about half the length of his front ones, while the
top end of him towers up to an apparent height
of about 14ft. In spite of this tendency to
length without breadth, the animal is fairly nimble
and graceful on his feet. He is the first giraffe that
has succeeded in landing alive in Australia, all the
others having died in the attempt. It is interest-
ing to know that he is not a child of the desert
but hails from the. Hamburg Zoo, where he was
bred, and where Phil Wirth acquired him. He
was christened Commonwealth with a bottle
of champagne, and the clown made desperate
though ineffectual attempts to catch the loose fluid
before it struck the sawdust and to drink it in
the air.

“ The Lady Dandies” close their career
at Sydney Royal on Saturday, and after that the
theatre will have a brief well-earned rest. Any-
body who hasn’t seen the “Dandies” is hereby
urged to hasten like Lot leaping out of Sodom.
** * *
West’s Pictures are now firmly estab-
lished at Sydney Town Hall, and the receipts at
the ticket office indicate that the love of the fleet-
ing image does not diminish. At present the
programme leans to “ furrin parts.” Some fine
representations of snow sports in Switzerland,
with sleigh bells tinkling in the distance, and the
thud, thud, of the ski-jumpers who flash down the
long glittering slope of the snow track, are very
realistic. From the ice-bound winterof Switzerland
the audience is whisked away to the Soudan, and
finds itself growing thirsty in view of straggly
palms, dreary expanses of moss-dotted sand, and
low buildings of sun-dried bricks. Between the
African black and the Yellow Man there isa great
gulf fixed, but the cinematograph bridges it in a
few seconds and goes reeling through Korea, show-
ing the native of that distressful country in the
haunts out of which the Jap is now busily engaged
poking him with the thin end of the bayonet.
*# * *
The Kaufmanns continue to play amazing
tricks with their bicycles at Sydney Tivoli. Their
turn never stales ; when they have worn out 943
ways of not falling off though the laws of attraction
say they should, they just go and invent 711 other
ways, and so they keep the star place in the bill.
Sylvo has, however, arrived with a competing
turn of mixed clowning and juggling; and the
loud laugh that proclaims the full gallery rattles
throughout his performance.
** * *
At Adelaide Royal, Bland Holt, who has
a long season before him, has played “ The White
Heather ” for two full weeks, though half way
through the second there was very nearly a sudden
stop to the whole show. Bland has to ride
on in the Park scene on a bicycle and collide with
a chair. One evening he cannoned off it, and
went straight for the footlights. For one second
he felt desperately for a back-pedal brake, but tire
machine did not happen to be built that way, and
the next second it was down on top of the big
drum. Bland himself fell on his chest on
the rail between stalls and orchestra, and
bent it considerably. Blanche Arral has sung just
once more at the Town Hall, which is now
occupied by the “Scarlet Troubadours”; and
there are about half-a-dozen other shows of varying
excellence open for selection every evening.
Concerning these “ Scarlet Troubadours,” they are
“devised and controlled by Mr. Edward Brans-
combe,” and they show themselves (on starting
the serious business of their Australian tour at
Adelaide Town Hall) a tuneful and humorous team
of eight. At their best, the eight are really funny.
At their not-quite-best—or, indeed, in any case, if
their work is not accepted in the same spirit as
offered—they tend to be amiably futile. In two
words, they are Quite English, of the type which
runs a perpetual matinee in some hall off Regent-
street for the particular benefit of the schoolgirl and
the country clergyman. They play a settled pro-
gramme, printed in England before starting, and
changed every second evening. Its ostensible
price is “ annas 4,” showing that it has already
been offered to the discerning Hindu. The six
men and two ladies of the team make a feature of
costume, putting in the first half in cloaks ot
black and red, then getting into pierrot guise, and
finishing in scarlet evening-dress. Singers, pianist,
sketch-artist, and “jester’’are all skilled in their
way, and they play up to each other perseveringjy
with a dance or a mouthful of chorus. If a lad)
sings of a ghost or a golliwog, someone steas
away and comes back with a face whitened or
blackened to correspond. It is all very merry
and musical, and should be a success in the cities.
Up-country would be puzzled by it, but then up-
country is hardly likely to see it.
** * *

Within Melbourne Bijou at present the
fracas under “ The Southern Cross ” absorbs the
attention of Commissioners in Lunacy, and mental
specialists, and other folk who take an interest in
the shattered mental condition of the British lord-
ling. The nobles of “ The Cross ” closely follow
the traditions of the stage peerage. They suffer
from all the evils of riches and malnutrition, and
are as inept as may be. “East Lynne” is pro-
mised for next Saturday night. On that subject
comment is strictly withheld. It wants to save
itself up.
** * *
The Dutch-flavored idyll of Sally Hook
continues its cheerful course at Melbourne Royal
what time Sally’s “ popper ” pursues his invalu-
able recipe. So far the old gentleman has not
proved his title to any fervid oblation. He has a
certain muzzy philosophy and a turbid joke or two ;
but he doesn’t burn with Homeric fire, so to speak.
His humor is too pensive, too sad, inevitably
recalling peace and green fields and lambkins, and
the solemn kine from which a short-skirted
Phyllis extracts the cow-juice in the dusk. Old
Hook’s failing is that he is too flaccid to be
astounding, and too arid to be much esteemed.
Thus his audience considers itself bitterly deluded,
for it prefers uproar to ideas and farce to nebulous
japes, and getting neither, it turns its attention to
Sally and to those of her acquaintances who inter-
polate the yarn with song. Well, the songh—
especially “Bottles and Bottles,” by the male
chorus in Act ll.—do much to mitigate the
criticism that old Hook demands.
** * *
Tittell Brune and co. at Melbourne
Princess’s begin to be more satisfactory as
Peter Pan and his delightful corps. At
first, only Loring Fernie, as the sonorous
Hook and the doubled character of Mr.
Darling, kept right inside the filmy garments
Barrie has given his characters. Now everyone
is easier and more spontaneous; and the
show flies as gracefully as Peter does. The
boys—amongst the juvenile performers—are
really artistic in their acting, or their neglect
of it. And the Brown Tittle herself is less
affected than ever before. The last act, which
discovers the remorseful Mr. Darling as the occu-
pant of the pet dog’s kennel, has the neatest dia-
logue in the piece. The explanation that he is
now much sought after in Society is simple. All
the ladies ask him to dinner because he is “so
quaint,” and say “Oh! do please bring your
kennel. It’s so dreadfully interesting.” By
living like a dog, a lucky man may make a lion
out of himself in London, hints Barrie—so long as
his roars are not true.
** * *
Among West’s Pictures, at Melbourne
Olympia, is a striking film of Tangier. Before
the spectator whirls a succession of narrow, busy
streets, thronged with camels and picturesque
hooded Moors. One sees strange, wide-eyed
children peeping from the doorways, a mendicant
with a withered leg, a native musician, a sheikh,
an occasional European—but very little dirt. One
passes through the teeming life of the bazaars;
through the citadel with its rows of ancient
muzzle-loading cannon—all in a few minutes. In
addition, West turns on to his sheet the mad
delirium of carnival time at Nice. Around a pro-
cession of Gargantuan monsters, decorated with
ribbons, bells, and all manner of things, yells
(you only see the yell) a cosmopolitan whirlpool—
pierrots, harlequins, and all kinds of grotesquely-
dressed lunatics, leaping about as if on a grid in
Gehenna. Such films are more instructive than a
whole library of travellers’ tales.
*# * *
Henry Rickards fastened Frank Har-
wood to the bill, at Melbourne Opera House, last
Saturday night. Frankie gives a performance
composed of a song, a dramatic reading, and
some jokes with the tang of Paunch. It is a
placid and not unpleasant entertainment; but it
is really more suited to a curate’s smoke concert
or the anniversary of a Girls’ Guild. The depart-
ment of gymnastics is as heretofore ; but it will
presently lose considerable prestige by the de-
parture of the Artois Bros., the neatest bar
specialists this paper has noted for a very long
time. Meantime John Higgins, champion jumper,
fails to dumfound the populace; while the sketch
by the Deane Tribune Co. seems to this writer as
dragged-out as the search for a body in a river on
a cold afternoon.
“C.K.Y.”: Various scribes, probably out-of-collar
mummers, heave rocks at the flickergraph. Can’t see it.
Have looked in at all the Sydney shows, a dozen more or
less, where the screen gives a night’s entertainment for a
sprat, and count I had good value. When the films are
new the shivery effect is not perceptible. The introduc-
tion of the singer with the picture, the audience joining
in the chorus, is an item that catches on and gives a
friendly picnic feeling that, helped by peanut*, makes
everyone feel at home. Dad, Mum, and the nippers have
a real good time together at a nominal price. If the
snows eventually kill the bellowdrama and the inane
musical comedy there will be plenty of dry eyes at the
funeral.
Apparently the tragedy of Madge Graham,
whose body was found down a shaft at Ballarat
(Vic.), will now fade away into the dim region in
which the demoralised Victorian police force keeps
its unsolved mysteries—and the number of them
is something terrible. William lies has been
tned in a quiet sort of way, and acquitted
in a quiet sort of way, and that pre-
sumably ends the matter. The attitude of the
force in such a case as this is a quaint sort of busi-
ness. It fixes its eye on one man—the probable
jhan, or the improbable man, but, anyhow, the
handiest man. It looks for him. It finds him,
or else it doesn’t find him. If it finds him,
■rod he is tried and acquitted, then it sits
down heavily and drops the subject. Its
mental attitude is something like this: (1)
'ye are infallible; (2) we caught Smith; (3)

infallible and we caught him; (4) he was
acquitted by a mentally deficient Court, and
e can’t be tried again, and as the man
who did the thing is thus put out of our reach,
hj l ? no booking for the man who
ion t do it; (5) and all these things arise
oecause we are infallible.” All the same, Mel-
ourne wants a new cemetery. To it should be

AT POVERTY POINT.
Melb. : So Lauri has no good-bye for our city. Well,
he wouldn’t sacrifice the big- sum which a Melbourne
benefit would yield him if he could help it. Gouriet is an
excellent comedian, but will not fill Lauri’s hiatus. That
is to say, if the new pieces have Lauri parts. But fashion
changes so. They may have Gouriet parts. Gouriet is
similar to Elton, or Courtneidge, a character actor.
Lauri is the comic lecturer, like Greville or Toole. He
comes on in an irreeognisable make-up, but directly he
opens his mouth the wig flies up into the flies, the clothes
vanish through invisible traps, the paint washes itself off,
except that inevitable streak of rouge under his lip. This
helps a faint suspicion that there is always a bit of the
pantomimic in Lauri, as with George Leopold and Edouin.
Yes, a smack of “ Here we are again,” and “ How are you
to-morrow?” I suppose Lauri’s screw monkeyed round
half a monkey a week. Yet even after he had so wonder-
fully replaced Elton in “The Old Guard” and the like,
there came along a class of operas like “ The Gay Parish
enne ” and “ The French Maid,” where he couldn’t shine.
He was away from Williamson for some time. He even
did variety turns at Melbourne Opera House with Addie
Conyers. Then came a swoop of pieces with Lauri parts
in excelsis. As Hazlitt writes of Jack Bannister, he puts
a character over himself like a surtout, warm and com-
fortable. He is an actor of temperament, even as Eleanor
Duse is such an actress.
Mrs. Dolores Drummond, a .well-known actress of half
a century ago, has just completed her 72nd year. At
the Princess’s Theatre, Melbourne, in the ’fifties, she was
Miss Dolly Green. Her husband, known sometimes as
Sprague and other times as Drummond, was a law
clerk in Chancery Lane. Her earliest appearances were
with Mary Provost and Joe Raynor, in “ Camille.” Some
years ago.she came to Sydney as Mrs. Dolores Drummond
with a monologue entertainment. Her last appearance
in London Was 20 years ago, when she appeared in
“ Peter’s Mother.”
“Micawber”: Philip Wirth(B. 24/4/’08) speaks of picture
shows driving circuses out of business on the Continent.
In America the condition of vaudeville is critical, hun-
dreds of houses going over to the ranks of the flicker-
graph. As representative here of a Yankee vaudeville
paper, I received the following letter last week —it explains
itself : —“ Kindly give particular attention to the moving
pictures in your town ; also send along any newspaper
clippings of all available matter relating to this particular
branch of the profession. This is important, as the Bio-
graph threatens to be the staple amusement producer,
and as such we must cater accordingly.” Further ex-
tracts state that in several towns vaudeville is now an
unknown quantity.
“ Bus ” : A hard toiling shoveodrama co. has been play
ing “The Power of the Cross” and “The Betrayed
Chopper,” and other horrors, in M.L. Went to see the
vampire thing here (Christchurch) the other night, and
had my supper beer spoiled. “ Lingford Carson, Esq.,”
and “Geo. de Gray, Esq.,” ought certainly to be censured.
Their works drawgood audiences in M.L. Yettheawfulness
of them ! They do not, as is the wont of normal melo-
dramatic perpetrations, aspire to thrill merely ; it is their
mission to chill the very marrow of patrons, and send
them home in an ecstasy of unhealthy horror. And so
the small boy here, emulous of the ’Uming Vampire,
wails dismally in the streets o’ nights, and the gentle
fact’ry ’and has done in most of his and her cash getting
horrified. For it has been written there is no money-
spinner in M.L. like a brand-new hair-lifter.
“ Star ” : The enthusiasm of the Melbourne daily paper’s
young man in reviewing “ Peter Pan ” beats me. You
would think J. M. Barrie had worked a miracle, whereas
he has given a sort of hotch-potch of the familiar make-
believe of little children, with the accustomed fairies, and
the regulation pirates, and the harmless necessary animals
and Indians, with occasional slabs of Barrie pathos, all
without form and void. If you are not thrilled, then
Barrie believers tell you you have no sympathy with little
children and their tender sentiments. My reply is that
there is more art and truer sentiment in the kiddies
whom I can watch playing their own little dramas in the
back yard. Barrie’s kids are not true kids, unless they
cultivate juvenile angels in Scottish nurseries ; and when
Barrie’s babes are represented by bounteous damsels of
18, 20, and 40, I contend that the young idea gets lost in
the shuffle or scuffle.
“ Snell ” : Thomas Bent has been asked by Melbourne
Shakspearian Society to assist in the establishment of a
Shakspearian theatre built on the simple lines of the
Elizabethan playhouse. Tommy can’t see his way clear,
but announces himself as sympathetic. “ I am pretty
good myself on Shakspeare,” he says. I have heard
Thomas attempt to recite scraps of “ Romeo and Juliet,”
and would like to hear worse if it is anywhere discover-
able. Oh, if only some person or persons would persuade
Tommy to play Falstaff, in “The Merry Wives”! It
could be done, and that is the only joy we can offer the
American squadron that could not possibly be available
to the Yanks anywhere else on God’s good footstool.
A pleasant little play is now being acted at the Guignol
Theatre, Paris, which makes a specialty of realistic hor-
rors. The scene is a sculptor’s studio with the reputation
of being haunted. The last occupant went mad. The
present occupant works uneasily, while the lamps, cur-
tains and furniture in general play fantastic tricks. The
model has a kind of hypnotic vision. She sees the former
sculptor kill his wife and encase her in plaster-of-Paris,
Deeming fashion. The model tells her story. The sculp-
tor remembers a block of plaster left behind by his pre-
decessor, rushes at it, smashes it, and out falls the skele-
ton of a woman. Curtain falls just here.

“ Euroa ”: Some biographical scraps re an Australian
family—the Gibson Girls, now appearing at Melbourne
Opera House. The three girls are sisters, and Australians,
they have fulfilled engagements with Stephenson (Mel-
bourne and Sydney), Puller (Maoriland), and Bain (Tassy).
Refused to sign on for a tour of Manila because a clause
in the agreement stipulated that no marriage or marriage
engagement must be entered upon during the con-
tract. Another sister, Miss Beanie Galletly, who
Pickards’ some years ago, was leader of
the ballet, and arranged most of the dances. There was
a ballet at Rickards’ at that time. She married Hamilton
Hill, and both went to England. In the Smother Land
she doubled up ” with an English girl, and, as the
Osborne Sisters,” appeared with some success. An
injury to her ankle compelled her to give up dancing for
a while. Later, it was her wish that an expected domes-
tic event should happen in Australia, but her husband
was unable to cancel certain engagements, and their
future baritone was born in Pogland, though he was
brought out here a few months later. Still another
sister, Miss May Galletly, was with J.C.W. until
she married a member of Her Majesty’s (Sydney)
orchestra, and lived happily ever after. Of the sons, one,
now deceased, was a well-known Carlton cricketer The
mother is Australian born, and the father arrived out
here at the time of the gold rushes.
THE WORSER PART.
All the speakers at the Premiers’ Conference empha-
sised the necessity of negotiations with the Common-
wealth Government being conducted in a conciliatory
spirit.— AGE.
DEAKIN: “I don’t see your olive branches!”
STATE FRIGHTS CROWD: “YOU'RE to do the
conciliating. We’ll do the slangwhanging!”
Brisbane Royal, which purveys leg show and coon songs
at small prices, has an unusual sort of item on board just
now. It is supplied by Maurice Evans, a young Queens-
lander, who is making his first theatrical appearance. He
mimics the cries of the pigeon, the jackass, the cuckoo,
the curlew, the magpie, and the rooster, the howl of the
dingo, the barking of a dog, and the infernal row made
by the midnight cat when engaged in an argument with
the canine from next door, and does them so well that
the animals themselves don’t know the difference.
“Acacia”: Olga Gray, whose song and dance imper-
sonation is a feature of “ Humpty Dumpty,” is in private
life Gladys Fanning, only daughter of the Charlie Fanning
who used to black his face and be merry and gay in other
respects at Sydney Tivoli a while back. Her mother is a
sister of Mrs. George Musgrove, and she is cousin to the
talented Rose. Olga made her stage dbbut some years
ago, playing with Mrs. Bland Holt as “Two song and
dance artists ” in one of Bland’s melodramas.
“Nil”: I have read all I could get hold of about the
Butt, and now at last I have heard Her. As to what I
have seen in print, why, one says She has a contralto
organ, and one that She has a soprano, and another that
She is a contralto with some wonderfully high notes, and
yet another that She is a soprano with some phenomenally
low notes. And now I have heard Her, and the mystery
is solved. The Butt has two divine voices, absolutely
distinct and each remarkable of its kind. Her soprano
voice is of rare quality. I have only heard one like it, and
that was not possessed by a star, by any means ; but, if it
had been, the result might have been even better than that
heard in the Butt’s soprano. We have not anything like
a decent vocabulary for expressing timbre. But the
voice I mean is one that gives me a subjective impression
of thin, thin gold. It is rather a clinging voice, and leaves
the soft palate with seeming reluctance ; essentially is it
a sensuous voice. Further I cannot describe it. But my
little girl recognised the almost unique timbre independ-
ently, so there must be some special distinction. As to
the con traltoprofundo of the Butt —well! when She
plants Herself firmly on Her Feet, and finishes up by
delivering to the chandelier a note from about six inches
below the stave which you can hear above the Town Hall
organ, there’s no poodle-faking about that! It’s contralto,
and darned fine music too.
“ Sr.” : Part singing strikes me as an innocent amuse-
ment for old men who are strict teetotalers. On occa-
sions I’ve seen 50 grown-up married baldies of all sizes
and shapes sing what are called “ part songs.” With the
zest of school kids they bob off their seats at a sign.
Then, as the whiskery conductor starts performing his
drum-major act, a party of the stout push set off in a
peculiar harmony, something about “ My rose is but a
simple lump, lump, lump ; oh, yes, it’s but a simple
lump, lump,” 17 times. Just when they go to draw a
deep breath, some other blokes shake their heads,
and shriek something about “Beef it out, oh Beef
out it, Beef, Beef, Beef it out oooout,” 34 times.
Then a dead slow crowd in the middle of the team remem-
bers that it’s supposed to be playing, and off it starts and
trills something about “ And then he did his block. Yes,
strike me ! Yes, strike me ! Yes, oh yes, oh yes!” 73
times. And when the whole show gets moving altogether,
and the whiskered conductor is rousing and threatening,
you’d think it was 1379 Johnsons addressing the Speaker
on a point of order, set to music. They all catch up to
one another in the last lap, and just when you least
expect it, they bump up against a short high note and
sit down, and then I say fervently, “ Amen.”
Notable the change that has taken place in things
theatrical during the last few years. Once scratch and
stock dramatic cos. used regularly to desert the cities and
“go on the road ” at Christmas time. To-day, partly
owing to the flickergraph, which has ousted the “ legiti-
mate ” in the country person’s affections, and partly
owing to the demand that has arisen for locally-manufac-
tured mummers, the back regions hardly ever see the
villain undone in correct fashion ; and the injured woman
with the che-hild, the black shawl, and the cheap straw
“ lid ” is a rarity in those spots where cow-experts abide,
and strong men argue interminably about the state of
each other’s drains.
In a Cold Country museum a section was recently
devoted to an exhibition of Chinese curiosities and
works of Art, probably looted from the semi-sack of
Pekin ; and the introductory notes to the catalogue con-
tained the uncomfortable information: “The visitor’s
eye will be struck on entering' the room with a porcelain
umbrella.”
“ Harrison O.”: I have in my time visited a good many
shickergraph shows, and have found that, like Cleopatra,
age, custom, and other sundries do not change them one
iota. They always start with a view of Niagara Palls, and
then work on to an alleged Australian corroboree, and
reach the summit of fatuity in depicting a popular
murder—the Thaw-White event, for instance. The
shickergraph man, it happens, was just having Is. worth
of theatre, and seeing a murderous look in Thaw’s eye, he
rushed home, and got his machine, and got back again
(after having an argument with the fellow at the door
about taking the machine into the theatre) just in time to
witness the horror and take a flickergraph picture of
same. The most popular are always the tiresome but
apparently inevitable “chase” pictures. A person de-
camps with a leg of mutton, and is pursued by the
butcher, who seems quite annoyed, judging by his gesticu-
lation. The sausage bloke is at various intervals joined
by 219 kids, 23 motor-cars, 49 women, 17 police-
men, and 1934 dogs. They follow The Man With The
Mutton down hills, up hills, over fences, under fences,
across rivers and ditches. The motors exceed the speed
limit, the dogs go mad, the women fall over every fence
they come to, until one gets quite tired and longs for
them to get over one without falling ; the kids play leap-
frog ; the law’n’order blokes (after the manner of their
kind) bungle everything with systematic regularity, and
each and every man, woman, child, animal and
machine fails to observe the rule of the road. The Man
With The Mutton is finally—amid roars of laughter—run
to earth in a beer-barrel, and in this is rolled down a hill
into a convenient river at the bottom thereof. Then a
picture of the National Anthem is thrown upon the
screen, and I heave a sigh of relief, for the show is over—
and there is an hour yet to Closing Time.

social reform. “ The Lord of Latimer-street ” deals with
the problem of the
rich and the poor ;
“ Diana of Dobson’s,”
with its dormitory
scene of shop giris
disrobing’ and going
to bed, treats the
subject of “living
in” ; “ The House ”
centres on the poor
law problem. Most
of the plays pro-
duced by the Stage
Society and similar
organisations are full
of social criticisu).
“ Votes for Women ”
was the title of a play
last year. These
social dramas are
supposed to educate
the public on the
questions of the
hour, and the ex-
ample might be use-
fully followedin Aus-
tralia. Someone
ought to write a
comedy on the Federal
capital “ injustice.” The
heartrending agony of a
Sydney free importer ruined
by the new tariff is a sug-
gestive theme. “Looking for Land in Victoria,” with
Bent as a villain, would make a good tragi-comedy;
and finally Deakin might be the protagonist of an Aus-
tralian “ Hamlet.”
Probably the oldest musical critic alive is Mr. Edward
Fetis, who is now 96. For 75 years he has contributed
criticisms to the Independance Belge. He still writes
for the paper, and is an almost nightly attendant at the
opera.
“ Wrock ”: Discovered a weird entertainment in a
Maori ivhare near Moeraki (M.L.), the other day. The
show runs for 12 hours out of the 24, and a handful
of cigarettes or tobacco will admit at any time of the day
or night to the stalls, circle, or pit (it is mostly pit).
Occupying the centre floor space of the one and only
room was an aged and decrepi* gramophone, evidently the
magnificent gift of some benevolent pakeha. On the bed
in the corner w'ere two or three dozen records. Otherwise
the auditorium was full of Maoris —men, women, and
small fry—and over all hung a dense cloud of smoke. As
I arrived the alleged musical instrument rent the night
with the opening bars of “ Waiting at the Church.”
The Old Man of the hut fed the screechophone,
running right through the list of records, and
commencing again with No. 1. The records were not as
young as they used to be, and some of them produced
little more than a wheeze, but when a break occurred in
a selection the audience filled in the gap with the chorus,
until the machine picked up again. Little discretion was
used in arranging the programme, such items as “ The
Holy City ” being wedged in between “ Bill Bailey ” and
“I was Shaving Myself at the Time.” Fortunately the
rats had been at the cylinder of the Holy song, and the
greater portion of it consisted of a mighty rush pf wind.
Still the house was getting its money’s worth. It was
past midnight when I left, and they had just started on
the third lap with “ More Work for the Undertaker ;
Another Job for the Tombstone-Maker.”
Probable that Australia will shortly have a spell of
novelist Hall Caine as lecturer, under manager Ernest
Collins, lately Musgrove’s lieutenant. Caine would
undoubtedly prove a big draw of a sort—the sort which
takes delight in being horribly shocked at the guilelessly-
suggested immorality of the “ Christian” and “Eternal
City.”
“ Billy T.”: One of the most obnoxious inheritances
from the late Boer war jamboree is the hideous Bushveldt
hat —the drab, mousy, or dirt-colored cady of soft felt
“ hitched ”up at one side. Do you notice that this has
become an Australian institution, and have you observed
the loathly ugliness of it in that capacity? When a blithe
daughter of this happy land dresses herself up to repre-
sent Australia at a fancy-dress hop, skip, or jump, or in a
fervent music-hall ditty about her native place ; or when
a benighted management puts a contingent into a panto,
bally to whoop it up for the Commonwealth, that banal
hat always tops off the costume. It is an ungainly re-
minder of a shameful mistake, and should be rooted out
on that account, and ostracised, and eradicated, and
burnt to the ground ; and I contend that, for its ugliness
alone, it ought to be howled off by a discriminating popu-
lace every time it puts in an appearance professing to be
an Australian institution.
MUSICAL COMEDY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Some few years ago the demand for musical comedy
was such that writers of libretto and composers of light
music worked overtime to fulfil the supply. . . . The
scheme is usually extravagant, and serves only as a frame-
work on which to hang many ludicrous situations. There
is no definite plot . . . etc.—Daily Paper.
The Major slick, the lunatic, the affable Bagdad Ram,
The comic relief, who holds a brief for the prehistoric
Damn,”
The girl and the Earl, the low-born churl, the King and
the drunken sot,
All stalk in the flesh, a mad procesh, in search of a
vanished Plot.
With dance and song they prance along, each playing his
wild, mad part.
•A- gay soubrette does a pirouette—(lt’s pretty, but is it
There’s a two-verse lay of far Bombay, where the sun is
always hot,
But never a sign in word or line of a staid, hard-working
Plot. h
We thrill with bliss at a stolen kiss, and we scent a great
romance,
But with laugh and shout it fizzles out in a futile song-
an’-danee;
While Angeline makes a tuneful scene on the neck of her
Launcelot,
The nobly-“ bawn ” in the circle yawn, and rack their
brains for the Plot.
There’s a crew-el scheme, a maiden’s scream—we look for
a murder neat,
But the villain bold, with dagger cold, does an acrobatic
feat.
There are soulless clods, among the gods, who term it
“ bloomin’rot,”
And call for a flood of red ber-lud, and a sanguinary Plot.
The hero sings of wedding rings to a maid by a canvas
sea ;
There’s a plaintive baa from the orchestra, pitched in a
mournful key ;
Midst a blare of drums the parson comes, and he ties the
nuptial knot,
Then the curtain falls, and the drowsy stalls go forth for
something hot.

“TO AMUSE THE CHILDREN."
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The act in which Mr. Batty enters a railed-in arena with
a veritable menagerie of lions, leopards, wolves, and dogs
was particularly exciting, and one small incident occurred
which served to show how unsubdued was the real char-
acter of the beasts who did his bidding. While Mr. Batty’s
back was turned, a lion and lioness reared up, snarling
fiercel}', and it seemed as if the next minute they would
be at each other’s throats. There will be a children’s
matinee on Wednesday. — S. M. Herald (4/5/’08.).
Haste to the Circus, Willie, and be thrilled—
Step up. Don’t wait.
A dog or two may possibly be killed—
Which will be great.
When leopard seizes wolf, the gore that flows
Is something fine.
A wolf may howling die to-night—who knows?—
And freeze your spine.
Produce your bob, my little man, and see
Leo and wife
At holts. (The odds are 6to 4 that she
Will lose her life.)
With “ lions, leopards, wolves, and dogs ” about
Blood’s well-nigh sure :
Are you the child to cynically flout
So sweet a lure ?
“ But,” you may say, “ what I want’s human gore.”
The point is fair ;
And has, moreover, been provided for—
Batty is there.
N.S.W.

SOCIETY
PENFOLD'S
WINES
Australians who think of letting their
daughters go as missionaries to China should read
a recent Conan Dovle story, “The Pot of Caviare,
in which a kind-hearted old scientific man, who
lias “been there” before, poisons a whole house-
ful of women rather than let them fall into the
hands of a Chinese mob. Just to be killed is
nothing; but “ I have seen the death of the hot
eggs, I have seen the death of the boiling kettle,”
he says—and then he leaves the reader thinking
hard.
« « * »
Some of the evidence of Thomas Miller,
tfcimber carrier, before N.S.W. Arbitration Court
•during the hearing of the sawmillers’ case, now
proceeding: —
He carried loads up to about 3001 b., and had climbed
tip stacks 25ft. to 30ft.' high carrying such weights. Found
that it affected his heart and lungs. He also suffered
from rheumatism at times through carrying wet timber
from submerged rafts. The loads he carried caused the
skin to be worn off his shoulders, which bled in the morning
on commencing work. He was an exceptionally strong
man physically. He had borne on his hack 6cwt. of
wool and from 2001 b. to 3001 b. of wheat. His wages for
the 15 months he had been timber-carrying had not
averaged more than 30s. a week. He had a family of
eight children, two of whom were earning 265. and
12s. 6d. a week respectively. To eke out his earnings he
sold Sunday papers, working on that day from 3 a.m. to
1 p.m.
That’s the dignity of labor ! A horse may not be
worked with raw shoulders, but this man, made in
the likeness of God, had to drag his bruised back to
work, sore or not sore, and to bear his rheumatism
and his heart pangs ; and then he can only live by
selling papers on the Day of Rest !
THE WORKHOUSE
GUARDIANS.
Men who would think nothing- of spending a sovereign
on a dinner were calculating in a nice and exact manner
as to whether an old woman over 70 ought to get half-a-
crown or 2s. a week out of the poor rates.—BART KENNEDY
in “Wander Pictures" (Cassel and Co., 1906).
The striking unusualness of woman was
again displayed in Melbourne Criminal Court last
week. As the story was alleged in evidence it
was this: On March 6, He, and his name was
Abraham, having secretly insured his wife for
£5OO, entered their unhappy home and tried to
murder Her, and her name was Alice, by choking
Her with a chloroform-soaked handkerchief. She
resisted, got outside, neighbors intervened, and
there was no death. A month after, She charged
Him with attempted murder. Justice Hood
couldn’t see light through the case. To him it
was inscrutable. The jury couldn’t see light
either, and said “Not guilty.”
“Hassan.” : Whoever is responsible for the
Eight-Hours procession in Melbourne (and in
Sydney, too, for that matter) misses the whole
meaning of the affair. Eight-Hour Day is the
day of Labor, and the procession should give
some indication of that fact. Does it do so? 1
don’t think it does. The march of a Trades Union
is heralded by a florid banner, upon which a
couple of females, very much out of drawing, pose
for the general eye, and in the corner of this
gorgeous towel one sees the usual foolish person
trying to break a bundle of firewood, when any-
body but an ass or a State Premier would know that
the easiest way is to untie the bundle. This tassel led
bedspread is held by two gentlemen who look like
the sons of importers, so nicely is their hair oiled,
and afterwards comes the ruck of the Union m its
best Sunday clothes. It might be a union of
pawnbrokers (except for the noses), or a union of
hosiery-counter assistants. It doesn’t look, at
any rate, as if it did the hard work of the Com-
monwealth with hammer or lathe or drill, it
ought to look as though it did. The ironworkers
should march grimy, as they toil in the hot gloom
beyond the molten red of the furnaces, sledge-
hammers in hand ; the sailors should pass
in gum boots and sou’-westers ; the cooks and
bakers in caps and aprons ; the bricklayers grimed
with mortar, and with hods at the slope; each
man dressed according to his trade. For a trade
is not a thing to be ashamed of on Labor Day.
Such a procession would be vastly more pictu-
resque than the nondescript thing which now holds
up the trams for half a day, and Labor would be
worthily represented. For the dignity of Labor
is often taken off with its overalls, and the workers
at a man’s trade should be proud to wear Labor s
livery on its ceremonial day.
“J.G.” : In Sydney Worker (23/4/’08)
Herbert Low tells a tale of the mace that was
stolen from the Victorian Assembly some 18 years
ago. It is practically the same tale which was
smothered with so much difficulty at the time.
According to the legend, an M.P., of high import-
ance in these days, had invited some friends to
supper, and the party annexed the bauble to
grace the festivity, and held a mock Parliament
with it. Then the crowd, full of food and fizz and
revelry, is said to have proceshed to a House of
Flossie in Lonsdale-street, where it had more
fizz, and chased the mirthful Tottie on flying feet,
and put on a bogus aspect of a desire to smite that
damsel with the stolen property, and held carnival
and devilment generally, and finally departed,
leaving the gaud behind. After that no one
seems to know what became of the article, though
goveral different yarns went about for some time.
But just after the gold-headed mace was stolen
the Ballarat Courier, a very democratic paper,
alleged that it was “ shook ” as described by Low.
The Courier’s article caused the Devil’s own row
in Parliament, and kindly-looking old Bob Clark,
the proprietor, and the military Captain or Major
(now Colonel) Williams, the editor, were called be-
fore the bar of the House, and to save themselves
from being sent to the leads, the Courier had to
publish an apology, and also pay £lOO. If Low’s
version is correct, then the Courier s account was
correct, and its apology should be withdrawn, and
the £lOO should be disgorged. Some people
said at the time that an offer of a big reward
would have brought to light a lot of facts that the
House didn’t want to hear about on any account.

Bishop Neligan, of Auckland (M.L.), has
raised a storm in the Dominion, by stating in
England that, in consequence of the secular system
of education, the children in M.L. are growing up
“Pagans,” or “ heathens,” or something equally
appalling. Local clergy
have joined in the
shindy and mostly
agree with him, though
some say it is the fault
of the Churches. And,
of course, it is. It is
the Churches’ business
to keep the kids from
growing up into heath-
ens, and if they are
growing up into heath-
ens, then the Churches
are neglecting their
job. If the men who
get paid for providing
the youngsters with
trousers said the youngsters were going naked on
account of the disgraceful conduct of the boot-
makers, the public would drop to the meanness of
the trouser-makers. But somehow there are quite
a lot of people who still swallow the parson’s yarn
when he blames “ this Godless education,”
Proposed by Alderman Meagher to build
a tramway to Mrs. Macquarie’s Chair, Sydney
Domain ! Only in a city which is still municipally
in the hands of Goths and Vandals could such a
proposal be made. The Inner Domain, bounded
on one side by the harbor and on the other by
the Botanic Gardens, is the one really quiet
spot amid Sydney’s clang and roar. Hyde
Park is noisy, surrounded as it is on
all sides by tram lines. Wynyard Square
is just a dot of green in a wilderness of traffic ; but
the point which is known as “ Lady Macquarie’s
Chair ”is a haven of peacefulness. The man who
proposes to bring to it the clang and disturbance
of the electric car would put switchback railways
along the heights of Heaven.
WHERE THE TROUBLE GAME IN.
SHE: “I lost on every race. And I wore my opal bangle, too, and I was certain it would
bring me luck.”
HE (soothingly): “ Well, so it would, my dear, if you'd backed the right horse.”
It transpires now that between the time of
the arrival of news of the Sunshine smash at
Spencer-street (Melb.), and the departure of the
first ambulance train, the military ambulance corps
arrived at that station from the Easter encampment,
bringing with it tools, gear, doctors, first-aid men,
and all the rest of it. But not one railway official
in Tait’s muddled battalion had nous enough to
hitch the cars to an engine, and run them out to
Sunshine; and men who were eager to gain ex-
perience that happily doesn’t come their way every
day were allowed to go off home in complete
ignorance of what had happened. That prompt
first-aid might easily have saved at least half-a-
dozen lives, besides much suffering.
* * * *
“Engineer” submits his matured opinion :
Now that the main facts of the Sunshine disaster have
been told, the cause of the smash seems fairly clear. It
may he assumed that the heavy train came past the dis-
tant signal at 40 miles per hour or more, and, nearing the
home signal, the driver put on the brakes hard. The
night was a trifle foggy and the rails would be greasy, so
the wheels right along the train skidded. The evidence
in favor of this is the assertion of a passenger seated in
the Ballarat train, who looked out of the window and then
told her friend alongside that “ a train was coming up be-
hind and that its wheels were on fire.” The skidding
would produce more sparks than the brakes. Further
(and this is to me conclusive proof that the brakes were
applied), the Bendigo engines crashed into the Ballarat
guard’s van and the carriages in front of it at perhaps 40
miles per hour, yet was stopped dead in 40 yards. Had the
Bendigo carriages behind those engines been running
free, the foremost cars must inevitably have been tele-
scoped. Yet not a passenger was hurt. Only the
luggage was tumbled from the racks, as is frequently
the case when a train is stopped suddenly.
The skidding of the wheels would have partially
braked the comparatively light, many-wheeled cars, but
would have little effect on the 90-ton engines. (A care-
ful examination of the wheels on the whole of the Bendigo
train would probably reveal slight “flats” on the
running faces ; but the cars would have to be raised and
the wheels spun against a testing chalk.) The theory
that the isolation cock on the brake of the second engine
was open is untenable. Had that been the case, when
the brake was applied (and, as shown above, the carriages
must have been braked) the train would have experienced
a series of jerks that would have torn the couplings to
pieces and shaken the very lives out of the passengers.
So much for the direct cause of the smash. The indirect
causes were many, and, of course, cannot be touched on
at present. * * *
By the end of this year, train travellers
in the Eastern States will be able to eat some of
their fried sausage while the machine bumps them
along over Tait’s lines. For a start the new
dining cars will be kept in Victoria ; but the
S. Aus. Railway Commissioner wants to turn
his long-distance trains into tucker trains, too,
and is only waiting for Parliament to vote the
money; so within 12 months the Melbourne-
Adelaide traveller probably won’t have to leave
the train to do any wayside feeding. On the
Sydney line the feeding cage will only run as far
as Albury ; and for the Melbourne-Mild ura line
a cookshop is to be set up on one of the old
Adelaide “sleepers” which have been partly
thrown out of use by the new and gorgeous cars.
So far, the only regular dining-car in Australia
runs out from Perth to the Goldfields, and that
was introduced chiefly because of the tremendous
difficulty experienced in capturing anything much
except gohanna when the line gets past Northam,
on the way to the Goldfields. On lines where the
feeding car won’t run, Tait has an idea of instal-
ling a sort of hot spud barrow, which he is going
to have pushed up and down the platform while
the engine is busy at the water tanks. So The
Bulletin sees still another Tait-created superin-
tendent looming in the distance probably
Superintendent of Peas and Pies.

* • * «
An Australian woman, of a persistent turn
of mind, acquired much experience but little
satisfaction the other day. Tripping into a
butcher’s shop she bought a pound of chops, but
on reaching home she found they were quite un-
fit for human food. It wasn’t the first time that
had happened, so she decided to “ teach the
butcher a lesson.” Rolling the chops up again
she visited an inspector of Foods for Export, who,
having smelt the chops distantly, recommended
her to take them back and change them. But she
wasn’t that kind of woman ; so she went to the
Public Health o'fice at the other end of the
city, where an official viewed the meats, and said
they were very bad, and suggested that she go to
the Town Hall. She went there, and encoun-
tered a gentleman typewriting. He inspected
the mutton, and opined that its funeral had
been somewhat delayed. He also said he would
go and hunt up an inspector. Presently an
inspector arrived. He viewed the remnants of
deceased sheep once more, and then faded away to
bring a medical officer. By the time that person
reached the scene the meat had to be nearly held
down by force ; but he and the lady ultimately
escorted it back to the butcher’s. That cute
tradesman knew a health officer at a glance, and,
of course, no bad meat was to be found in his
emporium ; so the officer assured the lady that
she must have got all the bad meat in the shop.
Then he said “Good afternoon,” and invited her
to call on him again should she buy any more bad
meat.
* • * *
Plaint by The Bulletin’s Oldest Inhabi-
tant :
Although we have an Historical Society, which is doing
its best to educate Australians, the newspapers blunder
daily. Alluding to the demolition of the oldest building
in the neighborhood of the Sydney Exchange, a morning
journal says that the structure, hovel, or caboose in ques-
tion was erected bv one Thomas Reiby, who, in 1809, was
described as a “ pilot in Sydney Harbor,” and that many
years ago it was the site of the business of the Bank of
New South Wales. As a matter of fact, the building was
erected a century ago by Simeon Lord, a colonist from
the early 1790’5. We find him mentioned as a prosperous
man in 1798, as a merchant and Government contractor
in 1800, as a shipowner a little later on ; and as having a
quarrel with Bligh, and being imprisoned through the
action of that Governor. He was also one of those
who signed the memorial to Johnston for the
deposition of Governor Bligh. His name appears
fourth on the memorial, being sandwiched be-
tween those of Surgeon Mileham and Garnham Blaxcell.
In and before Bligh’s time he held a lease of the land, an
acre and a quarter, on which the recently-demolished
building stood. This Acting-Governor Foveaux converted
into a freehold, in consideration of the large sums of
money laid out in the erection of the dwelling-house and
stores. The grant was wiped out as illegal, but Mac-
quarie regranted the land. Lord had grants of thou-
sands of acres about Sydney, and was certainly an ener-
getic colonist. We find him the pioneer of the woollen
weaving industry, and of the hat industry, at his Botany
mills. A son became Treasurer in the Martin Ministry of
1870. Thomas Raby (original spelling) settled in Sydney
about 1792. In the ship came also a young girl known
as Mary Haydock. This girl Tom Raby married, and he
then set up in business as a merchant. While carrying
on business as a merchant he became a pilot in Sydney
Harbor ; but death removed him a couple of years after
his appointment. The name was changed to Reibey, and
as Mrs. Reibey the widow carried on business for many
years. She died in 1855. Thomas Raby’s house was at
the end of Macquarie Place, opposite to that of Simeon
Lord, and in Rabv’s house the Bank of New South Wales
commenced business in 1817. Thomas Reibey, now 90
years of age, an ex-Premier of Tasmania, is a grandson of
t his Tom Raby.

At a meeting of the Tenterfield Agricul-
tural Society (2/5/’08) a letter was read from the
P. and A. Union, Sydney, confirming the dis-
qualification for years passed by the Inverell
Society on a couple of Mullumbimby sports. Also
th® Sydney P. and A. Union excused the mare
Ada K., the property of the twain, from further
show service for a like period. The combination
had offended by jumping Ada K. as a “maiden
hunter” at Inverell, she having already won at
Tenterfield as Lady Stace, and at Glen Innes as
Melba. _ Which suggests things. There would
be a big void in the Show entry schedule
if all the horses which have competed
in the maiden classes, albeit prize-winners
elsewhere, were warned off; and if all the
owners who have knowingly rung in such
animals as maidens were dealt with, there
would be a hideously depleted list of entries.
-Lhe maiden 5 exhibit that is a maiden but in
name is getting to be outrageously frequent. At the
Sydney Show, a few years back, the first three in
a maiden event were all subsequently disqualified
on the excellent ground that they had won prizes
all over the State previously. In each case mere
flukes led to the discovery of the impostures.
“Stony”: It was an occasion for dolor
when Imabel reached the eve of Em-
pire Day without an invitation from her
steady William to spend the day where
the briny breezes dash themselves against
the shore-line. At that stage, William,
being in another suburb on “lodge busi-
ness,” Bess Jones arrived with a proposi-
tion that Imabel should join a “dragger”
down the coast—two bob a head. Imabel
agreed, and went off next day without
having seen her steady William in the
meantime. During Empire morning,
steady William browsed around to where
Imabel’s mother was hanging out a clo’ or
two in the back-yard, and demanded news
of his vanished love. Ma explained that
Imabel had gone off for a beanfeast toMor-
dialloc. Then steady William, full of
wrath and remorse, girt up his necktie
and tore for the next train. Arriving at
Mordialloc, he set forth upon a feverish
search for his one and only, and found
her all too soon. She came obliviously
down the road which wandered beside the
sea and shore, and in her hand she held a
man, while a masculine arm clasped her
slender waist. Other maidens were strung
out beyond with other men, to make a
gay, laughing line of Pagans, who sang as
they strode and laughed as they sang;
and they poked borak at steady William
as he wilted by, the voice of Imabel not
the smallest among those that slungper-
siflage—until Imabel saw her error. Then
it was too late, for steady William had
fled from the sound of revelry and taken
to cigarettes and beer, therein to drown
his sorrow and his love for ever.

* * * *
Tamworth, the Peel River (N.S.W.) town
with the fine land and the big landlord, is putting
up something like a record. A few months ago
its boss-parson resigned, because his flock wouldn’t
put on enough shepherds, wherefore he put on a
couple at his own cost, and then found that he
had about 17s. lid. a week left to starve on. Sub-
sequently, after no end of a commotion had been
made, the boss-parson was invited to withdraw
his resignation; whereupon he replied that,
though his flock professed to be amazed at his
disclosures, he noticed that it hadn’t done any-
thing to improve the finances, and so his resigna-
tion would have to stand. Now Tamworth
seems to be quite proud about the re-
cord of its hospital matron. Typhoid patients
poured into the hospital last summer, and the
place was under-staffed, and nurses, one after
another, wore themselves out; but the matron hung
on to her work, until she finally collapsed, and died
from no visible cause except sheer over-work.
Tamworth therefore talks vaguely about establish
ing “a permanent memorial to one who by her
actions fatally recognised that there was so much
to do and so little time to do it.” Another way of
putting it would be that the self-sacrificing woman
found so much to do and so few people paid to do
it.
The Light Horse regiments did not go
into Easter camp this year in N. 8. Wales. There
was lack of horse-trucks to carry their mounts,
owing to the demands made on the rolling stock
by the drought-stricken pastures of Riverina Of
course it would not be good policy to allow
starving sheep to wait whilst horse-soldiers were
brought into camp. But are not Light Horse
supposed to be mobile? Could they not have
moved on their own horses to district camps ? Of
all branches of the service the cavalry should be
least dependent on train transport.

Marshall Hall, having regarded with sur-
prise and scorn the proposal that he should be
conductor of the May Day musical programme for
the Yarra Bankers, has been further spoken to
briefly, more in sorrow than in anger. Thus the
May Day secretary in his best official manner :
The grotesqueness of the view that you could be in the
remotest degree interested in the common people has
dawned upon me since receiving your illuminat-
ing letter, and I now see the audacity of the out-
rage. ... I can therefore endure with exemplary
equanimity the inconsequential irrelevancies of your
communication. Your letter is an admirable example
of pure bunkum, as, although I carefully fur-
nished you with ample material for intelligent criticism
of the committee’s aims and methods, you deliberately
refrained from so doing, but devoted your space to com-
ments on the Marseillaise (the classic hymn of modern
democracy) which might have more appropriately
emanated from an antediluvian cave dweller. . . . The
translation you criticise was made by Richard Brinsley
Sheridan. But Sheridan is dead, and a live puppy can
bark at a dead lion with impunity. . . . The committee
begged you to help establish equitable conditions, and
your luminous (save the mark !) reply is—a futile, fatuous
screed, dealing with a non-essential detail. Robert
Burns, in his abounding charity, did not despair of the
Devil himself. Surely your redemption is not beyond
hope !
Also, surely Hell hath no fury like a May Day
scorned.
N.S.W. Public Instruction Department
is embarking on a promising novelty in the shape
of a travelling bush school. The idea is to fit up
a suitable vehicle as a small academy and send it
perambulating round the sparsely populated
districts on a fixed itinerary, after the manner of
gallivanting dentists, opticians and herbal cure-
alls, The idea must have some good results;
through it a certain crowd of children will at
least get a fleeting glimpse of a schoolmaster,
where now they never see or hear of him at all.
But if the scheme is to be a big success, the
circus school must be placed in charge of a gifted
enthusiast not a Departmental misfit, whose
qualification is that he is Z 3, and that the van
is also Z 3. An eminently adaptable man is
wanted, and a man may be Z 3, without being
adaptable.

How tiresome is the youth who endeavors
to get drunk upon 9£d. and a few odd cigarettes.
When a couple of these chaps (they are not blokes)
get together they have a very mild soda and
whisky, exchange hats, light up their fags, and go
marching down the street singing a musical
hall ditty, and hiccoughing at judicious
intervals. They tilt their straw-
deckers back upon their heads,
and gaze admiringly at one
another, and consider
themselves devils of fel-
lows. They repeat this
weary performance about
once every five weeks,
and next morning they
tell their co-workers,
“ Had a night out las’
night. Painted the town
red. Rottenly big head,
haw.” And their fame'
spreads, and they get
cheap reputations as
rakes, and thus become heroes in the eyes of:
persons who wear skirts and admire devils.

The WILD CAT
COLUMN
The month’s returns show that the State re-
venues have, on the whole, got into the backwash.
Certainly, owing partly to some doubt as to what would
be done with regard to a few Customs duties, the Com-
monwealth receipts, and consequently the Commonwealth
refunds, were less than they were this time last year;
but apart from that there was considerable sagging.
Railways in Victoria, which has got another taste
of drought, returned £47,350 less; and, though the
revenue from that source kept up in N. S. Wales, land
revenue—which includes land-sale money—shrank to the
extent of £47,385. Queensland alone shows larger figures
for April, 1908, than for April, 1907, and that was wholly
due to the Commonwealth refunds, which for that State
show no shrinkage. Even in Queensland land revenue
fell off to the extent of £17,673. Here are the totals for
the month, so far as they can be ascertained :
State Revenues for April.
April, 1907. April, 1908.
N. S. Wales .. £1,318,790 £1,179,587 Dec. £139,203
Victoria .. 781,182 660,019 Dec. 121,163
Queensland .. 480,285 476,908 Dec. 3,377
Westralia .. 279,968 280,273 Inc. 305
Tasmania .. 103,602 72,231 Dec. 31,371
S. Australia, which has got used to horse trams and other
leisurely proceedings, can’t manage monthly returns. Its
figures to the end of March are, however, included in the
next summary :
State Revenues for Year so far.
1906-7. 1907-8.
£ £ £
N. S. Wales (10 mos.) ..11,034,190 11,839,991 Inc. 805,801
Victoria (10 mos.) .. 6,771,921 6,972,514 Inc. 200,593
Queensland (10 mos.) .. 3,469,292 3,759,920 Inc. 290,628
Westralia (10 mos.) .. 2,771,181 2,771,486 Inc. 305
Tasmania (10 mos.) .. 791,994 829,688 Inc. 37,694
S. Australia (9 mos.) .. 2,231,586 2,705,552 Inc. 473,966
That looks better; but the bulk of the increases comes
from the earlier part of the year, when the boom was still
in progress. It isn’t only that here and there drought
has looked in again ; the fall in prices is a serious matter
too. Statist Knibbs, in the new Commonwealth Year-
book, shows what the effect of the boom-prices of the past
two or three years amounted to. In 1906 the Common-
wealth’s exports, apart from gold, were valued at
£52,842,704 ; but if they were valued on the basis of 1901
prices (and they are swinging back that way now) the
total would have been only £42,295,310. Small things
like that were disregarded when Carruthers and Wade
between them recently cut off large slices of N. S. Wales’
future revenue.
As to expenditure, this is all that is available :
Expenditure for Year so far.
1906-7. 1907-8.
£ £ £
N. S. Wa1e5(10m05.)..10,435,935 10,478,451 Inc. 42,516
Queensland (10 mos.).. 2,719,060 2,951,497 Inc. 232,437
Westralia (10 mos.) .. 2,780,012 2,706,718 Dec. 73,294
On that basis N. S. Wales at present shows a surplus of
£1,361,540; but, as the months of large receipts are
past, the total will probably be reduced to about
£1,000,000 by the end of the financial year (June 30). This
is called surplus, though last year land-sales money
amounted to £1,029,439, and the total will most likely be
as great, if not greater, this year. Consequently, as this
money isn’t revenue, but the realisation of assets which
can’t be realised more than once, N. S. Wales will have an
actualdeficit even iftheGovernment’s promised “surplus”
of £1,000,000 comes to pass. As for the other States,
there is either no information or no change of any con-
sequence.
While N. S. Wales’ Ministers get on the top
branch and flap their wings as they crow about the
surplus that isn’t a surplus, they don’t do a single flap
over the enormous increase of their borrowings. For the
first 10 months of 1906-7 the loan expenditure was
£1,082,608; for the past 10 months it has been
“Reformed ” until it has reached £1,764,255, or at
the rate of over £2,000,000 a year. Heaven only
knows what dizzier heights it would reach if they
had a “surplus” of £2,000,000 a year! Mean-
time, however, this Government is kept in office
with the help of some members who alleged that
they were full up of the borrowing curse, and pro-
posed for the future to insist upon C.O.D. principles.
Possibly some of them have been deceived by the
absurd yarns told by Treasurers and others who have
talked about their large repayments; but, if they
scratched only an inch below the surface, they could
find out that there isn’t one single State that hasn’t
actually increased its debt in the past two years. As for
N. S. Wales, Carruthers and his “ Reform ” party, includ-
ing Wade and Ashton, threw Waddell out in 1904 on the
ground that he was a financial incompetent and a spend-
thrift ; yet the Commonwealth Statist shows that between
June, 1904, and June, 1907, the N. S. Wales public debt
sprang from £80,033,581 to £85,607,832 ; and the increase
since June, 1907, has been more rapid than at any time
previously during this period of “ Reform.”

These are rather ancient figures ; before the returns can
get from Australia to England, where the head-office is
and most of the lords and other shareholders live, over
three months pass ; and nearly another three months go
by before the accounts get back to Australia, hall-marked
by this time with the familiar names of Blogg, accountant,
and Jeans, manager. Of the great profit shown during
the past two terms, some, it is admitted, has come from
the recovery of debts formerly written off as bad ; but
how much this amounts to nobody knows who doesn’t
know more than the published accounts tell. If there
are considerable recoveries now, there must have been
unduly large amounts written off profits a while ago ;
wherefore, though recent profits can’t be regarded as
normal, being too high, neither can those of a few terms
previously be regarded as normal, because they
were probably too low. The amounts actually
shown as written off have throughout gone to-
wards reducing the premises account, which now
stands at only £239,572 ; and the properties in Sydney
and Melbourne alone can’t be worth much short of that.
This would mean that, so far as the books and the
accounts go, the bank gets all its office-rent outside those
cities free, and any rents from office premises let off to
tenants comes in the shape of a bonus to the bank ;
further, the profits which have been used to write
premises down in this way—£2o2,ooo in the past six years
alone—are presumably available for ordinary banking busi-
ness. So a position which is strong on the surface is
stronger when it is examined a little underneath.
A further comparison :

proportion, which partly accounts for the high earnings
shown. These later, based on capital and reserves, have

The past half-year’s profits have, by the way, been

The Bank of Australasia, which works under a
charter, finds its deed of settlement antiquated, and in
some respects harassing, and it proposes to bring the
machine up to date by a round-about road, which, on
inspection, looks the most passable. A while ago, when,
rather than put up their dividend beyond 10 per cent.,
the Bank of N. S. Wales and the Commercial of Sydney
increased their capital, it was explained that the ’Asia
couldn’t conveniently do the same thing, because of some-
thing in its constitution. Probably the new up-to-date
machine is intended in part to remedy the defect.

The deposits and other debts to the public total £8,882,853;
the shareholders’ capital and reserves amount to
£1,672,918. So for every £1 of its own capital the bank
has £5 6s. 3d. of the public’s, which is a very fair, but not
large, proportion.
With that proportion, however, the earnings
ought to be more, calculated on the basis of capital and
reserves combined, than the following list shows they
have been :
Sept., 1904 .. £4 12 1 p.c. Sept., 1906 .. £5 9 10 p.c.
Mar., 1905 .. 4 9 7 „ Mar., 1907 .. 517 1 „
Sept., 1905 .. 4 9 1 „ Sept., 1907 .. 6 0 1 „
Mar., 1906 .. 5 1 3 „ Mar., 1908 .. 5 1 5 „
There are two or three possible explanations : (1) A good
deal of the assets may still not be very productive ; (2)
some undisclosed profits may be going towards writing
down the just-mentioned assets. This is all the more
likely—the former especially—because this bank did
nothing very heroic in the way of cleaning-up after the
’93 earthquake. It then had 250,000 ordinary shares paid
to £4 ; upon these it called up £3 and wrote off £2,
leaving them at their present £5. Then for many
years it had to pay mostly a too-high 4} per cent, on
its reconstructed deposits ; so, one way and another,
there has probably been a good deal of very doubtful
remnants to struggle with and gradually write down
during the past few years. As one consequence, the bank
hasn’t been able to materially reduce its premises account
until now—it was £449,826 five years ago, and in the
present balance-sheet it stands at £443,509. That there
has been a pretty extensive cleaning-up in these recent
years may be judged from the fact that the real estate
item, apart from bank premises, has been reduced in the
same five years from £337,837 to £63,201—an excellent
feature of the accounts. It was noted above that there
had been a severe shrinkage of liquid assets. Yet, in
spite of that, what may be called the cash position is
still strong ; 34 per cent, of the total liabilities is repre-
sented by cash, public securities, etc. As to the future,
one thing has to be remembered : in any future increase
of dividend, the preference people must share as well as
the ordinary, so it will take more to pay an extra 1 per
cent, than it has been doing hitherto. Pref. shares are
only pref. as to capital until they have had 10 half-yearly
divs. of 7 per cent, or more.

Down to about the middle of last year there were 110,000
shares fully paid to £2O in this co. Then it was decided
to issue another 15,000 shares, in the proportion of three
new to every 22 old shares. Of the £2O payable for such
new shares the co. contributed £5 out of reserves, and
the shareholders have either paid or are paying the other
£15 —there are only 320 shares on which the full £2O has
not been paid. For £l5, therefore, the old shareholders
got a £2O share which at once carries 10 p.c. ; and that
really makes the present dividend equal to £lO 6s. per
cent, all round. The authorised capital is £3,000,000 ; so
the way is clear for further pleasant little extensions of
capital as circumstances may warrant. Twenty years ago
the paid capital was £648,720 ; that was the year after
the co. absorbed the New Zealand Sugar Co. and in the
year that the Victorian Sugar Co. was swallowed.
Even thus early, though it was then only paying
7 per cent, divs., the co. had acquired the
art of throwing a small disguise over its profits
by the process (now repeated) of transferring
reserves to capital. Ten years later the paid capital had
been increased to £1,702,000. The dividend by that time
had got up to 8 per cent. ; it reached 10 per cent, in 1890,
and it has kept there ever since, except that a. few years
ago the co. scattered an extra £50,000 or so in cash to
celebrate its 50th anniversary. What the real profits are
from term to term it is impossible to say, because the
amounts deducted from gross profits to provide for de-
preciation, doubtful debts, etc., are never disclosed. It was
no doubt because it was found that these internal reserves
had grown unnecessarily bulky that the extra £50,000
was first thrown into profits and then divided in 1905.
The one speck on the future of this co. is the doubt whether,
when it gets a complete monopoly of the sugar-supply of
Australia, it will be allowed to keep it. Little by little it
is getting a monopoly : within the last few weeks, for
instance, it bought up the Ripple Creek mill, in the
Herbert district (Q.), and for the future all the cane
thereabouts will have to go to the C.S.R. mill. So for as
the investor is concerned, the risk is that some day the
Commonwealth will get alarmed and will decide that it is
time for Private Enterprise (without competition) to go
out and public ownership to come in.

The depreciation shown is on “properties, buildings,

can be traced, run to £277,335, so the amount written off
is over 7 per cent, of book value ; yet another £lO,OOO is

other large items—merchandise stock, £175,147; and
shares in shipping and other cos., £70,634. Much or little
may be written off these items—it is impossible to tell.
One can only judge about what he can’t see from the
things he can see ; and on that basis it looks likely that a
safe policy is adopted with regard to these items, through
which, in other circumstances, a lot of trouble and
disappointment might creep in. Apart from ordinary
trade debts, which run to £148,504, the co. owes nothing;
on the other side it has book debts amounting to
£235,972 ; and, besides the merchandise stock shown
above, there was £79,779 represented by island produce
on hand and in transit and sundry advances. The paid
capital of the co. is £300,000, of which £250,000 is in paid
shares (£1), and £50,000 in shares paid to 10s., and liable
for another 10s. The other way of looking at this last
feature is that the 10s. is liable to be called up in order
to share in the 10 per cent, divs., and help to absorb
the bulky profits. #
