This society, being registered under the Queensland
Building Societies Act, is consequently barred from
taking deposits at call, or total deposits exceeding three
times the amount of the paid capital. That limit
is far from being reached. The paid capital is £72,857
(in addition to £2275 credited to subscribing members),
and the deposits amount to only £61,336. From the
depositor’s point of view, the position is therefore very
strong. And it is still stronger because £13,157 is in cash
or bank balances. Deducting that amount from the
deposits (£61,336) and other liabilities (£2290), the society
owes £50,469, against which it has £124,168 of advances and
£7OOO of freeholds, or £2 12s. of assets for every £1 of
debt to the public. But while this means excellent
security for creditors, and -comparatively small risk for
shareholders, it also means comparatively poor profits.
The net earnings for the year show only £4 11s. 4d. per
cent, on capital and reserves. That, however, is after
writing off £7lB, of which £5OO was transferred from profits
to contingency account, where it disappeared. There
is no way of tracing the year’s property transactions ; but
the chairman told the shareholders’ meeting that “ of the
properties in possession about one-sixth of those held at
the beginning of the year have been sold.” In regard to
realising house properties, Brisbane concerns have had a
slight advantage over most others. Suburban Brisbane is
mostly timbet\ and as timber has moved up a great deal
in price, concerns with wood-and-iron buildings on their
hands have had visits from people who previously would
have gone to the building contractor.

Besides the amounts shown above as written off, the fol-
lowing have been charged direct to profit and loss for
plant depreciation during the past 18 months : —March,
’O7, £2035 ; September, ’O7, £2257; March, ’OB, £2851. Of
the amounts shown as written off in the past 12 months,
£6OOO further has gone to reduce the plant account.
Mining properties have been written down heavily for
years past, and now stand in the books at only £15,499.
This includes the East Greta freehold and the Stanford
Merthyr leasehold. Besides these two mines, the co.
owns and runs the East Greta railway ; land, railway and
sidings stand as an asset for £61,685, and plant (including
mine plant) for £101,124. In spite of the large surplus of
earnings in excess of dividends (in the past three years
£29,239 has been paid in dividends, and £26,369 has been
written off or put to reserves), the rapid growth of busi-
ness on the East Greta line has produced an overdraft of
£15,255 on “new rolling stock account.”, On current
account, however, there is a substantial credit balance ;
wherefore, at the end of the half-year, as against liabilities
of £35,741 (including the current dividend), there were
liquid assets totalling £31,926. Colliery investments
carry, of course, their own special risks—tire, explosion,
and labor troubles; and the East Greta has quite the
average of those risks. But it is unusually well off in
two respects—though the co. is only ten years old, it has
written its coal properties down until they represent only
about one-tenth of the paid capital; and it has a railway
line serving a coal district which has moved rapidly to
the front, and seems certain to have a great future.

This co., after a long and profitable life, found its old
mine worked out, and it had no money to buy or open
up a new one. On account of its improvidence it
deserved to go out of existence ; but it had plenty of
assurance, and, besides, it had a business connection and
a plant worth anythingfrom £20,000 to £30,000. Itspaid
capital had for some years stood at about £103,000 ; and,
though it has only got in another £5OOO, it has managed
to secure a new leasehold colliery—the Neath, at South
Maitland—and develop it so rapidly that for the past half-
year the output was no less than 54,000 tons. Circum-
stances have been all in its favor, of course ; no sooner
had it begun to open up the new mine than it found an
exceptionally good coal market waiting on the doorstep.
The result is that, though it got in so little fresh capital,
its liabilities are now only £16,541 in excess of cash and
book debts—a remarkably small price to pay for a new
lease of life. The co.’s capital is in three classes of shares
—old ordinary, paid to £1 ; new ordinary, paid to 18s.;
and 12£ per cent, pref., paid to £l. Of the last lot
there are, fortunately for the rest, only 20,000; but 12£
per cent, even on that little bundle means £2500 a year
before the others can have a bite.

Out of the profits it was proposed to pay 17£ per cent.
(£28,646) this month (April), and to transfer £44,000 to
the investment reserve and contingency fund ; but those
appropriations will not be shown till next year. In the
foregoing this contingency fund is not reckoned among

to £lOl,OOO. At the end of 1903 it amounted to only

Twelve months ago it was announced that, finding that
fire-insurance cos. were cutting into accident business, this
co. had decided to cut in at fire business ; but to what
extent that accounts for the big growth of income isn’t
nuHiifest from the published statements. The tremendous
figures of the Ocean are mainly due to the fact that it got
vOry early into a new business in England, where it
mostly lives, and then found out (at considerable cost)
what branches of the business were best left alone. But
there are so many branches of the other sort that last
year the gross revenue was about nine times greater than
the total capital; and so, though the claims amounted to
47-5 per cent, of total income, and the expense rate was
36'2 per cent., a profit remained equal to 135 per cent, of
the paid capital.
Another of the chickens of Parkes’s “born
financier,” William McMillan, is moulting. For years the
Farmers and Settlers’Co-operative Society and McMillans
principal business (McArthur and Co.) have heen so
mixed up that nobody knew where one started and the
other left off. Last week the Farmers and Settlers, etc.,
issued a circular to the effect that it had gone out of busi-
ness except as a grain and live-stock agent. Yet it was
the grain business that landed the society in the difficulties
from which it has never been able to thoroughly free
itself. Now, this week, there is a notice intimating that
liquidation is proposed. * #
There are times when Sydney seems to actually
encourage the daring, not to say reckless, trader. A
firm of accountants a while ago offered an assignedstocK,
book debts, etc., and notified that the man hadn t been
much more than two years in business. Yet, though ne
was such a beginner, and had no capital at all to star
with, and then only started as some sort of an agent, tne
accounts showed over £5500 deficiency, even assuming
that the assets realised book value, which wasn t likei).
The unsecured creditors were owed nearly £II,UOU.
large amount of this was for goods and accommodat
bills got from small tradesmen, while the secured credi
were in nearly every case money-lenders. In other wor ,
this trader transferred practically the whole of his a
to a few money-lenders, and left the dozens

Australian Office :
Equitable Building, Melbourne.
The Aerial Tramways which have been erected
for the British and Sulphide Corporation (Central) Mines
at Broken Hill are fine examples of present-day skill and
technical knowledge. The Mines will effect considerable
saving by means of these Aerialways as compared with
any other known mechanical contrivance for handling
ores on a large scale. The British Mine will economise
to over 2s. per ton. These two plants have already been
subjected to severe tests, and have amply demonstrated
their worth and ability. Both Aerialways were supplied
by W. & J. Leinpriere, of Melbourne, who represent A.
Bleichert & Co., of London, the largest firm in the world
for the manufacture of transport appliances.*
* * * *
Adams’ Asbestos Boiler and Pipe Covering.
Gives better results than any other. It saves from 20
per cent, in fuel, keeps steam pressure uniform, prevents
condensation in steam pipe, is very light in weight, and
easily applied. I bag covers 40 super, square feet 1 inch
thick; price 20s. per bag. Wm. Adams and Co.. Ltd.
163 Clarence-street, Sydney.*
After conducting exhaustive tests in the con-
centration of the ores at the Broken Hill South mine, the
manager, Mr. Wainwright, has finally decided to instal
the Wilflev type of table, modified in the direction best
adapted to. his particular local requirements. The great
advantage with this type of table is that it admits of so
many slight variations which adapt it to meet all classes
of pulverised ores. Provided the pulp for treatment is
graded it can be used to treat all varieties of ore from
slimes up to coarse grades. When treating slimes and
lightly mineralised ores, care must be taken to have the
slope of the table accurate, and a fine, smooth dressing
surface beyond the feather edged diagonal termination of
the riffles. When treating coarsely ground ores or ores
heavily charged wjth mineral, as at the Broken Hill South,
the riffles or grooves can be carried further along the table
and in some instances right up to the concentrates dis-
charge end. Mr. Wainwright ordered six tables as an
experiment, with fewer riffles or grooves than customary,
and with some of them—the lower ones—carried to the
extreme end of the table. So successful has this type of
Wilfley table proved that he has ordered 50 more from
the Austral Otis Engineering Company. This same firm
has been placing several of the patent Callow Screens at
Broken Hill and elsewhere. This ingenious and simple
mechanism has solved the problem of grading prior to
concentration.* rs

of our mining districts firewood is becoming so scarce
that economy of fuel is of the first importance, so that
the old-fashioned “steam chewers”have to stand aside

power with the least expenditure of fuel. This is where
a Corliss Engine “ comes in.” Cameron and Sutherland
have several of these engines in stock, from 120 h.p. to
200 h.p. They are practically new, and the price is low.
Also a large assortment of high pressure boilers suitable
for all requirements. Address—Post Office Chambers,

Street, Ballarat.*

The engineering and pipe works of Mephan
Ferguson, Melbourne, present a very busy appearance
just now. We notice in course of construction two steam
boilers, with mountings, uptakes, funnels, etc., for the
Melbourne Harbor Trust, also a number of dredge
buckets for same Trust; stamper shoes, dies and cams,
heavy and light forgings for mining plants, shipping,
etc. ; also fluming, bolts, nuts, washers, Helyambo scoops,
and every description of contractors’.iron work. Amongst
machinery completed is a new hydraulic multiple
punching machine, weighing over 20 tons, and capable of
punching 120 holes in 10 seconds, with a pressure of 520
tons per stroke. This machine will be used in the manu-
facture of 48in. and 54in. diameter pipes for the Melbourne'
water supply, a contract for which has been signed.*

The Electrolytic Refining and Smelting
Company of Australia, Ltd., have placed their order for
complete boiler-house equipment with Babcock and
Wilcox Ltd., Haymarket, Sydney. The order, which com-
prises five large boilers, each with superheater and
mechanical stoker, one economiser and two induced
draft fans, was obtained in keen competition with other
makes of water-tube boilers. Babcock and Wilcox have
also received an order from the same firm for a Reavell
Air Compressor for which they are sole licensees in
Australia.*

engineering world has come a demand for more powerful
machine tools. To meet this, J. and P. Hill, of Sheffield,
have introduced a high-speed screw cutting lathe, which
has been specially designed to avoid complicated motions,
which may cause trouble, and to enable it to successfully
combat the severest strains. Gibson, Battle and Co.,
Ltd., 7 Bent Street, Sydney, have just landed two of these
lathes, and those interested in machine tools should not
lose the opportunity of inspecting them.*

* * * * •
The Moore Government is resolved on hitting
J. Bull Cohen in the eye at the Shepherd’s Bush Exhibf-
tion. Many rich specimens have been borrowed or
bought, and a “ month’s output ” is being borrowed to
set off the collection. The main object ot all this window-
dressing is to distract Cohen’s attention from dwindling
gold returns and failing mines—to distract it long enough
at least, to borrow a few more millions.
• * * „
Golden Horseshoe’s recent development at
2000 ft. is being made much of in the West. The point
where values were struck is just across the western
boundary in the newly acquired Ivanhoe South Extended
lease, and a westerly underlay is pushing it further into
that property. Whether the ore is a continuation of the
western branch of the Ivanhoe middle lode ora new make
has not yet been settled. The local papers are doinu- a
good deal of premature hifalutin about the find having
“an important bearing on the future of the field.” It
would be wiser to wait and see if it extends laterally.
Boulder Perseverance (W. A.) announces another
batch of “good developments” in depth. Unfortunately
however, the mine has had to play second fiddle to the
market for such a long time that no one takes any notice
of “ new finds ” nowadays. The property may still have
years of profitable work ahead of it, but, despite the
sophistry talked to shareholders, dividends will be much
leaner in the future.
• * * *
Brown Hill Extended (W. A.) still keeps a tight
hold on the cash in hand and an eye on developments in
adjoining mines. A bit of rich telluride ore—the remnant
of the Brown Hill shoot—still remains to be taken out but
theproperty hasn’t much apart from this asset, so the erec-
tion of a treatment plant would be uncalled for No
doubt the block will be absorbed later on by the Orova
Brown Hill, and that is possibly the best thing that could"
happen.
* * * *
Nothing satisfactory has yet resulted from the
arrangement made some months ago for the joint work-
ing of the Hannan’s Star and Boulder Deep Levels
(W.A.). The combined cos. have still about £40,000 in
hand, but neither seems anxious to lay out this sum
There is plenty of ore in sight, but a suitable treatment
plant would cost at least £30,000, and even then no one
could guaiantee success. As a result shares are unsale-
able, and local holders are clamoring for the Government
to step in and do something. Liquidation, in the case of
the Hannan’s Star, would mean a dividend of 4s per
share; but, as this paper previously remarked, 4s. is'onlv
worth 4s. when you can get it. J
* * * *
The Ivanhoe Junction, mine and plant, is to be
sold by tender. As traffickers in stolen gold can defy the
law in Westraha by holding a lease as a blind even this
property may find a buyer. After that the local papers
may gravely announce from time to time that good ore
has been struck, and that a rich parcel of dirt is bein«-
raised. As for the plant, that part of it which will stand
removal will probably find its way back to the second-
hand machinery depot.
« * * ,
Chairman Fimister spun a doleful yarn at the
Golden Pole meeting (W. A.). Sounding what is likely to
be the death knell of profitable mining, he announced
that the first two months of the current half-year had
shown a loss of about £7OO. Something was also said
about the profits being eaten up on account of four tons
having to be extracted for every ton milled. It was
admitted that neither the Board nor the manager was as
hopeful as they were six months ago. In September last
The Bulletin said that the directors ought to put
sophistry aside, and admit that the mine was in Queer-
street, with the end of profitable mining- round the first
corner. From the point of view of the prophet who likes
to see his prophecies come true now and then, this recent
confession is satisfactory enough ; but it ou-ht to have
come earlier.

S°m e thing had to give at Block 10. As at all
the other Broken Hill mines, the low price of lead was
pinching them, and if there was a ton of good ore showing
anywhere in the mine, it simply had to come out; and
that sort of mining so often leads to trouble. The pre-
sent movement really doesn’t amount to much, however ;
a ml* 6 stepping into a boghole on the march onward.
a little tune and money are wasted in getting out, and
when the co. gets out it knows that it will have to step
into a few more similar bogholes before it reaches the end
or its journey. A bad hanging-wall was the original
cause, and the movement will go on and'on till the shift-
ing mass finds rest at last somewhere deep down on the
Central mine’s (Sulphide Corporation’s) footwall. The
ground in the Central mine is continually moving, but
at quite a ladylike pace, and the mine oflicials are able to
effect alterations and repairs accordingly. Block 10 can’t
do that. There the ground comes down with a bump
every time, and the bump is getting nearer to the Central
boundary every time. The key of Block 10’s trouble
18 around Campbell’s shaft, where a wonderfully rich
shoot of friable ore beckoned to every manager that
Block 10 has had, and landed him in trouble when he
answered the call.
* * * *
Those who look at the mill results, and then
bMst a b°ut the profit B. H. Junction North is making
should remember that working costs are still about 20s. a
ton, and that last week’s output (265 tons concentrates)
wasnt worth more than about £3200 to the mine.
Deduct expenditure—£l74o for working costs, £1126
realisation charges, and £3OO for management and other
expenses—and only £34 remains. And this is allowing
for payment for the 85590 z. of silver contained in the con-
centrates at 2d. more than the market quotation, and for
lead at £l3 10s. The North mine is doing better than
that.
* * * *
The Broken Hill Junction intends to sink to
400 ft. in King shaft, put out a “feeler” northward on the ore
body, and if the body is promising in the matter of width
the co. will then sink towards the 600 ft. There is really
nothing else for the directors to do. If the lode is pitch-
ing north from King shaft, it is more than probable that
the ore followed south in the drive from Browne shaft,
and lost on the British boundary, is the eastern underlie
of the ore that will be found at the 400 ft. of King’s. A
feature of the eastern ore bodies is that they have an
eastern underlie, while the larger western bodies underlie
west. But so far as is known at present, the King shaft
ore body is going down nearly vertical.
* • • «
Tingha Consolidated (N.S.W.) has a name that
tells its own tale ; likewise, a balance-sheet. Half-a-
dozen little shows rolled into one, and put under the CBgis
of Coleman, Hermann, and other children of Israel. Five
dredges for six months won tin worth £11,568. The co.
ended up the half-year with £933 to credit of profit and
loss, whereas it began with £5457, and it owes £4374 to
sundry creditors—doubtless, on account of new dredge.
It seems that meantime £5OOO was paid in divs. at the rate
of 10 per cent, on the preference shares. If, with tin at
high-water mark, only the prefs. can receive divs., then
with tin at normal prices ordinaries cannot look for
even crumbs. To load up a tin-dredging show with
400,000 shares is cruelty. But the Lyell Blocks crowd,
which handles this mine in London, likes heaps of shares.
Innocent Melbourne shareholders want a local quotation,
but are put off with the story that to list the stock will
be to kill the English market. Which is quite likely.
* * * ■*
This in brief is the agreement between the
Queensland Government and the syndicate in connection
with the Mt, Eiliott-Hampden railway. Survey, con-
struction, and working are to be under Government
control. Of the line itself the Government is to own a
half-share from the start. The line is estimated to cost
£200,000 ; the syndicate provides £lOO,OOO. In 15 years
the Government may buy this half-share for 28 4-7th
times the average annual net revenue for the last five
years of the syndicate’s ownership. If the line returns
3£ per cent., it will be £3500 on the half-year share ; that
multiplied by 28 4-7th is £lOO,OOO, so on a 3£ per cent,
basis the syndicate will get its own money back. And
this is the minimum payment. If, on the other hand,
the line returns 6 per cent, on its cost, or £6OOO on the
syndicate’s share, this multiplied by 28 4-7tli will mean
£171,828, which will give the syndicate a fine profit.
Cheap railway lines now and then make very large profits
on their cost ; the Silverton line, for instance. Under
this agreement the State will either pay heavily to redeem
the line, or pay cost price for a depreciated railway with
possibly a worked-out mine at the back of it. The
syndicate seems to have the thick end of the deal whether
the line pays or fails.
* * * «
Stannary Hills (N.Q.) are being given another
share-jig, mostly in Adelaide. But, in spite of fair
returns at the moment, the outlook is poor. They are
getting pretty well half their ore, and pretty well all
their tin, from the underhand stope below No. 6 level of
the Ivanhoe mine. As the No. 6is the bottom level, this
means stoping below the shaft-level; and that seems to
point to the upper workings being “ done ” or, anyhow,
too poor. The co. gets the balance of the ore from the
Kitchener and Extended mines, and it would be interest-
yhafc this stufl tfoes b .y itself: it is very
Sii n*i^ er lt 1S , even Payable. The Black Rock and
entralHUl big tunnel is developing nothing, and looks
more than ever like a blind stab. Nothing of any conse-
quence was ever got above this tunnel-level, and the hope
or getting anything in the tunnel itself is fading fast.

r * * • *
The Klondyke field, out past Mungana (O.).
promised well in boom times, but is now as dead as a
stewed missionary. The Irvinebank Mining Co. has a
number of shows out there, and a lot of good smelting
ore was formerly sent to the Chillagoe smelter—high-
SrMewppcr sulphides and carbonates, with gold as high
as 30dwt. per ton. What is left of this ore will no doubt
be sent to the Molloy smelters in future. The Lamington,
one of Moffat s mines at Klondyke, has finally shut down,
and the winding plant has been transferred to one of his
tin shows at Koorboora. At Koorboora most of the
smaller properties are under exemption ; in fact, all of the
copper shows. Their only hope is a sufficient increase in
the price of the metal to enable them to get the ore as
far as Mount Molloy. About seven miles away from
Koorboora, Torpy’s Crooked Creek silver-lead mines are
doing good work, and ore is being stoped out at the 220 ft.
,*;*\ with the concentrates on hand, has been
sold to southern buyers. The lead furnace at Mount
Molloy is not likely to be ready for work for a couple of
months, but after that most of the lead ore in the district
will go that way.
*** ' *
The miners at the Anakie sapphire fields (Q.)
are wanting assistance. Last year this field produced
stones to the value of £50,000, and at one time there were
as many as 1200 men there. The difficulty has been their
inability to get fair values for the output; some half-
dozen buyers have them at their mercy. They are now
asking the Government to appoint a gem expert who will
grade the stones for them. If this were done, they do
not anticipate any difficulty in obtaining advances on the
value, pending sales in London, where they suggest the
establishment of an exchange, run on the lines of that for
wool sales.

with John Cock leading a little at the Valley.

Catherine Reef, Bendigo, which was so pelted by
the local Beehive crowd when all Australia was buying,
is having another jig, with the local people in the field.
They will have a hard task to allure the burnt child again
to the fire of speculation. Said burnt child knows by ex-
perience that the length of the shoot of gold isn’t sufficient
to warrant the present price of shares ; so the outsider’s
impulse will be to sell when the market moves. What
has happened is that the shoot has been picked up on its
pitch north at 2300 ft. Present crushings don’t show
much in the way of profits, 436 loads giving 97£oz.

Biff! Biff! That is what happened at the
Cathcart (Vic.) fight. Ten rounds in 10 minutes. 8., M.
and, Co. took hits with a smile. Dodds, “the Ararat
pet,” was in “ the pink of condition,” while “the firm”
stripped somewhat light through having taken off weight
by discarding the contributing scrip. The “mill” was for
the managership of the co.,which Bewick, Moreing and Co.
got when they agreed to finance this special Victorian deep-
lead show. They struck gold at first, then lost it, then
lost their drive, and then threw up their big block of
contributing shares, thus leaving things as bare as the
middle of the Sahara. Ararat then took charge, got the
co. on to gold, and now asks 8., M. and Co. to forego
their agreed percentage of the profits. 8., M. and Co.
don’t see it. Their St. Nicholas smiled at the temptation,
but his resolution to hold on was inflexible ; so 8., M.
and Co. sit tight on their percentage, while Dodds yaps,
“ Come on, and I’ll hit you again."

Birthday New Tunnel, Berringa (Vic.), being
in a crushed and deplorable state, even though it has the
aid of that man of patience and industry called Job, has
brought in geologist Moon to tell what is beyond the pick.
Moon has no diffidence about doing that. A slide has
crushed the reef, and the reef w'as solid and well
mineralised above the slide ; ergo below the slide it will
be found much the same. Exactly ; but as there wasn’t
much gold up there, is that same condition to prevail
down below ?

Alfred Clarke still clings as loyally to the dead-
beat Colonel North, Zeehan (Tas.), as he does to the
White Leads at Broken Hill. Zeehan badly needs some-
thing to stir it. And it would have it if all the
portly black letters in the manager’s report meant
anything more than printer’s ink. These are a
few of the sentences so honored: “The next half-
year being a productive one”; “a fair amount
of stoping ground available”; “from 6 inches to
over 3ft.” ; “ 9 inches of clean ore ” ; “going into maiden
ground.” Then “main lode-channel and “a most im-
portant point.” But it is a bit of a come-down to light
on the following sentences in ordinary type—“ I propose
letting all work possible on contract,” and “ Every place
being in such a state of sludge, it has not been possible to
single out all the most productive spots ; but you can
rest assured that no stone will be left unturned in trying
to produce as much ore as possible.” What an anti-
climax !
* * * *
H. Y. L. Brown and chief-assistant Gee have
produced another and better edition of the “ Record of
the Mines of S. Australia.” Though it hasn’t had much
gold to help it, the State’s mineral output from 1840 to
1907 is set down at £27,308,351. Teetulpa, in 1887, is
practically the only good gold rush S. Aus. has had ;
but it was a mere patch in the north-eastern
mulga, and nearly all the £300,000 worth came from
a square mile. Copper, of course, easily leads with
£18,743,481. Old hands talk fondly of “the palmy days
of the Burra and Moonta”;yet, as a matter of record,
no previous year touched the output for 1906—£718,609.
The nearest approach was the £680,714 of 1872.
“ Geology ’’ Brown always dwells with a little pride on
the State’s iron deposits. At Iron Knob, 40 miles back
from Port Augusta, “ a vast quantity, estimated at
21,000,000 tons, of high-grade (66 per cent.) iron ore
and manganic iron, is in sight.” The Broken Hill
Prop, got hold of this deposit many years ago, built
a railway and a jetty, and now draws its iron flux supply
from there—and gives a thought now and again to the
possibilities of iron and steel smelting. Unfortunately
the nearest coal supply is far round on the N. S. Wales
coast.

“ M.C.” : Three of our copper mines, Mt.
Lyell, Gt. Cobar, and Lloyd’s, smelt ore of about the
same value. But the results are very different. Values
herein are based on copper at £6O, fine silver at 2s. 4d.,
gold at £4. In March, Mt. Lyell, with the third largest
output of ore among Australasian mines, treated 32,232
tons for 667 tons copper, 17340 z. gold and 51,9580 z. silver.
This pans out 2.06 p.c. copper, ldwt. gold and 32dwt.
silver. Total value is about 325. Gt. Oobar returns
vary a good deal. In March the average extraction
from ore was slightly over ldwt. gold and 7dwt.
silver, with 2J p.c. copper. This would be worth
35 s. Lloyd’s may be considered a single-metal
mine, although there are small quantities of
silver and gold in the ore. The extraction is just on 3
per cent., worth 365. per ton. But in this case all the
value is eaten up by expenses. The first two are splendid
instances of cheap pyritic smelting ; while Lloyd’s has
silicious ore that consumes much flux and firewood, and,
to make mining expensive, the lode is frequently cut off
by faults. When Mount Morgan is on its best behavior
its copper-gold ore averages as high as Bdwt. gold and 3
per cent, copper: gross value, 68s. But Mount Morgan
returns vary a lot ; also costs are high because the ore is
very silicious, and the mine is young as a copper pro-

The more we master the forces of nature, the
more do our chances of accidents multiply, even as the
tamer’s dangers increase in proportion to the number of
wild animals which he “puts through their tricks” in
the cage. Formerly, we avoided the contact of these
forces as much as possible ; to-day, they have gained
admittance to our household. And so, notwithstanding
our more prudent and peaceable manners, it happens to us
more often than to our fathers to look pretty closely upon
death. The South British Insurance Company of 12

“ Karl ” : Behold me reaching with en-
thusiasm for the scalp so recklessly dangled before
mine eyes by “Cict” (B. 23/4/’08). He relates
minutely the animals which were exuded by a
flooded wombat-burrow, and mentions nine varie-
ties besides the rightful owners. Included among
them is the gohanna, which, in my experience, has
never been found guilty of living in wombat country
at all, nor have I yet found a bushman who has
known gohannas and wombats to exist simultane-
ously in one district. Further, I have examined
scores of wombat burrows, and have seen numbers
flooded out, and never have I been privileged to
see one lodger in an occupied burrow. The
wombat is jealous of tenants, and is so uncouth in
his manners and ungentle in his treatment of visi-
tors that they take the hint, and stand not upon
the order of their sprinting, but just sprint.
Bush boys, hunting for a wombat to drown out,
carefully examine the mouth of the burrow. If
the hoof-prints of rabbits, cats, etc., are seen
entering the tunnel, they come to the conclusion
that the occupant is the late «T. Wombat, Esq.,
and proceed elsewhere. Wherefore, I proclaim
“Cict” an unwholesome and extravagant sea
serpent and a probable claimant of the Page
Millions.
AN AWKWARD SCHOOL FOR TRUANTS.
The Department of Public Instruction has from its
very inception shown a kindly, sympathetic regard for
the bush child. Away back the travelling hawker’s van
brings to the bush-buried settlers the luxuries, and often
the necessaries of life. Now the van brings educational
sustenance as well.— Sydney Paper.
Rounding up scholars at Blackfellow Creek.
An indignant Westralian by adoption :
In a recent issue you say half-a-dozen Victorian
farmers are off “to try their luck in Westralia,
of all places.” Now, why “of all places”?
Westralia is not a gigantic sand patch, but the
proud possessor of some of the best land in this
continent. Have been here three years, and seen
country as good as any in the Eastern States,
also plenty of it. Am N.S.W. born, and know
my native State from Riverina to New England,
but find Westralia more inviting, and have
selected here. For the future please drop the “of
all places.”
“ Janor ” : Sorry that “ Gip ” had such
beastly luck on her bee-hunt (B. 2/4/’08). I advise
“ Gip ” to be guided on her next honey-quest by a
smarter bloke than George. It was all his fault.
Chopping with a “thick” axe! Flouring bees
and tracking them through the bush ! Tush !
Infallible signs of balminess—or else pure guff !
It is quite impossible to track the flight of bees in
bush country, and no bushman would be ass
enough to try it; he knows he would miss the
busy little creatures even if they were “ floured ”
with beer. The locating of bee-trees is quite
simple, entails little exertion, and can be done
without falling once. George was a boshter at
falls. As a matter of fact, bee-trees are mostly
found by the bush kiddies. All that is necessary
is to watch bees leaving water until one is abso-
lutely sure of their line of flight; then follow in
the same direction.. When close to the tree the
scent of honey does the rest. That is, if the day
is sunny ; and no one but a “George” would go
hunting “ sugar-bags ” on a cloudy day. How-
ever, if the searcher is too thick in the nose to
work by scent, let him keep a sharp lookout at
likely trees for the exit and entrance of the honey-
spinners. They hive notin saplings nor in young,
solid trees.

“ Nymsie ” : Of all the quaint denizens of
our inland rivers and lagoons the fresh water
mussel is the most interesting cuss. His enemies
are many, and his ways are consequently mys-
terious. The dusky tribes of old considered roast
mussel a prime delicacy, and to the fish, the
water-rat, the turtle, the crayfish and platypus
mussel is a staple diet. Which is rough on the
mussel. No other living creature of his order has
such keen sensibility of danger. Whether it is
acuteness of hearing, vision, or touch is not clear,
but watch the mussel with its armor open for a
breather, or feeding, or preparatory to moving to
fresh quarters. At the slightest noise the shells
are closed impregnably. Maybe a shadow
proves the warning or the sound-waves jar
some sensitive centre, but anyhow, the
mussel knows when anything approaches.
When moving, the bivalve rests on its open shell-
sides, and a movement of the body propels it in a
crawling fashion on the bed of the river or lagoon.
How the creature burrows into the mud when
seeking shelter or preparatory,to hibernating is
not apparent, but it is seemingly in the same way
as it does the trailing movement. Mussels survive
the severest droughts, buried feet below the sun-
baked surface of clay where a lagoon once stood
ere evaporation and soakage depleted the water
supply. Have kept mussels for fish bait. At first
they are very, very shy, closing their shells
at the approach of a human being. After
a while they are less sensitive, especially
if fed regularly, and they like and grow
fat upon a sparse sprinkling of oatmeal. In
their native element mussels live on insect larvae,
frog eggs and such like. They generally move at
dusk, and in the morning nature observers wonder
why freshly opened mussel shells are to be found
on logs and on river and lagoon banks without a
chip on them. The query, “What opens the
mussels?” has been put regularly, and mostly it
has gone unanswered. Yet the explanation is
an exceedingly simple one. Have watched the
wary, silent bittern stand for hours eyeing a closed
mussel in the water with the fatalistic patience of
a Mussulman. He stands like a feathered statue,
knowing that sooner or later, generally later, the
mussel will, when all is still, open his citadel to
feed, or to make a move. Till then the cunning
bird waits, and even his mate’s booming round
the bend passes unheeded. When the mussel
opens his armor, as he must to live, he opens it
but to die. One dart of the bittern’s bill and, as
Henry Parkes and George Reid both used to say
in their alleged poems, “all is o’er.” The mussel’s
carcase is wrenched from its shell, and a cycle of
creation is closed prematurely and permanently.

“Poddy”: The gentle Victorian cocky
again ! In the course of duty, had to visit a farm
to brand a cow condemned for disease in the
udder. Under certain favorable conditions cocky
is allowed to fatten and sell for' beef cows thus
condemned, but he must notify
the Department and have the
beast killed under supervision,
when, if otherwise healthy,
the carcase is passed for con-
sumption. But this cocky com-
plained bitterly that the polev
was a dead loss; the local
butcher resolutely refused to
buy it, on the ground that it
might injure his business to
have the animal seen about his
premises. In a burst of sym-
pathy, I suggested that, as
his own family was un-
usually large, he should
kill the beast for family
consumption. He drew
back, a look of horror en-
crusted his whiskers, and in a shocked roar he ex-
claimed, “ Good God, I wouldn’t eat the blanky
cow.” Then my sympathy evaporated, and,
branding the cow with particular care, I left.

“Agricola”: lie “E.R.” and wild dogs
(B. 23/4/’08). The half-bred wild dog has long
been the bane of pastoralists in N.S.W., and Vic-
toria. In fact, it is almost safe to say that there
are no pure-bred dingoes east of the Darling, and
they are not found anywhere in any quantity ex-
cept in the remoter parts of Queensland, S.A.,
and Westralia. It is quite rare to find a wild dog
now with any yellow markings, let alone all
yellow. Even the yellow ones are crossed, the
majority wearing a fringe at the end where the
tail ought to grow, or at most, a stump less than
Gin. long. The pure dingo is a rather small
animal, slightly larger than a fox, but some of the
half-breeds attain a great size. In the Upper
Murray district, a good many years ago, stag-
hounds were imported to slay dingoes. They
killed some, but crossed with others, and their
progeny still work havoc among the sheep there.
One gaunt giant, caught in a trap some three years
back, stood almost a yard high, and measured
sft. 2in. from the muzzle to the tip of his stumpy
tail. But the commonest type found in N.S.W.
now is a cattle-dog cross, black in color, with tan
spots over the eyes, and carrying the usual diminu-
tive tail between his legs.

“ Talking of animal freaks,” said the
gentlemanly old station hand, “we had one
down on the Upper Murray, years ago, in my
jackarooing days. It was a bull with eight legs,
and was frequently seen, but never yarded. As
it was understood Barnum was willing to give a
price equal to a Tatt.’s first prize, the whole
country side was on the look-out for an oppor-
tunity of running it in. The curious part was
that the front legs folded along to the hips, and
the hind ones overlapped them neatly and com-
fortably, resting against the shoulders. It was a
beautifully complete freak. It was nearly yarded
by Williams, the storekeeper, who went out on
the run to collect some horses, and saw the big,
black object among the cattle. He was an old
stock hand, and seized his chance. A flick of the
whip, a lurch to the right and a plunge to the left —
then the freak tore straight ahead, with Williams
in hot pursuit. He raced it through all sorts of
country, and headed it straight for the yard,
where, fortunately, all the rails were down. Into
the big yard it rushed with its tongue hanging
out, and Williams’ horse hardly able to raise a
trot. There was a newly-killed beast hanging on
the gallows in the second yard, and the smell of
the blood smote the freak on the nostrils as it
rushed at the gate. It stopped dead, and before
the overjoyed Williams realised what had hap-
pened, it turned a complete somersault on to its
extra legs, which were quite fresh and rested,
and, charging past Williams upside down, it
vanished like the Devil himself into the scrub.

One lately returned Island missionary
asserts that the only trouble occasioned so far by
the repatriation of the Kanakas is that some of
them are posing as holy men, and filling the large
gullible ears of their untravelled brethren with
tales about having taken holy orders, and being
ordained, and, in fact, barring the color, of being
“ likit big mission’ry.” And consider the cunning
of Thomas Mallicolo in his choice of a new pro-
fession. He might have posed as a doctor,
architect, or anything, but there is only one
avocation wherein incompetencecannot be detected
till after the customer is dead, so behold the
Reverend Tanna ! Plainly, there are rocks ahead
for the white-man parson. What chance will he
have against the ber-ruther who will do marriages,
with no questions asked, for two cocoanuts or
a bunch of bananas, and who, disdaining the
niceties of the profession, will be prone to round
up every sheep, and collect the shy and retiring
collection with a strenuous shark-toothed club.
Why, there won’t be tea and buns in the game
directly.

A while back (or longer) an Englishman
sent his son out from the ancestral home and
the groated mange to learn all about sheep and
things in N. S. Wales. He didn’t know what the
baronial halls of N. S. Wales were like, exactly ;
but, anyhow, so that the “boy” might do
honor to the family name, he was allowed
£450 a year—which was about twice as much
as the manager of the station was getting.
Bull’s Jackeroo, of course, didn’t do any work,
but he behaved magnificently in the township,
and owned 11 pairs of riding boots. The
old manager presently got tired, and pulled
Jackeroo aside. “ Look here,” he said, “ you’re a
decent fellow and all that, but, hang it, you don’t
do a stroke of work !” The new-ehum stared,
surprised. “Aw,” he said, “I weally didn’t
know that there was any blawsted work that
wanted doin’; but if you think it’s up to me—
aw—why, I’ll put a man on at a pound a week to
do it, don’tchaknow ! ”
“ Flinderino ” : “Vigilans” (B. 31/3/’08)
states that the cypress pine is plentiful along the
Katherine River (Northern Territory), and he
recommends that its cultivation should be one of
the future industries of the North. And 1 quite
agree with him. The cypress is not only proof
against white ants, but it escapes the ravages of
that destructive sea-worm, the cobra. Very few
timbers are immune from the cobra, and wooden
boats and wharf piles, when once attacked by the
cobra, are rendered useless in a few months. Six
months ago the Cairns (Q.) Harbor Board built a
large punt, using hickory in the construction. An
examination the other day showed the bottom to
be honeycombed through and through, and as
brittle as a biscuit. A craft built of cypress pine
some years previous is just as solid now as when
it was put together. Red gum and ti-tree are not
affected by cobra to any great extent, but cypress
can always be relied on.

THE ENGLISH SPRING-TIDE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
London, April 25. —A great snowstorm swept over
England on Saturday. Several inches of snow fell in the
southern counties. Great damage was done to the fruit
trees. Communication was stopped, and the telegraph
service dislocated. — Reuter, %6lltfoB.
What joy to be in England, now that April’s there !
The month when blooms come out like mad !
And young oak-leaves shine forth ! The sad
Old cypress, even, seemeth glad
In his new clothes, I trow. There had
Been naught in botany that was not gay and fair.
And then the gladsome fowl-life of the English spring !
I wot the pretty birdies trill
On each green bough ; and ev’ry bill
Of helpless fledgling haste to fill
With worms ta’en by some sun-warmed rill.
And blissfulness broods—simply broods —o’er ev’rything.
No whit less merry is the lot of lowing kine.
These browse about the verdant lea,
Chewing that grass, which all agree
More sweet and succulent to be
Than any foreign milkers see.
And the effect on them is as of new, strong wine.
But gayer in the English spring than all the rest
The sturdy British farm hand is :
He who (as poets know) does biz.
About this time in air like fizz,
And fields resembling the Elys-
lan ones, where dwell the lucky legions of the blest.
Yet, to speak sooth, one trifling drawback lies ’gainst
these
Stock British spring-tide joys. ’Tis this :
April’s a month when winter’s kiss
Strikes the Old Land like snakes which hiss,
Or fierce Malay’s death-dealing kris.
The flowerets mostly do not bloom ;
The chilled fowl wear an air of gloom ;
The kine quake ’mid the mountain broom ;
O’er farm-hands Arctic horrors loom.
In brief, in April all are blanky lucky not to freeze !
N.S.W.
10-2-4.

“ Hamer ” : One meets one’s old jokes in
all sorts of places. In “ The South-Bound Car,”
by Owen Llewellyn and L. Raven Hill, a train is
held up for an hour by a cow on a Spanish line ;
and after the animal has been driven off with
coal and blasphemy, the train travels for balf-an-
hour, when a cow again delays it. “There jjiggjg.
to be a lot of cows on this line,” remarks-a
traveller. “No,” replies the guard, “same
cow.” I happened to meet that same cow on the
unfenced Tamworth-Manilla (N.S.W.) line, and’
told The Bulletin about it, and an old and con-
sistent reader (one of the sort that remembers
more about the paper than the editor) accused me
of rooting the obnoxious beast out of The Bul-
letin’s past life. Of course cows may obstruct
trains in all parts of the world ; it is even possible
that iNoah was obstructed by drowning cows ;
but the S. M. Herald, in reviewing the book,
devoted nearly all its space to this anecdote, as if
it were the newest of new tales.

“Jimmie Pannikin,” with some violence:—
“ Kola ” (B. 23/4/’08) may now get up off his knees and
try to stand erect, as all Australians should. I am sorry
he wears a very deferential countenance, though per-
haps it suits him. It is better to be 2J years behind the
times than to be afraid, and deferential, and obsequious,
and grovelling, and painfully polite. As a matter of fact,
I contributed that par re the geese sleeping in the water
to avoid foxes over two years ago to The Bulletin —so
that, after all, lam not so belated. During my travels in
the Yass-Cooma-Barren Jack district of N.S.W., I have
never seen lake or pond nests that had been plundered by
foxes ; nor have I heard of any, even from the oldest and
most unreliable inhabitant. The local folk assert that the
geese beat the fox every time by sleeping on the water.
Of course, the Victorian foxes may be rendered so
desperate by scarcity of food that they will tackle
nocturnal swimming in order to grab the geese that are
“ Asleep on the Deep.” If “ Kola ” really made his dis-
coveries “just three years ago,” he, too, is a bit belated.
Evidently I roused “Kola” from his Rip Van Winkle
sleep in the Bush. Rise up, “ Kola,” and carry yourself
like a man. I hate the wriggling, reptilian attitude.
Stand up straight l .

“ Brolga Bill”: Mention of the wicked
way in which the Shearers’ Accommodation Act
is misadministered on some of the big Western
N.S.W. runs, reminds me that it is up to some
loyal Labor member to introduce a supplement-
ary Bill for the purpose of securing decent and
healthy sleeping accommodation for all Out-
back employees. During my divagations
through the Sunset-country stations, I fre-
quently found that the shearers and co. were
decently housed, because of the S.A.A., while
the regular station hands had to be
content with small, badly-ventil-
ated sleeping-rooms-—some little
better than kennels. In my
rambles round Sydney and
suburbs, I have seen some
shockingly inadequate and
dangerously unsuitable ser-
vants’ rooms. The shearers
do get lots of fresh air in their
journeyings through the open
spaces ; but the poor devils of
domestic servants and regular em-
ployees Inback and Outback do not
fare so well —hence is wanted a God’s Glorious
Oxygen Supply Act for them as well as for
shearers.
* * ■* *
Dear B.—You are partly wrong in saying
(8.26/3/’08) that paspalum can be classed as a
failure. As a matter of fact, when the grass is
properly attended to, it makes a first-rate fodder ;
but, - when neglected, it becomes a pest, and a
nuisance, and a greater curse than a cantankerous
State Premier. To make a success of paspalum,
the principal thing is to feed it down from the
time it commences to grow. If allowed to reach
any height, it becomes rank and coarse. I once
knew a cocky named Montmorency, who planted
about three acres of paspalum as an experiment.
When it had reached the altitude of 18in., he
turned his sheep on to the paddock, and waited
with an exultant look for them to become as fat as
bper-casks. But no casks eventuated. Thejum-
bucks just sniffed at the paspalum, and then laid
themselves down .to await the end. The horses
were tried, with the same result ; they would have
nothing to do with the tall greenery. By the
time the cattle had refused to dine on it, the
exultant look that once lurked on cocky’s
frank, open countenance was replaced by a
look of murder and an expression of butchery,
and the word “ blanky ” occurred with
startling frequency. Finally, the paspalum
was dug up, and cocky heaved a vast
sigh of relief as it was carted off the pre-
mises. As for paspalum choking out buffalo
grass, it is even stronger than that. If planted
on the banks of a creek, it will eventually choke
out the rushes, no matter how thick they are. I
haven’t heard of it choking trees, but I believe it
will. Paspalum will choke the Devil if it gets a
fair go at him. In fact, I believe the only chance
to get rid of the Devil is to plant paspalum round
his haunts.—Yours, etc., Boronia.
“ J.G.” : Are there any cookies anywhere
in Australia with tougher hides than a lot of those
in the Rodney and Echuca shires (Vic.)? The
Waranga-Mallee irrigation channel is being made
through their land. When completed it will
increase the value of the soil manyfold. The
landowners who will benefit are not finding the
money for the irrigation works themselves —it is
being contributed by the whole of the people of
Victoria. Instead of being grateful for this public
assistance, the eockies and squatters have actually
tried to impose further on the public by claiming
outrageous amounts for the land that is required
for the main channel. These sharks were furious
when the Government valuers wouldn’t give them
the exorbitant price asked for their dirt. Now
they have gone a step further, and petitioned the
Minister of Water Supply, asking for compensa-
tion, on the ground that their land will have to be
divided by smaller water channels so that the
water given them by the State will be able to
irrigate their crops and make them richer and
more bloated. Truly, this infinite capacity for
begging, or brigandage, or whatever it is, compels
admiration.
* * m *
They have some pleasant-dispositioned
reptiles in Victoria. A snake was slain in Kil-
more, the other day—in a bar—that had been
making acquaintances for a week previously.
Apparently it had never been guilty of anything
except of being a snake. It had been seen play-
ing with a baby, and the baby suffered no harm
beyond grief at the loss of its plaything. A tiger-
snake, too.
* * * *
“ K. 0.5.” from the Sugar Land : Struck
one of the "‘old hands,” the other day, while
journeying into Sugaropolis. “Yes,” he said, in
answer to a question to draw him out, “I was
here long before there were any central mills, and
even before there were any steam mills. The first
one I worked in was a wooden mill—all wood,
building, rollers, and everything, and the bloom-
ing thing was worked by bullocks, just like a chaff-
cutter is worked by an old horse. Of course, it
was slow, and it took a terrible lot of cane to make
a ton of sugar, but sugar was a fancy price in those
days, and it paid well. Then I worked in some
of the later mills owned by the planters. The
planters couldn’t stand the farmers. Knew a chap
who went in for farming in those days, and got a
ringing good crop, but he couldn’t get his cane
crushed. He went to one of the mill-owners,
and asked him to buy the crop. ‘ Give you six
bob a ton for it,’ said the planter, ‘ and if you
don’t like that you can turn the pigs in.’ The
farmer had to take the price—there was nothing
else for it; and it was that kind of thing that
forced on the central mills, where the farmers can
get a fair deal. But if we don’t look out, strikes
me the central mills will soon be as bad as the
private ones, for the big shareholders keep buying
up the shares, and if it goes much farther, when
the mills are free of the Government, there will
be nothing to stop the few from owning the mills
again as in the old days. There ought to be a law
that no man can hold more than a certain number
of shares. Have lots of shareholders with few
shares, then things would be all right.” There is
heaps of sense in what the old chap said, yet if
you called him a Socialist he would be insulted.
“NO APPEARANCE.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
’Twas somewhere North in Queensland, in old Queen
Victoria’s reign, »
Waxed rich a sugar planter by the cult of raising cane ;
And as he saw his sugar and circumference increase,
The Government gazetted him a Justice of the Peace.
Long ere his elevation to the Bench was brought about
Bob Wrumm was much addicted to the drowning of his
drought
In cycles, as the sordid cares of cultivating cane
Delivered from its drudgery his bluff bucolic brain.
In spacious seasons such as these he raged around the
town,
Unmindful of his duty magisterial to the Crown ;
Regardless of his status as a Solon of the State,
He bellowed on the highways when the starry hours were
late.
Yet Providence and people seemed to both be bn his side.
The boys in blue a berth to him gave willingly and wide.
Fast friends with him the faithful Force, his' weakness
winked they at;
Whene’er they caught him in his cups, good-naturedly
they gat.
Until unto the town, betimes, a nevv-chhni “ copper"
came, '
Regardless he of Wrumm’s respect, or VVrumm’s rebuke
or blame ;
So when he saw the beery one concerned in raising Cain,
To duty true, the constable did run him in amain.
The friends of Wrumm bewailed his fate—they got him
out on bail,
But into court next day he came, nor seemed a- scrap _ to
quail.
When not one magistrate made haste to try his brother
Wrumm,
All, all had business out of town or else in Kingdom Come.
Then Robert Wrumm, J.P., arose, and said : “ IPs up to'
me
By virtue of my oath and my official dignity,
To sit upon the judgment seat, and to adjudicate '
And so he mounted to the Bench and seated him in state.
“ The court is sitting, call the case,” his Worship coldly-
said
Unto that new-chum constable who wished that he were
dead;
“ The matter is important ’’—and his voice boomed like a
drum.
‘‘l see that it is entered as Regina versus Wrumm.”
Three times the crier called aloud the parties to the case,
And thrice but echo answered him from still surrounding
space,
Whereat his Worship said, as he the charge struck from
the list:
“ No appearance of Regina, so defendant is dismissed.”
N.S.W.
CHAS. V. HYNES.
ANSWERS to CORRESPONDENTS.
No notice (except of a hostile character) will be taken of
communications insufficiently stamped. Name and
address must appear on MSS. No liability is under-
taken re contributions voluntarily submitted, whether
sent by post or handed in; and no MS. will be
returned unless stamps (of any State) accompany.
The Whistler : What, in the name of Heaven,
what does your poetry mean ? Some of your lines to
“ Death” herewith :
The soldier lame that cannot run
No use to him will be a gun
He’ll be—so—vexed
When you shall yell to him its time
He’ll try to offer prayer in rhyme
Pass on to next—
The nigger in his suit of tar
Will wonder who the deuce you are
With dreadful shakes
He’ll grab his boomerangs and spears
But you will settle all his fears
Of mulga snakes.
But —hark—here comes the man in blue
That was afraid of nought but you
He must away
To read the finger post of brass
For keeping others off the grass
On Patricks Day.
The shearer of the squatters fold
For shearing not the sheep as told
To you must go
And sing his little native song
He’y smoke Ho—Loppy come along
Our cake is dough.

Queensland Minister for Lands Bell is
taking steps to protect any trees marked by ex-
plorer Leichhardt. It was reported a while ago
that there were quite a number about Jundah
and Chinchilla ; but when search was made for
them they could not be found. Bush imagination
had created some and bush fires had probably
destroyed the others.

A novel truancy prosecution was
heard yesterday at the Coburg Court.
Sydney Shackleton was charged with
not having sent his daughter, Annie, to
school. The said Annie proved to be a
married woman, with children of her
own. In answer to the police magis-
trate, the inspector declared that the
young woman’s name was still on the
roll, and he was instructed to withdraw
the charge.
So the above painful scene will not be
enacted in Coburg echool.

A SIMPLE PRESCRIPTION.
Said to Core Rheumatism, Kidney Ttouble,
and Bladder Troubles.
We know that the readers of this paper
appreciate good advice, and we are glad, there-
fore, to be able to publish the following from a
prominent physician. v
It is astonishing to know the prevalence of
kidney trouble and other diseases, sitch as rheu-
matism, bladder and liver troubles, which are
almost invariably caused by weak kidneys. It is
said on good authority that fully one-third of the
people in Australia who have reached the age of
maturity are more or less afflicted with kidney
trouble in some. form. The most dangerous
feature of this disease is the fact that many people
have it without their knowledge, and consequently
neglect it until it has reached a dangerous stage.
The least symptom of kidney trouble, such as
backache, must be given prompt attention, and a
good reliable treatment should be taken, until
every trace of the disease has been eliminated.
We are glad to be able to give our readers a
prescription which we have seldom known to
fail in any case. It contains no harmful in-
gredients, is pleasant to take, not expensive, and
can be supplied by any good chemist. It is as
follows.
Tincture Gentian Compound, one ounce ; Syrup
Rhubarb, one ounce ; Liquid Barkola Compound,
one ounce ; Syrup of Ginger, five ounces.
Take a teaspoonful after each meal, and one at
bedtime.
We are glad to publish this prescription, as we
know it will help our afflicted readers who give it
a fair trial. If taken continuously for a few
weeks, it will be found to be an almost absolutely
certain cure for rheumatism, kidney and bladder
trouble, backache, and all allied complaints.
Note.—Other papers are privileged to copy the
above.*
PERSONAL
ITEMS
G. W. Smailes, who used to be a member
of N.S.W. Assembly, is again a full-blown parson.
He preached for some years with the Primitive
Methodists ; now he has joined the Anglicans.
Premier Kidston scorns the suggestion
that he intends to remain in the Cold Country as
Queensland’s Agent-General. “I am coming
back all right,” he shouted at Warwick as the
train moved south.
Of 11 Australian explorers who got the
Royal Geographical Society’s gold medal, only
Bigjohn Forrest is still alive. Ernest Giles, who
died a few years ago in the West, was the last to
get the trinket—it was about all that he did get.
Opposition leader Butler (S.A.) has quite
left off grieving that he was turned out of office
three years or so ago, because in the good times
since then he has founded a big and prosperous
live-stock business. He allowed himself no time
to look after his private affairs when he wasS.A.’s
most weeping Treasurer.
The name of John Pascoe Fawkner,
founder of Melbourne, was mentioned to a Vic.
Supreme Court Judge lately, when the sole sur-
viving executor of the old pioneer’s will—
Cornelius Job Ham—applied for leave to pay an
eleventh share of the estate to each of four
claimants, the sons and a grandson of Elizabeth
Lucas, Fawkner’s sister. The other seven
legatees are undiseoverable, so the State will get
the last remnants of the old chap’s property—a
bit of land at Benalla.
Rev. Pearce Carey is that rarity, a Rome-
flaring cleric whose views on Industrial Reform
are beyond reproach. At his Sunday night meet-
ings for the workers he speaks gospel truths con-
cerning Fat, thereby depleting his pews of many
wealthy worshippers. P.C. is a member of the
Anti-Sweating League, and chairman of a Wages
Board in the Yarra-side city. A horror of Rome
is, after all, a pardonable sort of pusillanimity in
a parson who weekly shows himself to be utterly
unafraid in the presence of a depleted collection-
plate.
HUME COOK, M.H.R.
“Gallery Note ” writes :
None of the new members of Q,. Assembly made any
notable hit in their first Parliamentary session just closed.
Barton, of North Brisbane, and Cottell, of Toowong, moved
and seconded the Address-in-Heply; but neither rose
much above the ruck. So far, Appel (Independent Philp-
ite) and Redwood (Kidstonite) look the most promising'
new men. Yet Barton is a scholarly and level-headed
electrician, with a solid business and a beastly big bank
balance ; and these utensils, in addition to brains, should
help him along. Cottell is a conveyancer, who put up
one of the best fights on Q. record, and bumped out the
pretentious and inflated Macartney to the consternation
of Boodle and the delight of Democracy. Cottell has grit
and some pelf, and has a commendable contempt for
humbug. Among the old men, Philp is as weak and
watery as ever, if not even more so ; skim-Philp he is
nowadays. And Joe Lesina—Joseph the balloon—still
takes seven hours to say that he intends to vote against
the Bill.
A well-known Maoriland ex-journalist died
recently in London. Thirty years ago Charles
Rous-Marten got a foothold on the literary staff
of the Evening Post, and climbed up the tortuous
pole to the editorship before he let go in 1883.
Later, he edited the M. L. Times. About 20
years ago he went to London, where he acted as
correspondent to the Post and other papers.
“Caber”: Way back, 42 years ago, the
Bank of Queensland went smash, and its
notes were rushed here, there and every-
where by their unlucky owners, to be
sniffed at with scorn. Summed up
they appeared about as worthless
as George Reid’s political opinions.
But just when people were won-
dering whether the notes ought
not to be burned by the hangman,
a benevolent shopkeeper started
negotiating the paper of evil repu-
tation. He gave goods to the
extent of 6s. Bd. on every “ red-
back ” presented ; and while a note
remained in that township, he did
a great trade. The bank eventually
paid 20s. in the £, and the store-
keeper passed in his pile of paper,
and waxed fat, and helped all his
relations. Why am I writing thus?
Because had that bank not gone
bung, the par (B. 9/4/’08) re Kidston
and Baines would never have been
written. “Wullie” might have been
Premier of Queensland, but it is pretty
certain that “ Walter ” wouldn’t have
been where he is to ladle out childish insults.
Henry Alfred Lenehan, N.S.W. Govern-
ment Astronomer, who died on Saturday, was
born, August. 1843, in Castlereagh-street, Sydney,
where his father carried on business as a
cabinet-maker and undertaker. Later on his
father’s private residence was “ Ranelagh,” a fine
house standing on the Darling Point-road. Young
Lenehan was educated at Lyndhurst College,
Glebe, and entered the Observatory on August 9,
1870. He succeeded H. C. Russell some years
ago, but for a long time he was merely “acting.”
As a full-blown astronomer he only held office for
about four months.
Sydney’s venerable sky-pilot, Archdeacon
Gunther, had an anniversary on May I—on that
date he completed 40 years’ continuous service at
St. John’s, Parramatta. When the birthday ap-
proached a deputation of parishioners waited on
Mr. Gunther to ask him if he had a mouth on
him, and invited him to give it a name. The
ancient clergyman considered the various drinks,
and decided that the brand he preferred was tiles
—he wanted a new tiled floor for his old church.
So the money—about £loo—was promptly put
up, and the floor was duly handed over on
May 1.
Rome has a lavish relative in Thomas
Loughlin, a young Ballarat capitalist. He has
just spent £30,000 building a church in Kilkenny
(Ireland), which Archbishop Carr will consecrate
when he reaches the land of good grievances and
bad landlords. Quite a lot of Australian monev
has gone to cold climes, owing to Rome’s machina-
tions, by the way. Mrs. Dalgleish (she was a
de Lauret, of Gonlburn, N.S. W.), who afterwards
married one Bellas is, member of a well-known
R.C. family hi England, paid for the completion
of the Dome of the Oratory, London’s second
largest church. It cost the lady in the neighbor-
hood of £50,000. A lucky speculation in Broken
Hill supplied the money.
The sculler Laycock family goes as near to
the beginning of things in Australia as most
families. One of its members found the overland
track from Launceston to Hobart (Tasmania).
The first settler at South Head, Svdney, was
Thomas Laycock. An entry (12/2/1793) shows
that Lieutenant-Governor Grose, who ran the
show in 1792-1794, granted him 80 acres on the
south side of Sydney harbor at Parsley Bay. More
grants were made to him later on. This Thomas
Laycock was a useful person in a number of
capacities, lay and military, and had the knack
of handing to his superiors butter on a lordly
dish. Especially he handed much butter on an acre
of dish to Grose. He started a residential area at
Kurnell, and thus linked his memory with
Captain Cook’s landing place. It wasn’t Kurnell
when Cook was there, but a man named Connell
married a Laycock and resided there, and mis-
pronunciation did the rest.
Queensland Railway Commissioner Thallon
is leaving on a trip to Europe and America.
Thallon has risen from the ranks in the Australian
railway service, and is a living object lesson on
the superiority of local over foreign training.
Although the Queensland Commissioner leirnt
the lumping and lamp-lighting branches of his
profession in Scotland, he developed all his
administrative capacity and acquired all his
higher training in Australia. And the result is
that, where his imported and more highly-salaried
predecessors failed dismally, he has been a monu-
mental success. The Queensland railways now
pay interest on all borrowed capital; and all the
big railway smashes take place inStates where
oinamental importations are in charge. Banana-
land owes Thallon a holiday—he hasn’t had a
decent one for 22 years.
“ D.,” on the Abruzzi engagement :
The recently-announced engagement,—now confirmed
m Home, according to London Daily Chronicle (22/3/’08)
—of the Duke of Abruzzi and Miss Katherine Elkins has
points of interest. Though the lady owns a millionaire
father, she will not be required to come down with a set-
Llenient on her ducal hub. He is a millionaire in his own
right already, and, further, has expectations from his
rich Belgian grandmother. This alone singles Miss
Elkins . case out from that of every other Ameri-
can girl who has snared a duke. Again, there
is no such thing as a “morganatic marriage ”
in the dictionary of the House of Savoy. A male
member may marry his cook or anv other humble
person, and she forthwith automatically' takes rank with
him. Accordingly the maiden from Hamland will jump
from the status of plain miss to that of royal princess.
She will be the first U.S.A-born royal princess in history.
Her offspring, if they arrive, will be of royal rank, and
will be in line of succession to the throne of Italy. Given
a really comprehensive bit of bomb-hurling, or a railway
smash on the Tait scale, and the unique spectacle may be
world of a Yankee politician (Elkins, senr., is a
™r- e . with a European king as his grandson.
Miss Elkins is capturing one who looms big in the world
apart from his rank. Her Duke is a capable naval officer,
mJITSV Qmous mountain-climber (he scaled
Mount St. Elias) the holder for long of the Farthest
North record in Arctic penetration, a skilled linguist a
prolific writer, and an expert in half-a-dozen sports All
round, he is one of the most astounding dukes, royal or
otherwise, now extant. y

THE YELLOW PERIL.—A TALE OF THE
EASTER MANOEUVRES.
“ Baro ” sends a few details :
Commonwealth Meteorologist Henry Ambrose Hunt,
although the King Hilly of the show, is third youngest of
Australia’s meteorological team. Henry was ushered
into this cold world on February 7,186 b, whereas First
Assistant Robert Fletcher Griffiths was sent off the mark
on April 30, 1857 ; Second Assistant Henry Maurice Josce-
l.vne on November 28, 1859 ; Third Assistant Andrew
Noble on July 28, 1868 ; Fourth Assistant Edwin Thomas
Quayle on November 11, 1862 ; and Fifth Assistant Edgar
Lambert Fowles on March 4, 1872. Hunt is a quiet, taci-
turn man of great ability, with a keen inner "know-
ledge of the possibilities of the political whirl-
pool, and a gift for successful speculation. “ Griff,”
who will boss our weather while Henry is careering
around the globe on his world tour, gained his knowledge
of meteorology at the Adelaide Observatory under Charles
Todd, where Cooke, of Westralia, was also officially dragged
up. Joscelyne, familiarly known as “Joss,” is a smart
meteorologist, and a hustler. He formerly aided and
abetted Cooke at Perth, and one time, before being seized
with the forecasting habit, was master of a grammar
school near Sydney. Andy Noble, who since the end of
March has been in Sydney on sick leave, owing to
insomnia and nerve trouble, has (meteorologically)
always been connected with his present chief, being' his
confederate at the Sydney Observatory. Quayle is, in
appearance, the “dead ring” of Farmer Hayseed from
Wayback, but except for a love of farming, the resem-
blance goes no further. Quayle hails from Pete Bar-
acchi’s establishment in the Yarra Domain, and is
perhaps more of an astronomer than a meteorologist.
The baby of the team, Fowles, was a disciple of the
whirling and restless Clement Wragge. Fowles was
chief weather clerk in Brisbane until Hunt happened
along and gave him a well-deserved lift. The whole team
works well together, and threatens to make things
meteorological hum in Australia.
Andrew Henry Barlow, M.L.C., who is
Acting-Premier of Queensland in Kidstoii’s
absence, is an old man (his years now number 71)
who has developed. Like William Lyne, he grows
more democrat c as his white hairs accumulate.
He has been in six Ministries, and when one con-
siders the chiefs he has served, the development
of Barlow becomes evident. He was Minister for
Lands under Mcllwraith and Nelson in 1893. He
held the same portfolio under Nelson in 1893-’96.
The next Premier (Byrnes) was then the rising
hope of Australian democracy, and Barlow took
service under his banner. He repudiated Pliilp,
but when, after an interval of five years, Morgan
came to the front, Andrew Henry Barlow, grow-
ing distinctly old but with much life still in him,
was Morgan’s Minister for Education. And he
has been second in command in the two Kidston
Cabinets. A big, heavy old man is Barlow, with
an inscrutable countenance, like a dead wall when
he likes to make it so, and a habit of dropping
epigrams that you could shave yourself with.
An old pressman writes :
Harry Foran, Yarra Bank orator, who heard the Divi-
sion Bell ring at St. Vincent’s Hospital, Melbourne, a few
days ago, was a remarkable specimen of his class. He
was a native of Ireland, and almost lifelong residence in
Australia failed to obliterate the magnificence of his
brogue, or the gorgeousness of his blarney. He was for a
while in the Victorian Education Department, but he
proved a man with grievances, and he aired them so
loudly and persistently that he became too eloquent for
the Public Service. So he retired, to take up his residence
in Sydney, where he blossomed out as a full-fledged spell-
binder of the Domain. Foran contested one or two bur-
lesque elections in N.S.W., one of them against George
Reid. Then, after some years, he returned to Victoria, and
continued to voice rebellion on the Yarra bank almost up
to the time of his death. The extravagance <*f Foran’s
public utterances and the flamboyance of his diction led
people to overlook his marvellous oratorical ability. He
was probably the most fluent public speaker in Australia
—not even excepting Deakin —and he was free from the
involved sentences that mar the Prime Minister’s plat-
form work. Foran was never at a loss for a phrase, let
alone a word. His matter was the most shocking fustian ;
but, as a claptrap orator, he stood supreme. Foran
followed no political party. Freetraders, Protectionists,
Tories and Laborites he denounced with impartial vigor,
attributing the most awful motives to the leaders of
every shade of political belief, and including in
his comprehensive charges of personal corruption
administrative and judicial heads in every department.
The writer once heard Foran create a tremendous im-
pression on a crowd of Yarra bankers by ostentatiously
pulling from his breast pocket a bundle of papers and
tapping them significantly as he remarked “ D’ye know
phwhat Oi have here? Oi have here the documenthary
ivvidince thot Chief Justice Blank an’ Premier Dash were
squared be the sum av £4 16s. 4d. to'shuppress th’facts
in th’ ca-ase av Blinks vershus th’ Society fur the Supres-
sion av Rivirince. An’”—his hand drew forth another
packet—“here are th’ shtatutory declarations,, saled,
shworu an’ delivered, to prove thot th’ Shecretary av
Poshtholes wuz bribed be Asterisk and O ). to permit
th’ paple av Victoria to be shwindled out av no less than
£480,000.” Foran spoke with such deliberation and
with such a convincing air that his assertions were swal-
lowed as gospel by a number of those who attended his
meetings. Though he always announced himself as an
advanced “ dimmycrat,” the Labor Party was the mark
of his most bitter attacks. Foran’s claim was that Reid,
Deakin, and Watson were really parties to a huge con-
spiracy to rob the people of Australia, and that, after
they had played out a clever comedy by attacking one
another on the floor of the House, they were in the habit
of meeting in the Ministerial room and cutting up the
loot. In fact, Foran led his hearers to understand that
he had been present on several such occasions, concealed
behind an arras, or something of the sort. In private
life, those who knew Foran asserted that he was a m'ost
genial and benevolent man.

“K. ”: Redmond Barry, the Solicitor-General
for Ireland, recalls Redmond Barry, of Victoria of
the olden tune. He was from Cork, and came to
Australia 70 years ago. He was admitted to the
Bar at Sydney, which then possessed the only
Supreme Court there was in Australia. Settling
at Melbourne, he became many things from Corn
missioner of the Small Debts Court to .Justice of
the Supreme Court and Acting Governor. In the
early ’forties Redmond Barrv and ex-Justice
Williams did all the legal practice in Melbourne
and weren’t unduly rushed even then.
The tale of a versatile man :
According to his own account, supplied to several iour-
nals, Joseph Tuekwell, who is now, at the ao-e of ss „
chorister at St. Paul’s Church, Canterbury (Melb ) has
crowded much variety into bis life. He was born at
Brighton (Eng.) in 1823. At the age of nine he became a
chorister at St. George’s Chapel, Windsor. Six years later
he was apprenticed to the sea for five years. Two years
of it, however, filled him up ; so he deserted, and got a
job as cashier in a draper’s shop at Windsor. Next year
he entered the Westminster Training College, and soon
after was appointed to the charge of a charitv school in
London. In 1843 he went on the stage at 13s. a week
and after getting engagements at a couple of
theatres, he went to Belgium and Holland with a
tableaux vivants show. After a short spell there he went
back to Bull’s country, and managed a tea establishment
for a while. In 1846 he tackled school-teaching a<'ain.
At his first school J. M. Pratt, now a member of the Vic-
torian House of Landlords, was one of his pupils. i t ,
1850 he came to Tasmania as religious instructor on board
a vessel which carried the wives and families of some con-
victs already in the Apple Island. His Australasian
career thenceforward was as follows 1851, Inspector of
Police (he doesn’t say where); 1852, miner at Fryer’s
Creek and warder at Collingwood Stockade ; 1853-7," de-
tective in Melbourne ; 1857, auctioneer at Richmond
(Melb.); ISSB-61, Melbourne detective again ■ 1861-4
chief detective officer, Otago (M.L.); 1864, gold searcher’
Dunedin (M.L.) Customs ; 1865, superintendent of prison
hulk at Port Chalmers (M.L.); 1866-8, gaol governor at
Auckland (M.L.) ; 1868, returned to Melbourne and
opened inquiry office, which he closed almost imme-
diately ; 1868-85, water-rate collector ; 1885-91, municipal
rate-collector at Williamstown (Melb.). Then he knocked
off looking after rogues and rates.
THE LATE H. A. LEX EH AN, Government
Astronomer of N.S.W.
Photo by Newman, Sydney.
The ingenious device for perpetuating the
memory of Burdett Smith, C.M.G., by depositing
a wreath bearing his name on the pedestal of the
Captain Cook statue in Hyde Bark, Sydney, was
repeated, on the explorer’s anniversary, on April
28. The sisterly affection which inspires the dis-
play is all right, but the incident is so characteris-
tic of chubby little Burdett himself that one would
almost fancy he came back to arrange the flowers
on the stone. He was a delectably happy mortal
in N.S.W. political life of over a score of years-
ago, with the manners of a courtier and the pose of
a beauty actor. He wasn’t strong on politics, but
he spoke prettily ; and his head of curled hair was-
the chief ornament of Macquarie-street.

WEAK STOMACH ; INDIGESTION ;
MISERY.
Sydney Girl’s Indigestion Cured by Dr.
Williams’ Pink Pills.
They make Strength and New Blood, and
Enable the Stomach to Digest Food
Without Causing Pain.
A weak stomach shows itself in indigestion.
Indigestion indicates a weak stomach. Every
healthy man. woman and child should be able to
digest a sufficient amount of nourishing food,
without pain or trouble. When they can’t there’s
something wrong with the stomach. There may
be pain or an uncomfortable fulness after eating;
heartburn ; belchings of wind ; headache ; tired-
ness; food doesn’t nourish, a general rundown
feeling. In extreme neglected cases ulcers form
on the coating of the stomach and the agony is
indescribable.
Make the stomach stronger. It’s reasonable to
suppose that if the stomach were stronger there
would not be these disagreeable symptoms. The
question is then, how to make the stomach
stronger —strong enough to do its work.
By their action in the blood and their tonic
properties Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills cure stomach
troubles. They’re doing it every day. They’re
the right thing for indigestion because they tone
the whole system. A. case of stomach trouble
cured by Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills: —
“ For twelve months I was ailing with indi-
gestion,” said Miss Lucy Wells, 13 Harwood
Lane, Pyrmont, Sydney. “It came on with a
heavy pain in my chest, which went right through
to my back. I thought I’d suffocate. For hours
my food seemed to lie on my chest. Sometimes
I’d be desperately hungry, yet the moment I sat
down to the table I felt as if I wanted to retch,
but I couldn’t. I lost my color and got very thin.
All my friends said how bad I looked. I had no
strength or energy. My tongue was thickly coat-
ed and I had a nasty taste in my mouth. I used
to get sick fainting turns —my head would go
dizzy, and I would have to sit down to avoid
swooning right off. At last I decided to go to the
hospital. I was there for six weeks and for half
that time I lived on crushed biscuits and milk,but.
I came out as bad as when I went in. It was
then that a friend advised me to give Dr. Williams'
Pink Pills a trial. The very first box did me a
world of good. Little by little the pains left me K
I was able to eat what I liked and 1 got back my
color and strength. I never enjoyed better health
than I do to-day.”
