POLITICAL
POINTS
RIP VAN WINKLEISM.
The colonists had achieved a grand work in building up
this part of Greater Britain, but, like true sons of the
islands “set in the silver sea,” they were still in their
deepest impulses under the spell of the vast traditions of
the home they had left. —Argus on early Australians
and the A.N.A.
“ Not all of us, Granny ; only a few. Most of
us hare outgrown that unfortunate heritage."
The remarkable news that an attempt has
been made to blow up one of the big guns at
Thursday Island should make Australia sit up
and think. Thursday Island is mainly Japanese,
and its little garrison and its handful of guns are
scant and feeble emblems of the white man’s
sovereignty thereabouts. And this tendency to
blow up one of the few emblems there are is sug-
gestive and disquieting.
“ Billy T.” : The State Premiers are at it
again, hullaballooing over the action of the Aus-
tralian Government in providing for a decent
maximum in cornsacks. They insist that the
right to so legislate belongs to them, and they
are going to fight the matter. The right was
theirs for a great many years, during which time
scores of men cultivated the spinal curvature of
the dromedary, without it troubling the states-
men worth a tinker’s profanity. These fellow's
remind me of Considine at Tw'o-Up. When Grey
dug out the big rock from under their dividing-
fence to use as a base for his swing-gate, Considine,
who had never noticed the boulder before, waxed
furious, and put in claims to a controlling in-
fluence on the stone that ended in a law-suit
costing £270. It’s a case of: “If nobody wants
the thing, anybody can have it; but if anybody
wants it I’ll see him d d first! ”
Sydney moves along in regard to municipal
lighting. For years three or four little concerns
sold light and power—sold it to consumers in
front of whose places the municipality’s big
electric-lighting mains trailed. By degrees, how-
ever, this wasteful overlapping is being blotted
out. A few months ago the Empire Co.’s concern
was bought; now the Strand Co., in which Hugh
Dixson has a finger—if it doesn’t amount to a
whole hand —is being taken over for £45,000.
N.S.W. State Premier Wade, who shows
a tendency to break out in unexpected places,
received what to another man would be a humili-
ating snub at the Premiers’ Conference. He pro-
posed that the States should take part in the Tokio
Exhibition of 1912. “Victoria won’t,” said
Swinburne. “Westralia declines to look so far
ahead,” said Wilson. “S.A. has sent word that
it won’t,” said Kirkpatrick. “Queensland
won’t,” said Kidston. So Wade’s bizarre propo-
sition, that Australia and Monkeyland should go
and weep together like brothers four years hence,
went underfoot.
One of the matters specially handed over
to the Commonwealth under Section XXVII. of
Clause 51 of the Constitution is “immigrationand
emigration.” Nevertheless, State Premier Wade,
of N.S.W., heaved himself up at the State Premiers’
conference, to propose that “ each State should be
left to control the development of its own immi-
gration policy.” This proposition was made to
the other State Premiers, who had no more right
or power to vote to themselves the Common-
wealth’s authority than a meeting of the Boggabri
football club has to decide to divide among its
members Mr. Wade’s clothes and boots. But the
monumental “front ” of Wade takes no notice of
small things like that.
John L. Taifc writes : Re the grain bag
trouble. It has been recognised by the owners of
pack-horse teams that 2001 b. is an average load
for an ordinary horse. And experienced packers
seldom exceed that weight, to any great extent,
knowing that if they overload it means the price
of a new horse in the near future. Of course it
must not be supposed that the man is of as much
importance as the horse. Still there are many
who think that he is worthy of a little considera-
tion, seeing that his strength is only calculated to be
one-twelfth that of the more important animal. In
any case it is safe to say that if those who advocate
the 3001 b. or 3401 b. bag had only to carry a 1001 b.
bag for eight hours at a stretch, they would be
pretty “full up” before their shift was over.
“ Poleaxe ” : Conscription pure and simple
existed at your front door for 30 years, and it is
only lately that “other arrangements” have
been made. Fiji has been a British Crown
colony since ’74, and in that period many
natives have been gaoled for not serving in
the armed native constabulary. This was
purely a military force, armed with rifles and
bayonets; it did no police duty whatever,
and the men served for three years at the
princely salary of 7s. a month. This conscrip-
tion was British, for the native had no voice
or choice in the matter. The pay was British
also. Usually most of the pay was forfeited
in fines and the like for petty offences, and
the ex-cannibals were court-martialled and
gaoled on a liberal scale. I have seen Fijians
sentenced to 12 months’ imprisonment with
hard labor for failing to appear for enrolment.
After a man had been obtained by conscrip-
tion, he was often converted into a gaol
warder ; but I don’t suppose you are going to
be as British as this with your Australians.
The Ma State publishes a monthly offi-
cial booklet of dry facts and figures, and the
March number casts the now familiar stone at
the ass who gibes at the unimportance of the
State’s manufactures. Mining, for instance,
boomed tremendously last year, and everybody
who owned a bit of mineral dug it out and
threw it on to the top of the market; yet,
notwithstanding all that rush and scatter, N.S.
Wales’ manufactures were worth 20 per cent,
more than N. S. Wales’ mining. And they
were worth nearly twice as much as N. S.
Wales’ agriculture; while as for the tremen
dous Cow industry, it was worth barely £1
for every £4 that the State owed to its
manufactures.
The Vic. Education Department, which
Minister Sachse says is now living the strenuous
life, and building new schools and teachers’houses,
and generally straightening things Up, is in such a
fearsome muddle that the other day it actually
summoned the father of a young married woman
of 24 for the said child’s alleged truancy. Appa-
rently the strenuous Department doesn’t bother
to inquire into particulars, but just grabs a roll—
any old roll—and summons every tenth parent or
so on the off chance.
Thus “Red Tape, Junior ” :
The Declaratory Act is now law, the Governor having
assented, and the way is clear tor N.S.W. to deal with
the Is. an acre improvement leases cancelled by the Im-
provement Lease Board. Among the cancelled are those
known as the Wingadee and Rater leases, which were not
among those granted on the bob basis, but were dealt with
by the Court simply because they happened to be gazetted
in bad company. The Wingadee leases are to be submitted
specially for Parliament’s instructions. These were
cancelled outright. The Rater leases, which now expire
in 1918 instead of 1931, will be readjusted in 1918 ; but as
the Rater people haven’t any guarantee of what the
public needs will be in 10 years’ time, they are angry and
excited. All the other leases proclaimed in this Gazette,
which was “faked” as to time, and signed by Harry
Rawson in his pyjamas, are now’ forfeited outright, and
will sooner or later be available for closer settlement.
Mariner Evans, of Tassy, at the State
Premiers’ Conference, threw the light of his great
inexperience on to the cornsack question. He
didn’t know much about wheat, he said —his State
was more interested in oats. “Say, for instance,
that a farmer has 1000 bushels, this will mean
that, instead of requiring 250 sacks at 6s. per
dozen, he will need 300 at, say, 6s. 3d.” So old
horse-marine Evans proved quite conclusively that
the farmer would lose £llls. 9d. In the same way,
supposing Mariner Evanshad 1,000,000 tonsof cargo
to shift, and supposing he decided first that he
would have to buy two 3000-ton steamers. If
afterwards he decided to buy three 2000-ton boats,
it is quite plain that he would be enormously out
of pocket, because ' if a 3000-ton boat cost
£lOO,OOO, a 2000-ton boat, being much smaller,
would naturally cost £150,000 or so. The mariner
applies the same brilliant ( reasoning to cornsacks,
and carts the argument to the State Premiers’
Conference.
For a member of the corpulent party
which advocates non-payment of members, J.
Darliri;, wheat-buyer, and formerly dictator to
a S. Aus. Government, is foolishly indiscreet.
Before the committee which has been taking
evidence on wheat combines and their methods,
he first claimed to be an expert, and then said
that on certain lines improvement was possible.
Asked if he would indicate them, he said “ he was
too busily engaged in mercenary pursuits to go into
that.” Asked further if he had no policy as a
citizen, he made this characteristic reply : “ No,
lam a merchant. In 10 years’ time I might talk
about getting patriotic.” Yet when John was
beaten by the beastly Labor party for his seat,
the whole Fat community bewailed the event as a
national calamity.
Broken Hill has a few unemployed on
hand, and it wants the State to do something for
them; whereupon “ Anti-Caucus ” writes to the
papers:—
In spite, however, of the patent fact that the tariff is
responsible in the main for the distress at Broken Hill in
the ranks of the workers, we find Messrs. Cann and
Griffith, Labor M’s.L.A., loudly denouncing: the State
Government for its “callousness ” in not finding: employ-
ment for an army of men whose troubles have been
forced upon them by another brigade of the Labor party,
without whose support Mr. Deakin’s Government would
never have been able to bludgeon the tariff through.
Yet one small fact does seem worth mentioning—
that Broken Hill lives on lead and silver mining,
and that last year lead sold in Europe at £2l per
ton and silver at 2s. 9d. per oz., whereas lead is
only worth £l3 now and silver 2s. Perhaps a
little fall from £2l to £l3 in the world’s price of a
district’s staple product is of no consequence;
but, anyhow, “Anti-Caucus” might just as well
have alluded to it.
Agar Wynne (Vic.), of the Opposition
corner, who voted against the Government in the
recent crisis, was for a few brief moments lately
quite the chief figure in the Reps. The House
was dealing with the Senate amendment to reduce
,the duty on rotary and percussive rock-drills from
20 per cent, to 5 per cent, and free. Tariff Bill
Lvne moved that the amendment be not made,
and Wynne, remembering his pledge to his
electors, spoke in favor of the higher duty.
Later on, when the House divided, Wynne was
seated complacently amongst the Noes. Hume
Cook crossed the floor and apparently pointed
out to him his glaring inconsistency ; but Wynne
did not move. It was only when Oom Bill him-
self crossed over and said a few words that Wynne
tore himself from his place and, amid the howls
of the Opposition, recorded his vote on the side
that he had spoken on. However, owing to the
defection of Iceberg Irvine and of the incredible
Palmer, the motion was lost.
There are no State Frights, anyhow, about
Vic. Labor-leader Prendergast. At the Melbourne
Eight Hours Demonstration the other day :
In his opinion (and he believed the views were widely
shared by members of his party) an extension of powers
should be given to the Federal Parliament. ... He
would amend section 51 of the Constitution so that it
would provide that the Federal Parliament “shall have
power to make' laws for the peace, order and good
government of the Commonwealth.” That would make
the Commonwealth Parliament supreme, without anv
limitation of subjects of legislation.
To which State Insect Bent replied with his usual
polish :
I am inclined to think that if there was a vote it would
be in favor of rubbing the Commonwealth out.
Well, there was a vote of all Australia less than
two years ago, and Australia elected certain repre-
sentatives, who have established a sort of com-
mittee, called a Ministry, to carry out their
wishes. Yet, instead of wanting to “rub the
Commonwealth out,” these representatives of the
people want the Commonwealth to exercise more
of the power it has ; and every time it moves that
way, Bent and others squeal. Bent professes a
lot of respect for “ a vote of the people ” ; but he
shows mighty little for the vote of the people
after it has been taken.

the reports of unemployed difficulties in U.S.A.,

were the result of a high Protective tariff, and
was he not aware that the Australian high tariff
made similar consequences possible. Protection
does make such conditions possible in Australia
by giving men employment which they can be
thrown out of if certain contingencies arise.
Certainly men can’t be thrown out of work if they
haven t any, and that is where Freetrade comes in.

THE OMINOUS BENCHES.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Renovation is sadly needed in the British House of
Lords. Anyone looking’ at the Peers when the House is
full would gain the impression that they sat in a curiously
huddled fashion. The explanation is that the scarlet-
covered benches are so worn, and the springs in the
cushions so broken, that in many cases a Peer does not
sit. He is instead surrounded by his seat. . . . The real
explanation of why the Peers do not assemble till late is
that they wish to disguise under artificial light the in-
tolerable dilapidations of their Chamber.— London Daily
Mail, 20/3(08.
Old, old as sin are they, and worn, and broken,
And feeble in the joints ;
A gruesome standing portent, sign or token
To those whom Rank anoints.
Gone are the days when British Peers were bowed to
By poor folk in the streets ;
Now, in their own House they’re not e’en allowed to
Have comfortable seats.
Huddled the titled ones crouch low in clusters,
And try to legislate
Upon the floor. (Whereat the rude Lords-busters
Stand smilingly elate.)
Home Rule, and Education Acts, and Land Bills,
And such-like garniture, *
These splendid paupers slay, ’mid shifting sand-hill?
Of ruined furniture.
Weirdly antique conventions, long dead phrases,
And mildewed views are theirs.
Yet one thing more than all of these amazes :
The deadness of their chairs.
Old, old as sin are they, and worn, and broken,
And feeble in the joints ;
A gruesome standing portent, sign or token
To those whom Rank anoints.
N.S.W.

FACILE PRINCEPS
A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, May 5, 1908.
My Dear Arint,—
A flavor of reminiscence and a suggestion of no-
matterness hung round the Northcote’s last
Society function in Sydney—the garden party.
Before the next G. P. (not general paralysis,
please) seasons blooms their Exes will have
gone elsewhere. Lady Northcote wore the
gown with Australian-made lace which enclosed
her when she opened the Women’s Exhibition in
Melbourne. And she clasped a thousand hands,
one at a time of course, with her feet on the
presentation rug that went with the gown on that
historic occasion. “ According to present-day
standards.” as the Last Scream op Fashion would
phrase it, her Ex.’s gown was out of date. Its
three skirts of glace, white chiffon, and black
chiffon rolled and billowed like a Manly breaker.
The short-sighted party who exclaimed after a
glance at the tram lying, a long soft pile,
along the carpet, “ O, someone’s lying
down at Lady Northcote’s feet,” was under-
standable. The no-matterness had to do with the
cards of invitation. One was not requested to
present her allotment of cardboard at the gate.
The usual stream of humans —soldiers, sailors,
politicians, professionals and their consorts —began
at 3.30, and was not dammed till an hour , later.
The only stranger of interest was Max
Schliiter. Max was here six years ago with his
fiddle and his Joachim, duck-tail hair. A
handsome youth he was then, and he
hasn’t gone off. Professional musicians whose
melody echoes round the globe have, with
rare exceptions, faces that would make a veteran
war-horse jump the nearest gate. Max is one of
the exceptions, and his modesty is also an excep-
tion. The Face-at-the-Window musician, like the
star male mummer, ought, with rare exceptions,
to be kept on ice under a steam-roller until he’s
wanted on the platform or stage. Count Morner,
Consul-General for Sweden, toted his musicianly
countryman to the party. But there had been
almost an interruption of diplomatic relations be-
tween the distinguished pair owing to the Consul’s
determination that Max should wear the conven-
tional section of black railway tunnel on his head,
instead of the soft, artistic thing with a viaduct
along the top and a saucy brim. Max said he’d
die first, and vowed he looked like a “ mon-key
in that kind of hat. The Count won, and after
all Max didn't look like the animal Cain is said
to have married.
As a crowd-collector a vice-regal carriage
standing in the street runs a tram accident close.
A fallen horse drags a crowd of a sort—mostly
men. A dog-fight (if they are earnest dogs) and
the arrest of a drunk haye their followers. But
the Northcote equipage, stopped in a city street
with a lancer in khaki at the tail of the carriage,
makes for a side show at which a real lady need
not feel ashamed to be present. When Lady
Northcote went into Vickery’s Chambers, on
Wednesday, to open the Arts and Crafts Ex. with
a bow and a smile and a. few words, the
“gallery” gathered to look at what she had
temporarily left. A small boy, carrying an
urgent telegram, paused to scan the horses. Then
he leant against some brick background, and kept
his gaze glued to the lancer. Within a few
minutes a crowd of some 150 persons had col-
lected. The men looked at the horses, the girls
and small boys at the lancer, and the women
stared at the rugs and wraps in the carriage. A
few persons, learning why her Ex.’s chariot
stopped the way, went into the Show and helped
local talent by buying a hand-painted- cup and
saucer, or a calendar worked with a blue silk
oat turning a somersault, or a dado design in
kangaroos and waratahs.
It was a show to make Australians proud.
Miss Mildred Creed and Miss Edwell were work-
ing at hand-made jewellery—necklets, hatpins
and the like. Lady Northcote secured some of the
hatpins—enamel and oxidised silver. Misses Ethel
Stevens, Dorothy Wilson and S, Yeomans showed
exquisite specimens of their own book-binding.
Miss Gether’s hand looms and MissEirene Mort’s
haqd-made toys and original designs for curtains,
cushions and friezes were amongst the magnets of
the Show. Her Ex. acquired a cup and saucer
painted with wattle and the purple sarsaparilla, a
stamped-leather bag, and other enviable atoms of
art. , - _
A bunch of most frou-frauish fixtures divided
Sydney’s interest on Saturday. The new
Lady-o’-War Poore gave her first party (it
was an under-tfle-welkin one); the Uni
Commem. occurred; but the function with
most rustle was the Automobile Club’s
first racing carnival at Victoria Park. A man
with the instinct of a State Treasurer told
me there was automobile to the value of £l5O 000
on the ground. A Sydney architect, named
Manson, is reckoned to have the most diamond-
dusted car. A merchant in the Asiatic trade E
C. Waters, likewise scores with a beauty. Also
this fortunate man has annexed as wife the most
beautiful girl in Sydney, who, in her turn knows
how tp, wear the superlative frocks that Providence
has layuihed upon her. And a little while ago he
had the most swan-like sea-craft on our beautiful
harbor. Yet the parsons say that Heaven doesn’t
80,0; w favoritism,. Ugh !
Hospital Saturday, another big fixture, brought
a record collection of over £6OOO. The “zeal”
displayed, by som,e of the girls engaged in the
bail-up charity work bordered on impudent vul-
garity, and it’s time the Government saw its way
to have a taxed tote, or some such circus, to take
the place of the circus girl as hospital collector.
A.s for the third rustle, the commemorating
Lm. students wore the usual quite superfluous
asses ears. ,
Carrie Moore, very much thinner than when
she left here five years ago, was at “The L-idv
Dandies” op Saturday night in a soft white silk
and a blaze of diamonds. Three huge ones struno-
- a stiff bit of necklet gold rather extinguished
Carrie s eyes, a,nd they do, not lack brilliancy.
The returned vital spark confesses that “ no one
could teach her singing.” She would not open
her mouth as the experts commanded, and could
not conform to artificial laws of voice-produc-
tion. She sings as the birds sing. When
she went to London she put in a certain
time of melody-learning from Ivan Caryll’s
wife (I forget her name), but personally she
does not think she acquired more technique than
when with the teachers Mr. J.C.W. sooled on to
her early in her. stage career. She narrowly
escaped a Parisian engagement. Before closing
with the offer, she floated to Paris to look at the
class of performance given at the Olympia. She
summed it up as mostly tripe, and drifted
back to London, where her post-card photo-
graphs have such a huge sale that the
firm that holds the monopoly must be
getting deliberately and sinfully rich. She
signs hundreds of these at so much a card, and
gives the money to a theatrical charity. Vital
sparkishness runs in the Moore family as fully as
voice does in the Castles brigade. Five sisters of
Carrie are on the stage, cr going there. The
youngest has been with the Pollards in America,
and is said to be a genius.
The engagement is announced of Miss “Pattie”
Mort and Baron Zezza di Zapponata. The baron
has been in Sydney for the last couple of years on
a business mission. He pursued the same with
unflagging and praiseworthy zest, despite the fact
that his ornate clothes daily and nightly gave the
lie to any suggestion of commercialism in his
regard. His future bride is a dainty Dresden
China type of maiden, who looks passing sweet On
a horse. She is a Laidley Mort, and hence the
“ good Roman ” that is essential when the Italian
nobleman decides to “range himself” and marry.
Most deadly is the trouble that upheaves when
the Fascinating Young Thing happens along, and
—wittingly or otherwise —burgles the regard of
her friend’s “ steady company.” That world-old
process is in train at present in local theatrical
circles. Abelard, the youthful, who self-sacrific-
ingly deserted a wife and several encumbrances
some years ago for love of his not-so-youthful
(though almost equally encumbered) Heloise, has
fallen to the boards the helpless, hopeless, blither-
ing victim of a dainty colleague. The sweet little
lady who is—wittingly or otherwise —responsible,
views the occurrence with equanimity, tinged ever
so faintly with pardonable pride. But Heloise—
well, mere Anglo-Saxon doesn’t begin to do jus-
tice to the agitated condition of Heloise, nor can
Shakspeare’s tongue cope with the powerful lan-
guage-strain she puts on it daily and demi-nightly
these times. If things go much further, she pro-
poses to expose the whole affair to erring Abe’s
wife—on a point of principle. While losing a
lover, she will thus surely achieve a firm female
friend. Nothing on this paradoxical old earth
draws two women together so closely as does the
silken bond of desertion by the same man.
Abelard’s temper is said to be something worse
than walking barefoot on prickly pear. On an even-
ing last week he came off the stage in a fury about
some mistake a new mummer had made. Appealing
for sympathy to Eloise, the lady said, “Serve you
right! ” It was then he fell upon and dealt her
several blows. “Such a thing never happened
before,” assert the veterans, and they are glad the
“ gentleman ” is not an Australian.
Mrs. Fanny Waddell, wife of Colonel G. W.
Waddell, died at “ Nevada,” Macleay-street, last
week. The bereaved, who commands the local
Field Force, is N.S.W.’s chief citizen soldier.
Mrs, Popplewell (her warble-name is Rosa Bird)
said good-bye—expressed in music and refresh-
ments —at the Australia on Thursday. The lady
has sung for some charities, collected a Saratoga
full of information about woman’s place in this
sorry scheme of things, worn some beautiful gowns
and, finally, has boarded the Oruba en route for
foreign parts. A small girl pianiste, Winifred
Purnell, goes with the departer, in pursuit of
the study that gives the student round shoulders.
Enthusiasm over Captain Cook raged like a
murrain during two days of last week. Only the
public didn’t know it till the morning papers
revealed and expounded the fact. As the foolish
month of April wanes, someone washes Captain
Cook’s statue in Hyde Park till it shines like
glazed calico, and sticks a flower in the great
navigator’s ear. The anniversary bath is followed
by the pilgrimage to Kurnell. This year the
Governor-General “broke” the flag—in plain Kur-
. nellese, he hoisted it.. Sir Harry gave him his moral
support; so did the Poore Admiral, the Trustees,
the Works Minister Lee, Miss Lee, Mrs. J. J.
Cohen, Mrs, E.. W. O’Sullivan and one of her
ewe lambs. Miss Lee is the? fiancee of Captain
Carstairs, late of Geelong, now of South Africa.
He is a cousin of Lord Methuen. As for
Captain Charles, M.L-C., if he were not.
present at the ceremony, the other “mourners”
would feel defrauded. Captain Charles is the
grand old man who, when he was a young man,
talked with an old heathen, who, when he was a
young heathen, saw the elder heathen slay Cook
in the Sandwich Islands,
On Friday there was more Cookey, when the
Yorkshire Society girded up its dialect and hied'
it to the w T ashed statue in Hyde Park and' affixed
a bronze tablet to the plinth, congratulating the
great navigator on having been a Yorkshirernan.
Mr. Kilburn Scott, president of the York Society,
stroked the intellectual fur of those present by
dredging up a whole bunch of distinguished
names the owners of which were Yorkshiremen.
Dr. Camidge, also a Yorkshire terrier, claimed
Sir Harry Rawsonr as one. The most fetching
Yorkshirernan I’ve met was in a book—John
Browdie, the corn factor, who ate with such
enthusiasm in “ Nicholas Nicklebv.’’
An Event locally was the marriage (30/4/’08)
of Mr. C. S. Parbury, second son of merchant
Charles Parbury, now permanently domiciled in
London, and Miss Doris Bloinfield. The Dangar,
Knox and Allen clans hastened to the 1 festival in
great numbers (they are all more or less related to
either bride or bridegroom), and afterwards the
wedded pair sped hurriedly off in a motor of
multiple horse-power to the Arthur Allen’s Port
Hacking cottage. Honeycomb House. ‘ In that
fair spot they • will angle unrecriminatingrly for
the whiting that biteth not, and the bream
which contemns the dilettante’s ill-adjusted
prawn-bait. For has it not been written that love
covers a multitude of empty fish-baskets ?
The Parbury-Blomfield wedding was like a
conference of peacocks for style. There was plenty
of tail and “side.” One wonders if Mr. Allen him-
self is ever a tenant of Honeycomb House. No
sooner has one smart bridal pair gone out of it to
start housekeeping on their own than two more
people are motor-careering down for their moon-
struck time. And the motor-car is also the fairy
god-father's property.
Another wedding of last week was a naval one.
Lieutenant Pritchard, a man-o’warof the Prome-
theus, was married to Miss Mollie Simmons,
youngest daughter of the late Mr. M. Simmons.
A frilly reception was held by the bride’s mother
at the Australia.
Talking of Big Sea Craft weddings—the India
was dolled up with a wreath on a day last week.
One of the officers, Worthington by name, had
not taken Punch’s advice to those about to marry.
But it’s not usual for the mercantile marine to
-frivol wreathfully on these occasions.
Vladimir V. de Sviatlowsky, Councillor M.A.,
D.E. Political Economy, Imperial University,
St. Petersburg, Russia, is a distinguished stranger
within our gates. He is asking questions on Labor
matters, like a catechism or a census paper.
Miss Eva Champion’s farewell concert will be
given in the'Centenary Hall, on May 12. Lady
Northcote has taken twelve tickets—the same
number as the tribes of Israel—and Miss Rawson
will be present with a party. The Girls’ Realm
Guild is managing the concert.
Miss Mignon Scantleburv, daughter of a well-
known mining man, has joined “the Lady Dandies”
Co., much to the co.’s benefit in the matter of
looks. While in her leggy, wingy phase of girl-
hood the damsel’s portrait was painted by
Longstaff. Now she is very tall, and dark, with
brilliant black eyes and a silky wave of hair.
The most “viewy” and fashionable meeting
that occurs in connection with a charity is the
annual one at the Ashfield Infants’ Home. It is
a hardy annual also, and is taken most seriously
by such persons as Mr. J. A. Hogue, Mr. Justice
Cohen, Dr. A. E. Mills, and Principal Harper.
Miss Rawson goes out year-ily to preside, ana on
the last occasion (last week) Mrs. Barbara Bavn-
ton, the novelist, who used to be on the com-
mittee, made a reappearance. The report dis-
closed that during the year 132 children had been
cared for, of whom 15 were the children of widows
and deserted wives, 31 were the children of
widowers, or had mothers ill or insane, six had
fathers out of work, 78 were illegitimate, one
was both motherless and fatherless, and one a
foundling.
Mrs- Geddes Crawford’s lecture last week was a
cleverly-strung-together series of anecdotes,
stories,' and impressions of travel. Walking on a
country road in Ireland one evening, the lecturer
Overheard a “ bhoy ” entreating his Norah to go
home by a still quieter and more leafy route. “ I’ll
not walk wid ye by that lane,” objected Norah,
“for if I did, ye’d be afcher kissin’ me.” “i
wouldn’t,” asserted the bhoy ; “ I’m too dacint.”
Later in the night the observer again saw the
pair, this time saying good-bye. * The bhoy
with fervent respect was saying it at arm’s-
length. “ Good-night,” said Norah, in a disgusted
tone; “ good-night, Patsy, and bad luck to yer
dacincy !” Mr. Scott Fellchairmanned the lecture,
and made an informative and humorous introduc
tory address. A reference contained in it brought
a sigh from the women present —a sigh that
sounded like “ So would 1.” Mrs. Crawford,
said the chairman, “has been a traveller, a
farmer, a journalist, and a housemaid. In the
last capacity, I would willingly guarantee her a
year’s engagement.” It was at that point the
concerted sigh occurred.
Contralto Ella Caspers has won a “Golonial
Exhibition” from the Associated London College
of Music. This means free tuition at that insti-
tution. Miss Caspers will give a concert at Goul-
burn presently, and then proceed to England and
the higher voice culture.
A ball that deserves all the support it
can get is that which will roll at the Mosman
Town Hall on Tuesday, May 19, in aid of the
North Shore Women’s and Children’s Hospital.
A grand procession of fancy sets will be one of
the attractions. Mayoress Leahy,- of Mosman, is
president. The hon. treasurers are Mrs. E. W.
O’Sullivan and Mrs. J. H. Phipps, and hon: secre-
taries are Mrs. F. Fraser and Mrs. M. Casper.
The Stork called in on May Day at the Bickem
hall Mansions (London) flat which is inhabited
by the young Rawson couple. The bird’s beak-
luggage proved to contain a girl encumbrance of
great beauty and lung power. Grandfather Raw-
son was straightway informed, by cable, of the
fowl’s gift and (for very joy) he has not been the
same mariner since. He hasn’t exactly led a
different life, but there has been a notable change,
and a tendency to leap several feet in the air.
Miss Maggie Knight (Mrs. H. Henderson) has
joined the Beatty-Mclntosh Co., and will appear
next Saturday as Madame Prudence in the weep-
ful “Camille.”
Miss Maggie Moore (Mrs. Roberts) and her
husband are staying at the Hotel Mansions. The
lady, though too opulent of figure, is wonderfully
youthful-looking, with quite ungreyed hair and
no make-up. t [
There was something apparently new under
the Easter sun—or, to be accurate, timer
the electrics at the Town Hall the other
night. Salvationists held a memorial service
for the dead relatives of those present. The
platform was crowded with girls wearing Red
Riding Hood garb. Out of the red hoods the young
faces “gleamed gashly,” as an onlooker phrased it.
Portraits of the dead—memoriamed one at a time
—were thrown on a screen, together with ,their
last words. So few “last words ” are worth publi-
cation on a lantern slide or otherwise. The
“ Roll wp the map 1 of Europe” and “Light, more
light ” phrases are rare—and generally faked by
the living. Therefore I am starting a bureau for
the supply of last words to the eminent dead
before they die. A catalogue of sonorous Sayings
will be sent around to elderly gentlemen who
don’t look well, and the price of the noble phrases
will range from £5 upwards. The purchaser will
select and pay for his final observation, and as
soon as his death is reported (accurately or other-
wise) the bureau will send his final utterance to
the press. If he disappears at sea, his hist noble
remark will be washed ashore in a bottle, and the
bureau will find the bottle on the beach. I expect
to make my fortune out of this idea.
“ Mario ” writes from Brisbane :—
The fierce light that pounds upon the vice-regal thron
was loaned out to the opening concert of the lirisbvn
Musical Union on Tuesday. The Governor anti h'
missus, with Beaudesert parson Hone and his new golden 8
haired other half, flanked by the usual A.DC and
private secretary and the meek little governess filled
the vice-regal enclosure. A huge audience of mere
common public wedged itself into the hall to hea
Miss Florence Quinn, a Madame Slap, student from
Sydney, assist the Union through “The Requiem"
a “Tannhauser” item, and various other things She
did it all very prettily. Miss F. Q. may not have a veri-
wonderful voice as yet, but she has a daisy of a chest that
shows large possibilities of development. Also she has a
pretty face and a graceful, willowy figure, and a pile of
pretty hair, and confidence. She is, in fact, one of the
best developed promises, in a throaty way, that has been
seen hereabouts since a period so remote that the memory
of man scrambleth not back to the contrary, but rather
loses itself in a wilderness of research and returns again
with empty hands and dog-tired as to the feet. *V;
Thursday evening saw the first ball of the season burst
its shell and hatch itself into a successful frivol, ft took
place on the sloping floor of the Exhibition Concert Hall
(this up-to-date city has no dance-hall of respectable size
except a barn of a place that smells like a fowlhouse) and
great was the curiosity it raised. No less than 600 folk
paid two threepenny tram fares and a shilling entrance
just to look on. The committee stood round anxiously
first mostly on one leg, and then mostly on the other’
to receive the Chelmsfords, Lord arid Dame, along
with whom came Mrs. Hone and Captain’s Ver-
ney and Newton. The ladies, together with our
plump little Mayoress and her mother-in-law, were all
laden with floral offerings. The equally plump Mayor
w'as there too, but no one so much as hurled an epithet
at him. When the vice-regal set was formed the on-
lookers nearly fell over the gallery with shock, for the
Mayor and Mayoress were left out of it! Everyone was
asking each other what they had done to be thus
slighted, but the fact is that they were once forced into
a vice-regal Lancers, and as on that occasion they nar-
rowly escaped with their lives, they are taking no further
chances. The dangerous honor went instead to bis
Wash up and wife of South Brisbane, who came through
intact but breathless.
Yours Affectionately,

MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, Mat 4, 1908.
My Dear Yarrie, —
Carrie Moore rushed through Melbourne like a
jolly little meteor on Wednesday, and blew kisses
round the city in the three hours' interval between
Expresses. A gleam from her eyes was about all
that was visible of the wanderer through the thick
cloud of worshippers.
The approaching marriage is announced of
Gordon Robert, elder son of widow Todd, of Kew,
and favorite grandson of the late Robert Laidlaw,
of Springbank, Heidelberg, to Ailsa, daughter of
Adam Smith, of Gippsland. Miss A.ilsa Smith is
just out of her teens —a small girl with a striking
personality and some brains.
The Laidlaws are sticklers for old customs,
old friends, old furniture, and other aged
props. The oak chest that the squire brought
with him from Scotland in the early ’forties still
holds a place of honor at Springbank. Adam
Smith was a shipmate of Robert Laidlaw. So the
friendship has come down to the third generation,
to be cemented in marriage.
A farewell benefit was given to Mr. Arthur
Greenaway on Wednesday night. The Melbourne
Savages brought their lubras and other belong-
ings, and made a solid block in the audience.
The Chief Justice beamed from the front rows.
Son Guy the Eat had a snug corner between the
two Atherton beauties. Most of the habitual
amateur Sassiety play-actors collected in force to
criticise the opposition amateur shows. In
an act from “School for Scandal,” a quite
freshly-blossomed amateur, Miss Gwen Burroughs,
made a hit as Lady Teazle. Her performance
was far beyond the ordinary amateur quality, and
she is a remarkably handsome girl to whom much
would have been forgiven, if there had been any-
thing to forgive, which there wasn’t.
The auburn-haired Evans sisters twirled on their
hard-worked Big Toes. The Zingari orchestra
tinkled some, and there was some warbling. Alf
Vincent, who was to have lightning-sketched, was
a poor sick man, home in bed. An act from “ The
Merchant of Venice” brought amateur John
Reid into prominence. His Antonio was an
artistic success, which belied the John Bullish
stolidity of his everyday name.
The Tait Brothers have added motors to their
everyday rush. Seven Taits and seven motors
make the life of Gollins-street amblers an unhappy
whirl.
The Taits gave Big Clara a diamond souvenir
of her Australian tour. It was a brooch, about
by 4in., patterned into “Cooee; ” Clara was
delighted especially by its size. She had
scarcely a single trinket smaller than a serviette-
ring or a cruet. The Butt consort got a set of black
opal waistcoat buttons from an anonymous lady
(who probably had been reducing the quality of the
kids’ school-boots to help pay for them). The pair
took away almost as many valuables from this
State as the ducal Yorks did, and left just as little.
Since the personal feeling these visitors arouse is
so slight, it must be an - epidemic of snobbishness
which loads the touring voices, and dukes, and
things with expensive luxuries.
In the “Hook” Co. local youths have found a
charming object for their enthusiasm in Miss
Gwen Boyce; she’s distinguished by not wearing
even powder, and is the beauty of the imported
company.
That admirable and un-actor-like mummer,
Arthur Greenaway, stays round the land of his
birth for a while longer. He joins his old friend,
H. R. Roberts, in the “Prince Chap,” to play
the same parts they played in the London pro-
duction of it. Incidentally there’s a whole mob
of glad men and women here looking forward
anxiously to the advent of Maggie Moore.
J.C.W.’s imported conductor, Mr. Carr, is to
blossom forth this winter as an orchestral com-
poser. If time and the exigencies of his orchestral
arrangements permit, the gifted Beard will con-
duct a Carr Symphony at one of his concerts. It’s
a good precedent. Until now such opportunities
have been limited to a coterie. Henceforth, with
the aid of enterprising Eredk. 8., we have a bare
chance of discovering a masterpiece.
A new scandal has slipped from the lips of
Rumor, like a toad from the mouth of the demon-
stricken little girl in Grimm’s story. A dainty
little lady with a quick temper is the victim.
Because she got into a fearful rage over a minor
folly of her bell-toppered half, and went to stay
with a relative for a couple of days, the malignant-
Dame says there’s going to be a divorce, and a
tragedy, and what not. Meanwhile, the lady has
cried on hubby’s shoulder, and forgiven him, and
said—as we always do in forgiving our dears—that
it was mostly her fault, and the two are again
untroubled by anything worse than the hussy
Gossip’s interjections and her disconcerting
questions. Real scandals have made us most
hungry cannibals. When human reputations are
not offered us to devour, we try to drive the
innocent into the killing pen.
The Ice Rink opened on Saturday for its
winter season, and things point to a fresh boom in
the cold swirl. Last season suffered a slight slump
to pay for the overdone fad of the first winter,
but Sassiety is getting its second wind in skating
matters,' and all the quite Upper Fluff cut up the
ice to the opening waltzes. The field was rather
thin and watery in its chilly middle, but that only
gave a realistic touch with its "Danger” and
"Thin Ice” signals.
President Henry Wrixon and his wife filled
three or four rows of the Princess’s circle on
Wednesday evening with a Parliamentary party.
Bent —bald-headed and curved —was put in the
very centre of the very front row—just to show
Peter Pan what he had missed by refusing to
grow up and become a Premier.
A pal writes from London of Australians in the
Fog:—
Amy Castles is blooming, and roly-poly fat. She re-
turned to-day from Holland, where she sang for a couple*
of nights at The Hague. The Queen’s mother went speci-
ally to hear her. Amy is also singing at Queen’s Hall on
Saturday, and on Sunday at the Albert Hall. Mischa
Elman, the wonderful boy violinist, is performing with
her. She goes in a few days to sing in Dublin, after
which to Ostend, and to Schweningen later.' She was to
have appeared in opera at Monte Carlo. I saw her con-
tract —a very fine salary and everything most favorable.
But at the last moment her managers in London and
Berlin strongly advised her not to go. She was bitterly
disappointed, but had to abide by their decision. She
has decided to return to Australia in January or February
of next year.
Dolly Castles is at present on tour with George
Edwardes’ principal company. She goes to Dublin next.
Pretty little Eileen Castles is the next, to be launched.
Mr. D’Oyley Carte heard her sing last week, and promises
great things for her future.
Rose Musgrove is looking well. She has received an
offer to appear in London, and is considering it.
Mrs. War-boat-Doctor-Lavertine (Ethel Gunn, of Mel-
bourne) is in London with her husband and baby, on their
way to Ireland. His boat is stationed at Plymouth, and
Ethel has a flat near by.
Baritone Haigh Jackson is with George Edwardes on
tour—he is to appear in London shortly. Mrs. Haigh,
the magnificent, teaches singing here.
Ada Crossley is simply booming. She looks forward
very much to August and her Australian home trip.
Shirley Nicholson, the Adelaide beauty girl, now Mrs.
King, has a flat with her sister. The husband has
dropped out of sight since she took to stage work.
Willie Murdoch, the Bendigo boy, who won the Clarke
Scholarship for Piano in Victoria, is working hard. He
has just won the medal at the Royal College from all
comers, though he has only been there two years.
From “Tryphena,” in London: —
The marvel of John Bull’s “ upper succles ” at present
is the activity of the temperance microbe. So briskly
does it circulate that two French firms of champagne
manufacturers have cried out in distress. The Liberal
party is mostly credited with the cold water creed. But I
sat at dinner in the House of Commons crammed caf6 one
night, and observed that only at one Unionist table was
any golden wine a-bubbling, and then only in the case of
three big, busy, hot-throated members, while two other
males sipped “ Polly,” and the four feminine flutterers
sparkled on mineral draughts of assorted German labels.
At adjoining tables the universal beverage was water—
plain or fancy. At the Ritz, the day previously, I had
also noticed the activity of the blue ribbon microbe. Yes,
even at the brilliantly frivolous Ritz, parties sipped
and sparkled on iced draughts of that which eometh up
out of the earth. In the middle-classes of all-rural
England, the microbe has been long active, and the
calm-eyed, clear-complexioned, steady-nerved woman-
kind one meets at private feast or public banquet in
county centres, strikes one as being the admirable
result of the cold-water policy of life.
Of course, further down the social scale, the übiquitous
bedraggled female is the eyesore and disgrace and
horror of every village and town of Merrie (?) England.
She haunts the street-corners, and tumbles in and out of
bar entrances all day long. Likewise, there’s another
type of female thumbing the wine-lists in some of the big
popular cafes, and looking long upon the bubbly-water
and the glad red vintages. This type is frilly; her
clothes are Paquin-labelled; her complexion, her hair,
her teeth, her smiles, all bear the tone of the most ex-
pensive Art Upholsterers and Personal Decorators of
Regent-street. So she, giddily at the top of the Siren
class of this Old Smoke, and the vagrant or waster at the
other end, keep (on the feminine side) the wine trade
going. But aristocratic Clarissa Mayfair has taken to
“Polly,” and placid Elizabeth Bull, of Lancashire, has
quaffed scarcely anything but plain water since she
rocked in her cot. Also, no Englishwoman of any import-
ance takes sugar these days in her tea.
The next thing to call for virtuous reform is the passion
of clothes that rages in the heart of the feminine Lon-
doner. Shopland here is vast—Shopland is alluring—
Shopland is viciously fascinating to a class which does
not know what it is to desire and not to have. All day
long the colossal establishments of frocks and frills
in Bond and Regent and Oxford streets are
crammed with bewitched women, who are either
buying, or gnashing their teeth that they cannot buy. I
have seen the wild look of clothes-lust in the eye of
the she-purchaser, and I have watched a whole Purgatory
play in the eyes of a female who could not purchase.
Day after day the tragedy of this clothes-passion goes on.
Those who can pay are rushing from department to de-
partment, with an intoxicating gleam of triumph in the
eye, with a fierce, mad joy burning their fair faces.
Those who can’t pay and who can’t get the credit are
prowling round, eyeing the fascinating apparel with the
glare of hungry coyotes. Outside at the windows the
gorgeous Jezebels look laughingly at the show of new
alluring fripperies, and then scout for masculine advice
or aid in the matter. Others again, getting desperate in
their greed, march in with the air of duchesses and pur-
loin what they can, or try to exchange the bogus cheque
for the petticoats and the jabots and the laces and the
ruffles. Half of London’s Society spends all its wits seek-
ing to secure swagger apparel. It gets its saucv silk
stockings and its sable muff by fair means or foul. It
must get them.
The result is that a poor, innocent sight-seer, on look-
ing quietly into a clothes window in Regent-street, finds
herself wolfishly regarded by a dressy Hoggenheimer, or
slyly by the Honorable Augustus Hatigabout, and has’ to
move on sharply, if she does not desire any tender atten-
tions—for every philanderer in the Big Smoke con-
cludes that the way to Woman’s soul is via a shop
and that every female who stands to gaze upon a dream
of a hat is under a spell till she secures the hat. What-
ever they think, they render it impossible for any guile-
less member of the petticoat sex to stand examining
fashions in a shop front.
Another letter from London :
Winnie Yuill, of Sydney, now the Countess of Port-
arlington, is immensely pleased with her baby boy. She
was presented in Ireland recently. Motherhood is im-
mensely fashionable in the Big Smoke just now, and if
you have no baby you must borrow one. Abduction of
babies is also fashionable, no doubt on account of the
extreme prevalence of divorce. Smart Set fathers or
mothers are always bolting with the babes and spending
much money on the affair.
At Princes’ you see almost everybody of interest.
Noticed them the other day. Admiral Sir Percy Scott—
a round little man with rather a rough sailor manner.
He skates well, and affects the youngest and prettiest
girls as partners. His brother is almost as bio - a
man on the music halls as Admiral Percy is* at
sea. He is one of the cleverest of women personators.
Also noted a very tall, square-shouldered man in a
Guards tie, who attracted by his look of radiant youth
He is the son of the cricketer peer and his Australian wife.
Lord and Lady Darnley. The übiquitous Australian is
thick, of course. Mrs. Samuel, wife of baronet “ Ted,”
of Sydney, and daughter-in-law of the late Saul Samuel,
is one of the most assiduous devotees. Her girl Vera is
going to be a beauty when she comes out of schoolgirl
frocks. The Misses McQuade seldom miss a practice, and
at one time or another most of the Australians visiting
or living in London are to be seen there.
The marriage of Miss Vi Levy and Mr. Louis
Henry Stephens was a quiet evening ceremony in
the Australian Church, with an informal party at
the Winter Gardens afterwards. The bride,
who is very young, wore a pleasingly simple girly
frock, in place of the frill-frill of satin and court-
train, and a veil of wonderful embroidery.
After she had dispensed the wedding cake, she
got into toggery evidently tailored out of prunes.
The bride’s mother swathed herself in layer
after layer of chiffon, until it reached sufficient
density for necessary decorum ; the top layer was
a cobweb imitation of a Paisley shawl and some
mitred-point lace. Mr. Bradford supported the
nerves of the bridegroom; and a blue-clad cousin,
hidden behind a mountain of violets, played maid
to the bride. The guests included an intense
group of schoolgirl chums—notably pretty Daisy
Price, with silver water-lilies in her hair, and
Nellie Mackenzie, a poster-picturesque girl in a
pink dress like a Shirley poppy, clasped with a
silver girdle. The newly-weds wandered off to
Medlow and the damp, cold caves of Jenolan.
Birdlings have given all sorts of reasons for
flying from the family nest; but a young woman
supplied what looks like a brand-new one in a
Melbourne court the other day. Her father was a
dentist, she said, and happening into his surgery
she found him boiling down a man’s head, “ fresh
from the undertaker’s.” She didn’t say whether
he was doing it in the family kettle ; but she left,
anyway.
The christening of the Willie A’Becketts’ baby
was a ceremony of much moment. It took place
in St. Andrew’s, Brighton (papa and mamma
A’Beckett had both been christened at that same
font and under that same fane, and were married
at the adjacent altar; so the church is in the
family, so to speak). Baby Joyce submitted to
the water drops with equanimity—not to say
pride, since she was wearing the embroidered
robe that took the prize at the Women’s Exhibi-
tion, and was so lovely that every other baby up
for baptism that day turned pale-green with envy
and said things about her. The A’Becketts have
given up the Toorak-road house for a few months,
and are staying at “ The George,” St. Kilda, so
that Baby may have sea air for lunch, and may
catch a whale in the afternoons.
Kubelik, after holding up the U.S.A. at the
point of the bow for 112 concerts, arrives in Syd-
ney via Vancouver on May 18. He does a season
in the Harbor city, then descends on Melbourne
for concerts on May 29, June 1, and June 10. The
rampage round Australasia will be short, as he
must be in London for a tour beginning October
6. Kubelik is an intensely engaged man. His
home, Bychory Castle, near Kolin, is a fairy-tale
palace, and the poor man never gets home for
more than two months in the year. Mrs.
Kubelik comes with her husband. She used to be
the Countess Cznaky-Cnell, but she got so tired
of spelling her name that she got married for a
rest. Someone who has met the Countess
describes her as a magnificent, auburn-haired
beauty—quite a Hungarian Rhapsody, in fact.
From “ Rosna,” of Adelaide: —
There was a whirl of excitement as the Melbourne
express drew out with the Butt-Rumford party. Just
before it was due to start, fiddler Barr 6 wandered on to
the platform, and in reply to the frenzied official who
pursued him, said his ticket was in the train. The official
said he had no right on the platform without a ticket,
and tried to eject him. Various members of the co.
leaned out of the train and mixed their voices with the
fray, and the inoffensive-looking fiddler was urged to
deeds of blood and daring. Finally he was hauled
on board what time secretary Ibbs delivered an
address to the limb of the railway, to which the limb
replied by taking Ibbs’ name and habitation, and threaten-
ing an action for bigamy on the high seas, or something
exciting. •
That enterprising Adelaide scribe, “ Sydney Partridge”
(Mrs. Hal Stone), who, with her husband and small son,
has once more returned to this abode of torpid trams, has
just brought out a book of short stories, written, printed,
and published by herself. The volume is calculated to
put the efforts of any mere man-printer in the dark, deep
shade. It is done in tw T o colors, on beauteous thick paper,
with wide margin?, and yet costs no more than a shilling.
Call for “Life’s Wallaby” when you want it, and note
that it is dedicated “ To those Fellow Travellers on Life’s
Wallaby who write in Australia, of Australia, for
Australia.”
The Lyric Club opened its season on April 27 with a
welcome to Edward Branscombe and his musical trouba-
dours. Only members were admitted ; but as the crowd
was less vast than usual, the restriction was unnecessary.
Miss Alston had converted the cosy drawing-room into
a gorgeous rose-bower, and here the guests drank coffee
and ate cakes when the other part of the fray was over.
Said other part of the fray consisted of some’ strenuous
instrumental items by members, the hilarity of a flash-
light photo, and finally some mirth and melody provided
by the visitors. They own a quartette of fine voices,
which they didn’t seem to mind using, even though their
ticket-taker wasn’t at the door. President and Mrs.
Basse received the visitors and did the entertaining.
The ex-Mayer Cohens, famed in South Oss for their
gorgeous sprees and lavish hospitality, have gone to
dwell in Melbourne for a year at least. They gave up
their jolly house 12 months or so ago when the daughters
married, and they won’t start housekeeping again—for a
time. In Melbourne they will live at the Grand.
Neil Hepper, known in Adelaide as a skilful amateur
mummer, who has performed for most charities under the
local sun, goes to Sydney shortly, some say to pursue the
mumming art professionally. Dramatic clubs and musi-
cal folk are joining forces to give him a complimentary
benefit-spree, and the Walkerville Hall, in consequence,
threatens to be packed to the doors before this reaches
the indignity of print.
Two recent weddings of interest hereabouts—first, that
of Dr. John Cleland and a clever Paton damsel; and then
that of Solicitor Emmett, of Sydney, and Miss Annie
Rowe, whose father manages the National Bank at Hind-
marsh.
The pumpkin mentioned in Scripture was a stationary
vegetable compared with the average fortune which
somebody on the other side leaves to an Australian.
Adelaide the other day smacked its lips over the report
that “ a well-known citizen of somewhat humble means ”
had dropped in for “ something like a million and a half.”
Gradually, however, the pile dwindled until one of the
police officers who had helped to search the heir out said
that the real sum mentioned was 1100 dollars ; and when
the man himself was tracked down, he was still following
his “humble occupation,” and reckoned that it would be
time to knock off when he actually got something—he
hadn’t got a cent so far.
Yours affectionately,
JOHANNA.

The Federal Capital Site.
Mr. Herbert E. Easton writes
Column one, page seven, of The Bulletin a6/4/’08') was
to me of most absorbing interest. '
“Six Men Who Met In A Small Room And Made A
Secret Agreement,” clearly sets forth the fact that but a
very small portion of this island continent was for a
moment, considered as being worthy the honor of con-
taining the Federal capital. And immediately beneath,
this article was “Indaba’s” very pertinent letter, and
his query:
“ Why, I ask in all seriousness, not select a site for the
Federal capital in the Northern Territory ? I have never
been able to discover wherein Australia, as a whole, would
benefit by yet another capital within the coastal belt
upon a (comparatively speaking) well settled section of
this continent. ”
These two pars, furnish food for much thought, and
suggest that very superficial and passing Consideration
has been given to the most important problem before-
Australia at the present moment.
There can be no doubt that the necessary millions will
be forthcoming when required to build our new capital
city, and surely it behoves every thinking individual in
the community to pause and consider how the money can
best be invested for the greatest benefit to ourselves and
the generations to follow us.
There is now a good opportunity of creating an en-
tirely new centre (such an opportunity as will almost
certainly never arise again), and it should not be passed
over lightly. It is not flattering to us, in our rdle of suc-
cessful colonisers, to reflect that during something over a
century of occupation we have only succeeded in partially
populating a narrow strip along the east and south coasts
of the country. Therefore, in my opinion, any favorable
soot, outside that sphere, for our Federal capital should
have due consideration.
The urgency of peopling the back country, and particu-
larly the Far North, is so generally admitted that
need be said upon that point. The greatest difficulties
to be overcome, before even getting the Northern Terri-
tory considered, are not likely to be those presented by
Nature, but rather those certain to be created by institu-
tions and individuals who regard everything from a per-
sonal standpoint—the standpoint of immediate gain *
The Federal capital in the Northern Territory ’is an
original conception—idealistic and, perhaps, extravagant -
nevertheless it is not too much to say that the scheme
■would have certainly attracted the attention of the great
Rhodes, for that astute statesman, having once decided
that a white population was urgently needed in the
North, would have found a means of getting it there
Rhodes “thought in continents,” while we, including-
our politicians, have not yet got beyond “thinking in
States.” Some day, perhaps, we shall find time to forget
about the States and consider our country as a whole
Further information about the Northern Territory
would be interesting reading, and indeed a general dis-
cussion upon the many phases of our future Federal
capital would prove highly educational matter.
Our great (in size) daily papers are busily engaged in
fostering inter-State jealousy and ill-will, and therefore I
turn to your paper with the feeling that you will
continue your efforts in the direction of creating some-
thing approaching a national feeling throughout the
Commonwealth.
»
Sydney Telegraph resurrected last week a par-
tially disused insult, and threw it violently at the
face of N.S.W. It was the old gibe about
the high duties which the Government has merci-
lessly levied against primary industries for the sake of a
few manufactures around Melbourne.
This petty, ignorant scoff used to turn up almost
daily in the ’Graph, and people who read it came,
at last, to the conclusion that N.S.W. had no
manufacturing industries at all. Yet that State
has nearly 80,000 people employed in its factories.
Its manufacturing industries are of nearly twice
the value of its agriculture; they are nearly four
times the value of its Cow business; they are
worth £2,000,000 a year more than its agricul-
ture and dairying put together; they are worth
more than its mines, forests, and fisheries com-
bined. And withal, the Telegraph, in its hatred
of Australian manufactures, won’t even mention
that N.S.W. has any factories. All the soured
importing rag knows of is “a few manufactures
around Melbourne.”

‘‘ITAN”; The saturnalia of cadge on Hospital
balances up badly. What does the
£6OOO raised cost ? There is the £5OOO worth of
hypocritical grin from the beggars with the jing-
ling boxes; the £5OOO worth of unvarnished lies
told in self-defence by bored and cleaned-out

one day each) besides the red gold spent
in feeding the unblushing philanthropists,
Tagging up stands, and carrying out pre-
liminary work. The only assets besides the
cash appear to be the quantity of liberty taken
with the city generally in the wild dash through
sacred precincts, the development of precocity
amongst the younger damsels, and the chances
which Leap year afforded for special
license. When chanty mites have to be
wrung from people by the methods of
Hospital baturday, the whole business is
at least humiliating. The institutions for the sick
poor should be nationalised. Why waste the energy
of 3500 able-bodied women in such a campaign”’
1 here is no suggestion that the money gathered
is not all accounted' for, but there have been oc-
casions when it has been unsatisfactorily
accounted for. Consider the case which occurred
at Brisbane a few years ago. A fete, figure-headed
by Important People, was organised, and the
public packed the building the whole
season. It spent freely, too, and the hard-up
hospitals of the city seemed to have a bright
prospect ahead. Then the bills trickled in, and
at length swamped the whole of the proceeds.
On audit, it was found that the bulk of the profit
was needed to pay for the entertainment of the
ladies of the committee, who seemed to have eaten
an ox apiece or thereabouts.

The N.S.W. Liberal and Deform League has
selected, in the latter-day fashion, its candidate
for St. George, the seat vacated by Carruthers.
Every elector who cared to squander a shilling
could be enrolled a member of any branch of the
League, and every branch could nominate a can-
didate. Arncliffe and Rockdale, the two popu-
lous centres, each selected a person of local im-
portance, and the contest degenerated into a
purely parochial cockfight as to whether Arncliffe
or Rockdale could pull off the ballot. The Arn-
cliffe man got a majority of 362 on his own dung-
hill, and Rockdale gave a surplus of 342 to
its especial rooster. Finally, Sans Souci, a
Rockdale suburb, was induced to throw in
its lot with Rockdale, and the Rockdale
person scrambled home. So, all true “Liberals”
are expected to vote for the man whose merit is
ttiat he lived at Rockdale, and so could get the
pull at a bigger machine than the man who only
lived at Arncliffe.
A shipment of 3000 tons of American
hops was among the tough things Winston
Churchill had to chew at the Manchester
election. The lot was to be dumped in Eng-
land about three weeks ago—one single ship-
ment equal to one-third of what has hitherto
been the total average annual import. The
Yankee shippers made no secret about it; there
has been a steady surplus of American hops
lately, so in order to get a new market they were
going to sell at a big loss, quite satisfied that one
or two such “ dumps ” would drive hundreds more
English hop-growers out of the already sickly,
because unprotected, business, and end by giving
them control of the British market and the power
to supply it at their own price.

Your Winter Dances
Of course, you know what a jolly time you can have with a few
friends round for a good dance. Unfortunately you cannot always
secure a good pianist and sometimes the piano itself is not available.
With an Edison Phonograph you can get the best Dance Music,
played by the leading bands, just as it should be. “On with the
dance, let joy be unconfined,” will be your wish when you hear the
Edison playing.
And in between the dances the Edison will furnish the most
delightful entertainment—almost anything you please from a music-
hall comic to a Grand Opera selection.
The Edison Phonograph
wi ‘ h Gold-Moulded Records
Think what it will mean to have the house always merry during
the long winter nights ! No need for the young people to go out
seeking entertainment. The Edison provides that at home.
It reproduces so clearly that it is hard to believe the actual per-
formers are not in the room. Parents owe it to their children to
investigate the merits of the Edison Phonograph. Do not confound it
with any other kind of talking machine you may have heard.
Edison Phonographs and Records have been greatly reduced in
price and are within the reach of almost anyone. Remember
the Edison
Will furnish the Music
no matter what kind you want.
Write for lists, and the name of your nearest dealer. He will be
glad to let you hear the Edison —free of charge.

Business, Robbery, etc.
“Jean Sibi ” : Melbourne Age has been girding
at the hawkers who adorn the business end of Prince’s
Bridge. Good ! For if anything is an eyesore, it is the
few yards of the city’s chief approach whereon Smellop-
oulous and Macaroni have planted their barrows. Both
races have an instinctive antipathy to cleanliness and
orderliness ; and thus the neighborhood is littered with
decaved fruit, scraps of paper, pieces of wood, and
sundries. Besides, the dark brown gentlemen are
not over clean in themselves. In many cases, the
hands that paw the fruit won’t bear inspection, and the
face that opens to emit the broken English is often
unshaven and forbidding. But those are small matters.
The fellows occupy a fine stand on the mere payment of
a small license fee to the City Council, which seems to
regard them with paternal affection. Their wares, ex-
posed to all the dry grime that blows along the thorough-
fare, are permitted to be sold for human consumption.
Any white shopkeeper observing the same habits would
be held up to scorn and contumely. But no one seems to
trouble about the dirty Dago hawker. His dirt is incur-
able. Nor does anyone appear to inspect his weights and
goods. Of course, he has been allowed to monopolise the
stand on the score of his material wretchedness and
poverty ; but even that is a fallacy, for the Dago hawker
is in most cases also the proprietor of a shop in the
suburbs.
* * * *
Wonderful the amount of enmity that is created
over old worked-out shows—principally reefs. Have
known men hang on for years to a cherished claim, sup-
posed to hold fabulous wealth, keeping the rent paid up
by strenuous toil on heaps of blue-metal, when, according
to their own fixed conviction, they could more easily be
breaking almost pure gold out of the claim. The labor
conditions are side-stepped by subterfuges well known to
all old prospectors ; and while the claim is locked up it
forms the topic of thrilling pub-verandah yarns. But,
perhaps tired of battling with hard blue-metal, harder
damper, and poor mutton, the old battler dies, then
there are 444 peg's bristling round that coveted claim.
But go round in 12 months’ time and you’ll find the pub-
verandah multitude talking about the fabulous wealth
locked up in some other claim, the reef in the last idol
not having turned out enough gold to buy a tin of jam.
* * *..m
In the little town of Millicent (S. Aus.) there
dwells a poet whose fiery soul is steeped in the blood of
the lower animals. Hear how this lamb-destroyer per-
suades the people to buy his wares
“ As It Was And Is Now.
AND it came about that in the shambles of the land
there was a dearth of Meat—sweet, prime, eatable
flesh of sheep and kine ; and the shekels of the people
were laid small, and of promptness and attention there 1
was none. But up rose
W. SCHULLER,
The People’s Butcher.
And when the people cried out unto him: ‘Givens
Meat,’Schuller hearkened unto the cry, and gave them'
Beef, Mutton and the goods that are made thereof. And
the famine that was in the land hereof gave way, and
prices fell, but Schuller lives. Help to keep him living
by giving a trial to
W. Schuller, The People’s Butcher, Millicent.
Families waited on daily for orders.”
The last line (alas !) is bathos. No poet should wind up
like that. Fancy David stating at the end of the Psalms
that families w'ere waited on daily for orders.
* » * *
“Sr.” bitterly assails two ancient professions:
It were a dreadful' thing surely to be (1) a draper or (2) an
undertaker. Both must needs be supernaturally obse-
quious. The successful undertaker continually wears
a face of grief. He is a bogus altruist; the shedder of
vicarious tears. I can conceive nothing more wearing
than to be forced to weep over the corpse of a total
stranger. The draper, contrariwise, sports a countenance
of perennial delight. The compliant cordiality with
which he offers a high cane chair of minute seating
capacity to the weighty female who desires to purchase a
reel of cotton or a packet of pins; the blushless be-
nignity with which he shows her, later, to the underwear
department; his massive disapproval in the face of giggles
or other attempts at flippancy on the part of underlings,
command my admiration without arousing in me a spirit
of emulation. His “This way, Madam,” “The very
latest from ’ome,” and so forth, has a family resemblance
to the planting expert’s trade talk, viz. “ Will you
have an oak or a weatherboard?” “ Brass or nickel
handles?” “ To-morrow, then, at 3 o'clock,” and the like.
Such parley strikes me as banal. The draper develops
large, flat, plantigrade feet inevitably ; so does the under-
taker. This arises from the Agag-like habit of progres-
sion. Agag was the Amalekite who ambled on eggs.
Behind the draper’s air of unnatural gaiety and good-
fellowship lurks that furtive something which speaks the
secret spiff purveyor. The buryer is also slinking and
unlike the generality of mankind, as becomes one who
faces horrors daily and would not shrink from eating his
lunch off a coffin in the way of business. I know that the
vending of body-coverings (be the same of wood or of
rag) is a lucrative game ; yet nothing more reconciles me
to my own stone-broke condition than consideration
of the mental, moral and physical effect frame-concealing
has on him who goes in for it.
The man whose conscience begins to haunt him
20 years after the crime crops up every now and then in
a pathetic letter to the Treasurer or the Railway Com-
missioner. The letter is handed to the daily press, and
is generally something like this : A
“ Dear Sir.—When I was a voung and thoughtless child
I owned a small dog, of which I was very fond. One
year, when the dog-man called, I had not the money to
pay for it, and, acting on a sudden impulse, I concealed
the animal in the hot-water boiler, and told the official I
had no dog. I thus evaded paying the tax, but the
memory of my dishonest action has haunted me through
the years, and embittered my life. I now enclose to you-
the sum of 55., and trust, now that 1 have made full
restitution, that my peace of mind will return to me.
Yours humbly, J. Smith.”
The restitution seldom amounts to more than five orten.
shillings. The gentleman who smuggles opium in a large
wav, or the importer who “ does in ” the Customs for
some thousands, never seems to repent. He has to be
caught by the coat-tails and dragged into court by the
hair at the back of his head.
MELBOURNE MEMORIES.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
The unsightly and insanitary barrows on Prince s>
Bridge . . . are an eyesore to all who pass that way.
Considering it is the principal approach from the
city to Government House, ways and means should he
taken by the city authorities to have the nuisance
removed at once. —Melbourne Herald, 20/4/08.
Grey fish, long dead, and mossy fruit,
Grim, awful oysters of the past
Play purple tunes on perfume’s lute
And send the cit. side-stepping fast
To where the atmosphere is mute.
Red crayfish, age-old, and the ripe,
Rich flathead send their scents along
To join the incense of the pipe
Of Barrer Bill, who, right or wrong,
Shines pippins with a crimson wipe.
Blue cod, a long way off the ice,
A story of snide profits tell —
A tale of details not too nice !
Our Guv’nors notice it! This smell
Must be suppressed at any price !
Green are the far, fair English fields
To which (too soon !) our Guv’nors go,
And we should see that prudence shields
From them the smells they must not know
Those smells the Bridge of Barrers yields.
Look to it, lest these men of parts
Recall mere odors devilish
When some chance tale of Melbourne starts
Their thoughts on us—of strident fish,
Of half-forgotten oyster-carts!
VIC.
FRANK HENTY.


Two Hundred and
Reasons.
There are two hundred and sixteen reasons why you, if you are about
to purchase a piano, should obtain a Beale Piano.
Look at the four illustrations herewith. Start with No. 1.
You note those rows of holes in the wooden wrest-plank of the imported
Piano. When the piano is “ strung ” each one of those holes is filled
by a “ wrest-pin ” —a piece of steel shaped not unlike a blunt nail
without a head.
Each pin is a quarter of an inch in diameter. Each is driven into its
hole with a hammer—it must fit tightly, so as to resist the pulling-
round of the wire which is wound round it.
Each pin is therefore like a wedge in the wrest-plank, and when the wire
(or “ string ”) is tightened up by turning the pin round, and thus
coiling the wire upon it, each pin becomes in addition a lever , because
of the pull of the wire.
There are two hundred and sixteen of these pins. The total strain
exercised upon them by all the wires when at concert pitch
amounts to nearly thirty tons.
Remember that they are only held in a wooden plank.
No one can suppose that a single wooden plank, driven full of wedges,
which are also levers, can for long withstand such a strain.
Especially in a climate like the Australian.
Whence—see Figure 2. Now compare Figure 2 with Figures 3 and 4.
There is the whole story.
In the Beale Pianos those two hundred and sixteen pins are contained
entirely in solid iron.
+
They will v/ithstand indefinitely any strain that may be put upon them.
