SPORTING
NOTIONS
TheN.S.W. Rugby League scored a great ad-
vertisement on Saturday last in the match against the
so-called All Blacks. A fast, open game, full of reckless
dash, with bursts of splendid play, made a spectacle that
drove over 11,000 people wild with excitement. And the
fact that the local team was the winning one did the new
form of Rugby no harm. This was evidently a game that
the Walers took to as Scotchmen take to whisky. There-
fore it appealed to the popular fancy. All the same, the
Maorilanders were obviously out of condition, and some
of their best men had remained behind in Britain, added
to which they had the mighty Messenger against
them. And what a player the said Messenger is!
Occasionally some football scribe, fearful of being thought
a hero worshipper, timidly mentions that Messenger
*• was out of his place.” As a matter of fact the deadli-
ness of Messenger’s play is that he is for ever in his place,
the particular point where danger threatens, or where
the charging forward will, a hundred to one, sling the ball
when rushed. Wherever the opposition has left an
opening, or where it is most easily broken through, there
will Messenger be found. And when the tide of battle
turns, and the enemy are piling weight, to burst through
by sheer brute strength, Messenger will appear suddenly,
like the ghost at the banquet or the sins of youth, to
snatch the leather, and play some desperate trick with
which to turn what seemed to be certain defeat into an
attack on the enemy’s line.
H. R. Wright led the Maorilanders, and S. A.
Henessy, of South Sydney, commanded the Blues. Mes-
senger played, as usual, in the three-quarter line. The
N.S.W. captain kicked off, and some fine long range
shooting followed, till Messenger, intercepting, charged
into Maoriland territory, right through the Black for-
wards. A slip on the greasy ground brought the charge
to an inglorious finish, and the Maordanders came down
on the Cornstalk line with a splendid rush. Only
smart forcing prevented a score. Coming , agaitj,-
there was a wild scramble on the Blue ,lin'£,;
out of which the Blacks managed to feed their,
three-quarter, Barber, . who sent it over the bah.
First blood to Maoriland. Messenger led a. charge;
to the Maoriland line, but the Blacks raced it back to
half-way, where a penalty kick gave Messenger his first
chance. It*was an impossible kick, 10yds. beyond half-
way, and the ball was intercepted. But it was merely a
check, for within five minutes Messenger had equalised
the points, which stood two all. Another Blue charge
ended in Stuntz scoring under the posts, and Messenger
made the score seven-to-two. The audience began to
show signs of insanity just here. Both teams were play-
ing a sparkling game, the interchanges being clean and
remarkably swift, and Maoriland added another two
points. The performance was followed almost imme-
diately by another Blue charge which added five points,
making the game 12 to four. Maorilanders could not
play fair ; and they struck trouble with a referee who
knew his business thoroughly and did his duty grimly.
The Maorilander is a bad loser, judging by the footballers
he sends over to Australia ; and it was now apparent that
unless the home team collapsed, the All Blacks were in
for a bad defeat. At half-time tbe Blues w’ere leading by
14 to seven.
The second half found the Maorilanders fighting
a losing battle. Messenger kept up an incessant bom-
bardment of the goal from positions that would have been
regarded as impossible by anyone else, and the score
climbed steadily. The All Blacks had lost the game be-
yond all hope of recovery, and now they lost their man-
ners and their tempers, till in the agony of defeat they
kicked opponents on the ground and swung rights and
lefts at the Blues who tackled them when charging. Two
of their number were sent off the ground, and the dis-
reputable blackguardism did not improve their position.
Messenger’s bombardment continued, kicks from half-
way and from all sorts of impossible angles missing
the bar by inches, while the Blue forwards
went charging through right to the goal-line,
threatening every moment to increase the already
overwhelming score. Time was running out, and the
majority of the crushed Maorilanders were praying for
the whistle, but some indomitable spirit shouted for
one more charge, and they pulled themselves together,
even as Totila and his Goths pulled themselves together
at Taginae, for the last rush. It came so suddenly and un-
expectedly, and was so fiercely maintained, that the Blue
forwards were overwhelmed, the backs were scattered,
aifd then the whole mass went roaring down the field
together, over the N.S.W. line, where the Maorilanders
scored in the corner, but failed to add the points. It was
a great effort, but quite useless, and the Maorilanders left
the field beaten by 18 points to 10.
After the League's fast and hilarious display at
Sydney Agricultural Ground, the Union’s competition fell
rather flat. Glebe, after its two successive drubbings,
found a welcome respite in the bye ; University stamped
Manly flat ; while Eastern Suburbs continued its trium-
phal progress by wiping out North Sydney to the tune of
11 to three. South Sydney downed Sydney District by 14
to three in a wild rough and tumble at Sydney Cricket
Ground ; and Newtown beat St. George by the same figures.
Western Suburbs and Balmain scored a savage draw, with
three points each. The result was that University,
Eastern Suburbs and South Sydney are neck-and-neck in
the first line, with six competition points each, all
three having a so-far unbeaten record. -Sydney and New-
town are the second line, with four points each. Bal-
main is a line all by itself, with three. Glebe, >North Sydney
and St. George are together, with two each ;and Western
Suburbs is alone, with one. Manly, at the bottom of the
list, has scored nothing yet; but still it has had the benefit
of the fresh air and exercise.
The kicking of Mandible, the Sydney half-
back, by a South Sydney forward is a matter that should
be inquired into. Many spectators declare that it was
deliberate, and, if so, the offender should be flung out of
the game for life. If it were accident, the kicker’s char-
acter should be cleared by an impartial investigation.
» * * *
The Sydney division of the Australian Leaguers
got Its competition started on Saturday last, notwith-
standing dearth of grounds, with four first-grade matches.
In spite of lack of condition, play was interesting, and the
opening of the season was marked, as in the case of the
Union, by the downfall of the premier club (Sydney), which
was soundly walloped by Redfern (46 to 32), after a game in
which the Syrian suburb literally ran over the champions.
East Sydney crushed North Shore with 62 points to seven,
which, bad as it was, was a mild affair to the awful doing
Newtown gave Paddington, in which the figures were 194
to seven. The best match of the day was that between
Y.M.C.A. and Balmain, in which muscular Christianity
downed waterside Paganism by Si to 31.
• * * »
Altogether, Saturday was a great football day
in Sydney ; for, besides All Blacks, and Union, and
League play, the British Association also opened its com-
petition with four matches. In this case, the premier
club, Pyrmont, drew the bye, and the Naval crowd,
officially known as Garden Island, astonished the natives
—and "itself most of all—by romping over Northern
Suburbs with nine goals to nil. It is said that Garden
Island has been dumb with amazement ever since. Bal-
main beat Rozelle by three to nil, but the score gives no
indication of how very superior the Balmain crowd was.
It ought to have won by double the figures. Sydney
downed Ultimo by four to nil, but the game did not ap-
pear to be the gallop-over that Balmain put up. Glebe
and Western Suburbs were an example of the meeting of
the dead equal, and the two teams embraced one another
and reeled helplessly about the middle of the field, both
crowds having the luck to boot one into the net.
The irruption of the Rugby League has resulted
in the sudden discovery by the Union that the Australian
and Soccer crowds are its long-lost brothers, and the
Union, which has a heavy paw on most of the best football
grounds in and around Sydney, is going to help its little
brothers with playing accommodation. This sudden
blossoming of brotherly love is quite touching.
* * * *
In nice, cool weather, but on grounds that the
months of drought had left terribly hard, the Vic. League
clubs opened business for the season. A pretty crowd,
that must have gladdened the hearts of the treasurers
even as a fat oyster rejoices an epicure, crushed into the
Carlton ground to see if "Saints” could repeat last
season’s feat of demolishing the Premiers in one sudden
act. They made a strenuous effort, and at half-time they
were but a couple of points to the bad —2.5 to 2.3
but in the third quarter the Northerners used the
wind to the full, and piled up 31 more points
while Saints were standing still, flabbergasted at
the rout. The Holy Ones had the wind for the
fourth term, but want of condition told its tale, and
though Carlton was hard pressed it managed to stop its
opponents from adding more than seven points—winning
by 48 to 22. Carlton played three new men and Saints
four, but among these latter was Mick Grace, now their
trainer, and heretofore a shining light among Carlton’s
stars. This circumstance, among others, added con-
siderable vim to the encounter. The clashing of South
and Fitzroy also drew a huge crowd. These ancient
foes generally promise a willing game. For once
they gave each other the soft answer that turneth away
stoush, but the game was exciting enough nevertheless.
The languishing ’Roys played with such combination
that they were never more than three points behind
“ Skinner’s hirelings ” till the middle of the last quarter,
when the hirelings notched a lucky goal, and thereafter
took the best of care that ’Roy shouldn’t score. Latter
played six new men, mostly juniors. South had four
only, but as South is understood to have scoured the
Commonwealth for talent, and to have retained players
from Broken Hill, Sydney, Rutherglen, and Laun-
ceston, besides a dozen juniors, it is probably
keeping back its best forces in order to ad-
minister shocks later on. Oollingwood has also been
searching the country for desirable acquisitions. It took
Six of them down to Geelong to teach Slumbering Hollow
the rudiments of football, but was itself handed a lesson
Iti won’t forget in a hurry. It returned home in the
dumps, with a score of 12 goals 13 behinds against five
goals eight behinds to its discredit. Geelong, which has
recruited its ranks, as usual, from the local Grammar
School and College, is understood to have indulged
heavily in gingerbeer over its victory. If it can
manage to keep its head to the normal size and
retain the fine players it seems to possess, it may
yet regain the glories of a generation ago, when
it won the premiership seven times in nine
years. Of the two new League clubs, one made a credit-
able first appearance and the other made a blot. Rich-
mond beat Melbourne by 8.14 to 7.9—a result chiefly due
to the svstem inculcated by its ex-Collingwood captain
and ditto coach ; and ’Varsity went under to Essendon by
14.11 to 3.11. Seeing that Essendon was at the bottom of
the League list last year, this awful score looks anything
but promising; but ’Varsity nevertheless has the two
requisites of a hefty team—good ’bailers and unselfish
players. What it chiefly needs is practice, and plenty
of it.
•*■**-*
The Vic. Football Association continued its
more or less chequered career. Williamstown passed a
mild “ doing ” to Preston by 60 to 45 ; Footscrav stressed
its superiority over North by 54 to 20 ; and Brighton im-
pressed on Northcote that though both teams were first-
year associates, in a manner of speaking, they, the sea-
sides, were twice as lusty and scientific as the Northern-
ers, as proved by the scores—l2’B to 5'9. The lament-
able “Ports” journeyed to Essendon, whither
a large crowd followed them, expecting gore—
it was Essendon’s complaint that caused the dis-
qualification of Ports’ ground last season—but the
overwhelming attendance of Essendonians and the im-
mense superiority of that team’s play speedily drove Ports’
and Port barrackers’ hearts into their boots, and they
retired as depressed as a Foreign-trader when he views a
new factory, pondering over a score of 142 points to 46.
The prettiest game bf the day was wasted on Toorak,
where Prahran and Brunswick discussed matters. There is
a trifle of brotherly love between these teams, and as both
boast ex-League piayers and follow their methods, their
games are generally worth watching. Prahran went off
with a strong lead and held it to the third quarter. Then
Brunswick bogged in and piled up single points whole-
sale, finally coming out on top by 5.23 to 5.13. It cer-
tainly deserved to lose for its vile kicking, but Prahran
was almost as bad. One or two minor casualties moved
the sympathies of spectators during the afternoon, and
a couple of neat little mills enlivened the Essendon-
’Varsit.v and Richmond-Melbourne arguments ; but other-
wise all was sweet peace and holy calm.
Mr. Samuel Hordern’s magnificent new motor
yacht, Bronzewing, said to be the largest boat of its kind
in the world, was launched at Balmain (Sydney) on Satur-
day morning, Mr. S. Hordern, jun., representing his
father, who is in England. As mentioned in this column
some months ago, the new boat is the Australian
masterpiece up to date. Length over all is 110 ft.,
with a beam of I7£ft., and a depth of Bft. lOin.
But the size is nothing; the triumph lies in
the exquisite craftsmanship that has been lavished
on the glorious thing inside and out. Australasian woods
and Australian copper and other metals have been
moulded and graved most artistically. Built by Messrs.
Morrison and Sinclair from the design of well-known
naval architect Mr. Walter Reeks, whose orders were to
produce “ the best,” the Bronzewing ought to be
acquired by the Government and stored in a glass
case as a credit to its native land. It represents
about the first real opportunity the Australian craftsman
has had, and he has risen to the occasion in a marvellous
manner. Even the heavy guii-metal propeller and rudder
and the silky-oak case of the piano were made in Mr.
Hordern’s own factory. The only foreign products in the
yacht are to be found in the engine room, yet but for
foreign patent rights and a most inefficient Australian
patent law, it is safe to say that the work there would
also have been done in Australia. There are three in-
dependent engines, of Thorneycroft make, each develop-
ing 108 h.p., and capable of driving the Bronzewing at a
speed of about 15 knots. The masts are hinged, so that
the yacht can explore rivers regardless of bridged, and the
craft is so constructed that to sink it is almost impossible.
The Motor Boat Club of the U.S. has accepted
the challenge of the English Wolseley-Siddeley boat to
race for the British International Cup, which was won
by the Dixie last year. The challenger is already afloat,
and making remarkable speed runs. Considering that its
engines are a pair of 200h.p. affairs, the challenger cer-
tainly ought to walk some, as the ’Murkans say. The
Yanks are building a special boat to defend the Cup, hut
it will be driven by engines of only half the power of the
challenger. In the meantime the English boat goes to
Monaco to try conclusions with a 500h.p. Italian, and with
390h.p. and 480h.p. flyers from France. British papers
are confident that the Wolseley-Siddeley will down them
all easily enough.
» * * *
The Round-the-World motorists are now, pre-
sumably, nearing Vladivostock; The journey across the
United States proved that any idea of running up the
glacier coast of British Columbia and through Alaska was
sheer madness. One competitor got so disgusted that he .
shoved his car on board a train, and arrived to find him-
self disqualified. The Yankee car, the Thomas, arrived,
first in ’Frisco, but, having to vvait for a steamer, was ex-
pected to be overhauled by the Zust, De Dion and
Protos cars. The 900 miles gained by the Yankee
car was said to have been made up through
the unfair assistance given by the farmers, who cut their
fences to let it take short cuts, and set their hands to
shovel heaps of snow in the track taken by the foreign
cars. The Zust car men had a rough time, travelling for
five days between Wyoming and Utah on only four meals,
presumably because they were refused food for love or
money. It was only a 200-mile stretch too, but the
Yankee farmers appear to have managed their delay work
very well. A mighty poor idea of sport have some of the
people in the Uncle Sam country.
* * * *
Inter-State rowing and sculling championships
occur on the Yarra next Saturday afternoon. All the
States will be represented, including Tassy, which is dead
nuts on paddling two crews. This minute piece of earth
at the southern end of the continent should be forcibly
sat upon, and eight of its reps, sent home, or somewhere.
The benefit of any doubt that may •exist on the result
should be given the Vies.; they are lifting their own
water, and doing it well with a stroke who stands out head
and shoulders —Jim Donald. The Cabbage Staters are
principally “ Parks ” —four of them having seats.
* * * *
At time of writing there are two Tasmanian
crews in Melbourne to take part in the inter-State Eight-
Oar contest on Saturday. The general feeling among the
crews and delegates is that the two Tasmanian teams
should settle the matter by rowing off a day or so before
the race, the honor of representing Appleland to goto the
winning crew. And as this idea is the only solution of a
very knotty question, it will probably have been acted
upon by the time this appears.
* * * * •
No less than 16 hockey teams were in the field
in and around Sydney on Saturday last, and the press of
applicants is so great that even at this late hour it is
possible that more teams will be formed. The Corinthians
were represented by the First, A and B teams. The First
downed the Navy by five goals to four ; the A team went
down to the Rovers by seven to six ; and the B team was
beaten by the Bandits by six to one. Tfie Bandits second
team was downed by University 11. by three to one.
University I. put in a pleasant afternoon by beating
the Sydtiean I. by five to one. The game of the
afternoon, however, was that between Barbarians and
Pilgrim first teams. Excellent play and splendid com-
bination were shown by both teams, but the Barbarians,
presumably through living on raw meat and drinking
only water in their wild state, were in better condition
than the Pilgrims, whose walking exercise has not yet
got them quite hardened. The Barbarians won by four
goals to one. As an example of sound defensive work the
Pilgrims’ game was excellent. On the other hand, the
passing of the Barbarians during their attacks was very
pretty indeed, and completely eclipsed the form shown
by the Corinthians the previous Saturday, which was also
very high class.
* * * * %
As anticipated, Tom Burrows has again broken
out into a new record, and has swung two hunks of
limber in Wellington (M.L.) for 62hr. 2min. Another
unwise person accompanied him most of the time on
the piano, and broke his own record by playing con-
tinuously for 38hr. 2min., and beating his former time
by 2hr. 2min. Jack Griffiths, of Cobar (N.S.W.), who
recently twirled them for 62 hours neat, will now pro-
bably have'another shot, and twirl them some more, and
bring about yet another dreary display by holding out
for 62hr. 2m. lOsec. Why not get properly going and
swing for 78 hours, and then unless all is blue, keep
going steadily for a few hours longer, just to scare
Burrows and other dreary circumstances right off?
Fortunately there doesn’t seem to be a second record
piano fiend at large, and that infliction is likely to rest on
its laurels.
* * * *
S. A. Amateur Athletic Association has held its
second championship meeting. The lack of public
support was cruel. Adelaide, so prominent in all games,
has never taken up the idea of running—except short
“ Sheffields,” for cash—and the amateur body is only
kept alive by a small band of enthusiasts, at their own
expense. (And on them the parent Australasian body
has descended with the demand of a£s fine for not being
represented at the championships in Hobart!) The S.A.
winners were, as usual, mostly from the ’Varsity. Moyes
did 21ft. and sft. Bin. (long and high jumps respectively),
and Angwin covered the hundred in lOsec. (all three
watches agreed ; strong wind behind the runners). The
veteran president—“ veteran ” from the sporting juvenile
outlook—A. J. Roberts, of the Stock Exchange, ran third
in a mile handicap, won by his own son. Thus does Dad
get left when the younger generation arrives.
* * • *
Maoriland horse-owner, Mr. G. G. Stead, who
died last week, was a turfman of credit and renown for
over 30 years. In him excellent judgment and splendid
luck combined to make the perfect racing career. As a
breeder, he stands on a plane of his own, and almost
every important race in Maoriland, and many in Sydney,
at one time or other, fell to horses bred by him. He did
not race in Victoria; something he considered an
injustice in his early days caused him to register a
vow that his colors should never again be seen on a
course controlled by the V.R.C. And that vow’ he
kept,' with the result that the Victorians lost
the pleasure of seeing some of the finest gallopers bred in
the southern hemisphere. However, they are still alive
and apparently doing fairly well. One of the remarkable
epochs in racing history was the Stead scoop at Rand-
wick, at the 1905 Spring meeting, when he won eight
races in nine starts, his appropriations being the A.J.C.
Derby, Spring Maiden Stakes, Wycombe Stakes, Mem-
bers’ Handicap, New Stakes, Grantham Stakes, Squatters’
Handicap, and Randwick Plate. They represented £3570
in prize-money, and great was the mana of G. G. Stead.
* * * *
City Tatt.’s day at Randwick, on Saturday last,
completed the cleaning out of the few punters whom the
A.J.C. Autumn meeting had left over. Only in one
instance did a favorite score, when the imported Sir
Aymer got home in the Club Welter. And even then it
is'doubtful if Christian Israel got left, as a despairing
public had piled much money on Camera, the second horse,
and on Abandoned, who was unplaced. The notable
happening was the coming of Comely in the City Tatt.’s
Handicap. This mare had been backed considerably, but
without success, during the last 12months, and on Saturday
was generally written off as a hopeless failure. Then she
won easily, after being backed by those in the know from
15 down, to eights. Shimose, in the Novice Handicap,
was another that spread slaughter among the punters.
Altogether it is time the public awoke to the fact that
gentlemen sometimes run horses, and wise men lay the
odds on them, and fools back theni, and the one animal
that is always unplaced is The Public. He has been run
ning last since the days of the Roman chariot races.
W. Brown, up on Waitapu at Fielding (M.L.),
last week, has been riding racehorses for over a quarter of a
century. His mount (Louie) appropriated the Hawkes
Bay Cup in 1882, and eight years ago Brown rode the
winner (Malatua) of the Great Easter Handicap. He also
won Canterbury Derby and Cup on Seahorse.
* * * *
The.hero of the A.J.C. autumn meeting was
undoubtedly Tartan, but his rattling performances did
not benefit his owner, Mr. J. Macken, who was in a re-
cent number of this paper inadvertently referred to as a
trainer. Mr. Macken is still on the diamond ridge occu.
pied by owners. So hopeless did Mr. Macken believe
Tartan’s chances were, on the word of his vets.,
that he did not back him for the Autumn Stakes. Then
after he had, with 9st. 31b., beaten the great Poseidon
with 9st. up, and cutdo'wn Dyed Garment* and Peru(9st.
and Bst. 4lb. respectively) in a few lengths, and won by
nearly three lengths in one of the most phenomenal
bursts of speed ever seen on a wet course at
Randwick, his owner hied him to the city and
backed him enthusiastically for the Sydney Cup. Andthe
grand old horse made a great effort. With 9st, 91b. up,
he chased Dyed Garments with only 7st. 41b. on board
in an amazing manner. While Dyed Garments flew from
third to first, Tartan flew from seventh to second. In
the A.J.C. Plate, the following Saturday, he ran second—
and shot his bolt. Although/the bandage may'have
appeared unnecessary, on the hbrse’s performance, it was
there by order of the vets.

.7* . :•
Will Tommy Burns take on Jack Johnson, who
arrived in England in quest of the gore-grail last week ?
The odds seem against it. To begin with, Burns draws
the color-line, and in so doing has an immense section of
sporting America in sympathy with him. Johnson, more-
over, is the only fighter on earth whom the' experienced
fight fans of the U.S.A. tip as the present champion’s
master. And Burns is a business man. The heavy,
weight championship is a big financial asset, and he
isn’t the sort to monkey with it unless the odds
appear to be his way. And even if he does
decide to shelve his prejudice against Johnson’s
hue, and at the same time to risk his present
status, there is a likelihood of negotiations falling through.
Burns “ manages ” for himself and does his own business
well and hard. His stake and gate terms are the hottest
hitherto set up by any member of the fighting world.
Mr. Johnson, if he wants his match, will have to be satis-
fled with about a 10 per cent, dividend ; 80 per cent, will
go to Burns for a certainty, win or lose. He is a pug
whose matches are the only known ones in the history of
sport wherein it is possible to pick unerringly any num-
ber of days beforehand the financial winner (viz., Burns)
and the “ also rans ” (to wit, his opponent, his would-be
exploiters, the cinematographers, etc.).
* # * *
George Dawson, old-time Australian pugilist of
the second class, and at present boxing instructor to the
Chicago Athletic Association, has been handing the N.Y.
World (25/3/’08) his views on what he calls “ the deca-
dence of Australian boxing.” George ascribes the
“luxuriance of the lemon crop in the Antipodes” en-
tirely to the “emigration of proper boxing-instructors.”
In George’s' view', the fact that all the fighters of
his (George’s) calibre have gone to the U.S.A.
has ruined stoush in the Commonwealth utterly.
There is positively no one left to teach the younger
generation how to bash its fellow-man. It is a quaint
bit of egotism. “Larry” Foley, who is still teach-
ing in these parts, was, in his fighting days, classes
ahead of the egregious Dawson ; while, as an instructor,
it is doubtful if there is anyone either in England or the
States to touch him. The London National Sporting
Club, which pays its officials about three times better
wages than does any similar concern outside N’York,
angled eagerly for Laurence t’other day. Of Mister Daw-
son it had presumably never heard. The fact is the great
stoush-dispenser is about as rare as the great tenor or
soprano. Australia has had more than its share of front-
rank fighting men in the last 25 years. In the nature of
things, and by all the laws of averages, it can’t expect a
fresh crop of pugilistic marvels yet awhile, no matter how
many instructors it supports. Meanwhile it is doing a
little at raising a crop of singers and inventors.
DIABOLO.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
How do you perch the bobbin on tbe string
Before you send it skyward with a jerk?
How the diabolo do'you get the thing
To work ?
Hast night I practised for an hour or so,
Until a difference of opinion led
To the diabolo skulking in below
The bed.
I caught it twice—once off my looking-glass,
And once it got entangled with my ear ;
We’re needing three new mantles on our gas-
Alier.
I could admire the creature’s graceful waltz,
Did it reverse with less untidy glee ;
I can’t forgive its cowardly assaults
On me.
Are not its fascinations mostly sham V
Do its exponents really find the fun
Worth, all the Joss of temper and the datn-
Age done ?
Next year its name will be anathema ;
The present cry will in a month or so
Be changed to “ To the Devil with dia-
8010 ! ”

AOOOUNTS for the Half-year to 14th October, 1907. Presented to the Proprietors at the Annual General
Meeting, held on Thursday, 26th March, 1908.
REPORT.
THE Directors submit to the Proprietors the Balance-sheet as at 14th October last, with the Profit Account for the
a^r ar i?°u t r t ate ‘ After providing- for rebate on bills current, for British and Colonial rates and taxes,
a.na for all bad and doubtful debts, the net profit for the half-year, including some recoveries from old accounts,
amounted to £205,749 15s. 4d.
S o.? mus t ; be added £17,324 19s. lid. brought forward from the previous half-year, making a total of
o,ut4 15s. 3d., out of which the Directors have declared a Dividend for the half-year at the rate of 12 per cent, per

tv, t tv. 6 Erectors propose to recommend at an Extraordinary General Meeting, to be summoned as soon as possible,
inat tne present Deed of Settlement, which, in many of its provisions, is quite out of date, should be in effect repealed
anu a new Deed of Settlement, more in accordance with modern practice, adopted. In order to carry this out in a
mple manner, it is advisable that Clause 29 of the present Deed should first be amended, as proposed in the

wnich, in the opinion of the Directors, would be a needless expense.

About the most ghastly thing in criminal
neglect of the public good to date is the manufac-
ture of cheap “ glass ” eyes from celluloid, which is
so inflammable as to be almost explosive. The
man with a celluloid artificial eye would only have
to encounter a live spark to suffer an agonising
death. Minister for Customs Chapman won’t let
the fiendish things come into Australia, but pro-
bably the cantankerous State Premiers will take
legal proceedings, on the plea that Chapman is
doing a State Wrong, the same as in the corn-sack
ease.
Maoriland has a special reformatory for
young women who show an early tendency to
follow in the footsteps of Lais and Lady
Hamilton. Recently a commissioner was ap-
pointed to inquire into the methods of the place,
and, judging by his report, it is a strenuous em-
porium. In addition to labor there is a wise and
careful discipline. The unruly female, whatever
her age, is laid face downward on a bed, clad
only in a nightgown, and has instruction poured
upon her with a strap. Inmates who drop over
the garden wall are recaptured by the aid of the
police, and when brought back have their hair
shorn as a sign and a punishment. So when any-
one in M'aoriland is accosted by a female with her
hair about the same length as that of Bill Sikes,
he knows tfyat she is an escapee from Te Oranga
Home. The bnly drawback about the system is
that hair grbws again, but if the ladies were
branded they could be identified for the rest of
their lives. The commissioner reports that he
finds the treatment has a most humanising effect
on the girls.

Hand Became Swollen and Very Red
and Painful—Arm Gradually Af-
fected Up to Elbow —Impossible

Many Remedies Fail.
CURED BY TWO SETS
OF CUTICURA REMEDIES
“My trouble started with swelling in
my right hand, which became red and
very painful and irritating. This swell-
ing spread gradually up.the arm until it
reached the elbow. It is impossible for
me to give any idea of the agony I went
through during the three years that
this eczema continued. I tried first
one remedy and then another, only too
glad to test anything that my friends
suggested. Nothing seemed to be of
the least good. I went to a doctor for
four weeks in the provinces, where I was
living at the time, and the treatment
he put me under was as ineffectual as
the rest. I was almost in despair. One
day I happened to catch sight of an
advertisement of Cuticura Remedies,
and there I read of a case of eczema
having been cured by the use of Cuti-
cura Soap, Cuticura Ointment, and
Cuticura Resolvent Pills. The case
was so similar to my own that I thought
I would at least make one more try to
get my arm right. I bought the whole
treatment of the chemist, and I perse-
vered with it for a week. By that
time the improvement was apparent to
any one, and the relief I felt myself was
great. I should not have believed it
possible for any remedy to afford such
comfort in so short a time. My ex-
perience with the other remedies for
eczema —and I should think I tried
them all enables me to appreciate
Cuticura to the full. By the doctor’s
orders I was keeping my arm in a sling
for many months, but the sling was soon
dispensed with when I started with
Cuticura. I persevered with Cuticura
Soap, Ointment, and Pills for about a
couple of months, using in all two
complete treatments, and I can tell
you candidly and heartily that I was
cured. I have occasion to have great
faith in Cuticura in skin ailments, not
only because of the great good it has
done me, but because of the benefit I
know has accrued from its use in other
cases. Mr. T. S. Gillard, 3, Hetley Road,

THE PROOF.
(BY A SPECIAL REPORTER.)
With a view to bringing before the public a
matter of considerable importance, a reporter
rapped at 114 Glebe Road, Glebe, where Mrs.
Hannah Wells resides. The lady appeared in
response, and upon the pressman’s mission being
explained, she said :
“ Certainly. Nothing would give me more plea-
sure than to relate the story of my great achieve-
ment of four years ago, especially as it will be so
serviceable to many in want of guidance. You
see it was after fruitlessly trying several home
remedies that I saw about Clements Tonic in the
papers, and made up my mind to find out what it
was like for myself, as I was very ill, and was
willing to take anything so long as I got better.
I was as
WEAK AND MISERABLE AS ANY
WOMAN COULD BE,
and had such a sallow complexion, too, that to
look at me you would have thought I had the
jaundice. My chest used to cause me no end of
bother, for often I felt that something was there,
blocking my breath, besides having heavy, op-
pressive pains as well. I hadn’t any inclination
to eat.
NO APPETITE AT ALL,
so what could I expect but to get thin and deli
cate looking, which I assuredly did. From the
time I arose from bed of a morning till I returned
again in the evening I was as languid and indo
lent as could be. When I tried to sleep at night
I could not, and oh ! it was so wretched being
there hour after hour waiting for the daylight,
and when it came, what did it bring? Cny
another day of sorrow. My sight got bad, an
my eyes felt as heavy as lead ; the taste in my
mouth was awfully bitter, and the worst of it was
that my head was always aching.
“Indeed, I don’t know what I hadn’t got to
make life a burden, and if I hadn't taken Clements
Tonic when I did I am afraid to think what won
have happened, for I was going down hill fast.
“ Had your nerves given way at all ?
“Oh ! I was fearfully nervous, scarcely able to

ore, though, and my friends too, was the m

The Poison Cart and Bird Life.
It is about time N.S.W. settled down to seriously
consider the advisability of abolishing the rab-
biter’s poison cart. Driving through the country
between Cotter Creek and Queanbeyan recently a
Bulletin man was struck by the total absence of
birds. The poison cart had wiped them out.
Magpie, starling, crow and cockatoo had all dis-
appeared. The abolition of the crow is not an
unmixed evil, but the other birds, especially the
worm and insect eaters, are a serious loss. The
poison cart was not intended to abolish the birds,
of course, but the rabbits. But, judging by
appearances, Bunny never eats the stuff, as
writer saw millions of rabbits. Part of the
timbered country was subdivided by log
fences, and every log fence was a warren
towards which the rabbits made in scores when
the noise of the sulky was first heard. They are
not timid, and they rarely sought shelter, but
sat up within easy reach of home and waited
to see what would happen. Of course, it
is just possible that the poison does kill off
some of them, but they are so few compared to
what are left that they do not count in the balance.
In fact, it seemed an empty land. Human beings
were met but rarely, and the only signs of life
were the rabbits and the grasshoppers, which
swept away in clouds when disturbed. And,
while they last, grasshoppers are said to eat as
much grass as rabbits. Sometimes, when they are
numerous, they eat more and make a clean sweep.
Several observant country residents informed the
writer that, in districts where the poison cart is
rife, heavy rainfalls are usually followed by a
number of mysterious deaths, which are commonly
described as “stomach trouble.” If a downpour
of rain occurs shortly after the laying of the
poison the moisture preserves the poison and also
washes it into the human drinking supplies.
Then nice, respectable, old identities, who
never previously had a day’s illness in their
lives, quietly turn up their toes and depart
to that bourne which is six feet by three. That
the poison cart is not a successful method of fight-
ing the rabbit is proved conclusively by the
myriads of him that are still going strong. But
that it is a great eradicator of bird life is evidenced
by the birdless state of the districts in which it
has been in vogue any length of time. And that it
is a successful remover of the late well-known and
widely-respected townsman and settler is more
than reasonably suspected. It is up to N.S.W.
to consider the advisability of abolishing the
poison cart by Act of Parliament.
+
The other day a trades union represented by Drs.
MacLaurin, M.L.C., Sydney Jones, Mackkllar,
M.L.C., and others of great tonnage waited on
the N.S.W. Chief Secretary, and asked for a few
changes in the law. First, these persons wanted to
shut out from their union any doctor who belongs to
any country that won’t reciprocate ; after that
they requested that no such shut-out non-unionist
“ shall be entitled to recover in any court of law
for any medical or surgical advice or attendance.”
Thereupon the Chief Secretary was very sym-
pathetic and said he would consult the Cabinet
about it; and the Cabinet which he will consult
sympathetically will be the same crowd which, the
other day, when the Arbitration Act was under
consideration, almost refused to recognise unions
at all—that is, unions of miners, and coal trimmers,
and other common persons. It is interesting to
picture the sort of earthquake that would
have happened in the office of that Chief Secre-
tary, and that Cabinet, if those unions of com-
mon persons had dared to suggest the non-pay-
ment of wages to non-unionists and the slamming
of the door of the courts in their face. It looks as
if the coal-lumpers need a knight or two. If they
only had a Sir Bill Brown and a Sir “Baldy”
Smithkins to lead their deputations, the chances
are that Wood and Wade would give them quite
a different sort of reception.

That Superior Person who is fond of declaring
that Australian industries make him feel tired,
and that he wouldn’t give a fig for the local
article, has received a shock from Bakjsr, curator
of the Sydney Tech. Museum. This Baker re-
cited to the Carriage Builders’ Convention, sitting
at Sydney at Easter-tide, the story of how he
laughs a horrid laugh when he sees in the big
furniture establishments the familiar placards,
“ Solid oak,” “ Real old English oak,” “Finest old
oak,” “Best imported oak,” and all sorts of
ancient silly oak, designedly displayed to lure the
Superior Person aforesaid into the belief that his
dining-room suite once graced in arboreal gran-
deur the demesne of some haughty but impe-
cunious British peer. Baker stated that what
this “ oak ” furniture is made out of is really the
blue fig tree of the N.S.W. North Coast. This fig
is a valuable timber, and can be so polished that
it looks exactly like oak, and it is called oak to
amuse the unwise bounders who insist on imported
goods. It is a dishonest proceeding, but when one
considers the childish ignorance and foolishness of
the people who buy “English oak furniture,” it
seems hardly worth while being honest with them.
The United Kingdom now spends about
£30,000,000 a year on imported timber, and a lot
of it is oak, because the country has hardly enough
oak of its own left to make a table with. So one
might almost as well buy a saddle made of the
alleged skin of the British wild elephant as a suite
of alleged British oak furniture.
The British meat producer has lately been look-
ing upon some realistic,illustrations of the beauties
of Private Enterprise. For instance :
At Glasgow, the centre of the American cattle trade in
the north, the carcases are despatched mostly to London,
a distance of 444 miles, the freight upon which is 455. per
ton, whereas Scotch feeders and killers, shipping from
Aberdeen, the great market for Scotch beef, have to pay
725. per ton in freight to London, though London is only
79 miles further from Aberdeen than is Glasgow. That
is to say, the American is charged less than ljd. per ton
per mile on British railways, while the British person is
charged just about lfd. per ton on British railways. The
railway companies have not only given this enormous
preference to the Americans, but they allow them to use
their sidings all over the country, where the Beef Trust
may, and does, send refrigerator cars containing American
beef for distribution to the retailers in small towns;
and these cars are allowed to remain in the sidings
free of rent, rates, and taxes until their local agent has
disposed of the beef, even should it take him a week or a
fortnight to do sp. The great hardship entailed upon
local killers must be evident to all.
In other words Private Enterprise, in the shape
of the British railway companies, is helping tbe
American Beef octopus to strangle the British
meat industry. There should be a fine large
moral in this for those fatuous Australian
“producers” who sometimes assert that “this
country would advance more rapidly if the rail-
ways were privately-owned.”


AND CHAMBERLAIN’S COUGH REMEDY.
WHAT IS INFLUENZA ?
ITS EFFECT.
MEDICAL MEN INTERVIEWED.
These questions are best answered by several
eminent medical men in published interviews.
The most important points emphasised by the
doctors are these :
Influenza is highly infectious.
Influenza stimulates other diseases.
Influenza has an extraordinary effect on the
mental function.
Influenza picks out the weak points in a person’s
constitution.
The victims of influenza are adults who perish
from pneumonia or bronchitis and the aged who
sink from heart exhaustion.
Children, while prone to the disease, enjoy com-
parative immunity from its complications and
dangers.
Influenza shows a decided tendency to relapses,
a feature to which the indirect fatality of the
disease is in a great measure due.
Alcoholic stimulants are not only unnecessary
but positively harmful.
An attack of influenza seems to render the indi-
vidual more liable to contract the disease from
future exposure.
The attack comes on with lightning-like speed.
A person in apparently perfect health is suddenly
overcome by a feeling of discomfort. He feels
chilly, or shakes with the rigor worthy of an ague.
His head aches. There are pains in his eyeballs,
and other symptoms characteristic of the disease
soon follow.
Quite Different.
The ordinary cough medicine may soothe the
throat, but it has no power to heal; recovery is
not complete, and a second attack is more liable
to follow. Chamberlain’s Cough Remedy is quite
different, because it not only soothes but also
heals, so that the affected parts are restored to a
healthy condition, and the danger of a future
attack is removed.

THREATS
OF INFLUENZA.
ITS DANGERS.
ENGLISH AND CONTINENTAL ADVICE.
The increased mortality last season from Lung
Affections following attacks of Influenza em-
phasises more than ever the necessity for nursing
an initiatory cold.
Especially since the advices from England, The
Continent, and America indicate that there has
been raging in all those countries an epidemic of
Influenza of the most virulent type. It is only
natural that Australia will in turn have the same.
We are always anxious concerning the public
health, have repeatedly called attention to the
dangers of undue exposure during inclement
weather, and now reiterate the injunction with
still more earnestness in view of present con-
ditions.
Most of the fatal cases of pneumonia at this
time of the year are due to the lack of timely
treatment of what appears to be a simple attack
of bronchial catarrh. The taking of the stitch in
time is at the bottom of all questions of preven-
tion of other more serious ones lying in wait for
solution.
It is well to take into account in such connection
that our winter months are generally considered
the most fatal for pneumonia, especially when
influenza is prevailing even in mild epidemic form.
Thus far we have escaped a visitation of a viru-
lent form of the latter malady, but there is
abundance of time and opportunity for the
development of a severe epidemic, with the usual
aftermath of alarming mortality.
The effective treatment of an ordinary “cold”
is a matter of a day or two against a possible
subsequent sickness for weeks.
The indications of a severe attack of influenza
are headache, chilliness, general muscular pains,
fever, sore throat, cough and systemic lassitude.
When these show themselves no time is to be
lost, and the patient should give up work at once
and promptly place himself under proper medical
care.
There is no danger whatever of any serious con-
sequences from an attack of influenza when Cham-
berlain’s Cough Remedy is used, as it counter-
acts any tendency towards pneumonia: This
fact has been abundantly proven in many thou-
sands of cases during the epidemics of the past
few years.
Can be Cured.
Commence at first symptoms to use Chamber-
lain’s Cough Remedy. Thousands have used this
Remedy during the last few years, and we have
yet to learn of a single case where they were not
pleased.

in doses of two grains each, every four hours, for

Its Dangers.
An attack of influenza seldom results fatally,
but it is the indirect cause of many deaths. If it
does not result in pneumonia, which is frequently
the case, it leaves its victim with a cough which
lingers on long after every other symptom of the
disease has vanished. The system is thus left in
a weakened condition and is susceptible to almost
every other disease. The influenza can be greatly
lessened in its severity if Chamberlain’s Cough
Remedy is used, and any tendency towards pneu-
monia is promptly checked. There is no medicine
which has met with greater success in the treat-
ment of this disease. It cures the cough and

dition.

The Motor Hog.
In Yankee-land the motorist who rushes his car
through populated districts without any regard for
the safety or convenience of other people is called
a Motor-Hog, or a Road-Hog. It is a fine, appro-
priate term, and tills the bill to a nicety. Unfor-
tunately this human reptile is not confined to
Amur’ka, but is becoming alarmingly frequent in
this country. Victoria, especially, has been
suffering from quite an epidemic of him, and
elderly people are beginning to get “ the jumps”
on account of him. No one objects to the motorist
going as fast as he likes on some of the long,
deserted country roads. In fact, if he is that
kind of motorist, the faster lie goes the
better, as it increases his chance of breaking his
neck over a stray obstruction. But wherever
there is much foot-traffic, or wherever, especially,
there are children about, it is imperative that the
gentlemen with the evil-smelling, buzzing machine
should go slow. It is not sufficient that he should
give a blast of what sounds very like an old,
lonely dog at midnight, and still keep up his
speed. Here is a typical complaint from a well-
known man—Mr. T. G. Watson, clerk of the Vic.
beg. Assembly:—
The lives of heads of families have become a burden to
inem, owing to the risks run by their children from
'otors. On the day previous to this, there were two
ccidents [in his street], one of the injured being my
aughter, and, of course, the callous motorist cleared
ut....1 am quitting my house in consequence of the
P rii... accidents here are of weekly occurrence.
hat is badly needed is a system of numbering and
registration, so that offending drivers can be got
?■’ this manner the road-hog can be traced to
ri sty. England has long since adopted this
oinmon-sense practice, and it is wanted here
if not sooner. Then speed-limits
ould be fixed, and also enforced. Finally, the
Penalties for infractions of the law should include
i.-ieral sprinkling of gaol. Most road-hogs, or
1 eir employers, are well-off, and a fine is simply
ughed at. But 14 days in a cell, on hominy,
la -- t XClted m > rt h on the part of any well-regu-

A BTunicipal valuer has been giving the
t °^. awa y- The new Act in N.S.W. authorises
, tlon ,°)V unimproved land values, and the
w | - ne J dailies, always true to the Fat Loafer,
hirii *1 counc ils were clapping on “as
extt f » as possible, to the. extent of
tion«fi° n ’ thereupon the valuer before men-
wrote to say that the tendency was towards
low, and not high values. The councils, he re-
marked, let out the valuating job by contract, and
it was all to the valuer’s interest to get it over,
quickly. Hence the valuers put down as low an
estimate as possible, for low values mean few
appeals. The valuer’s job doesn’t end until the
appeals are all disposed of, so high valuations
are things to be shunned. This aspect of the
matter probably hadn’t occurred to the councils
when they put the valuing job up to tender, but it
is worth pasting on the bedpost as a reminder
later on.
If Sydney Telegraph is to be relied upon,
the local police have not yet heard of the “Spanish
Prisoner ” swindle, which is a grey and toothless
fraud these days, and has been exposed about a
thousand times. The Telegkaph devotes half-a-
column to an elaborate description of a revival of
the fraud, and concludes—-
What action the police intend to take cannot be ascer-
tained, but most likely they will communicate with the
bank referred to, to test the bona-fides of the statement.
Either the Telegraph or the Police Department
is shockingly ignorant. The Spanish prisoner
fraud has passed the stage of inquiry these many
years. And, anyhow, the financial editor, if he had
been consulted, would have explained with great
enthusiasm that the police search for “the
English Australian Bank in Victoria” —the in-
stitution wherein the prisoner, now allegedly
languishing in a Spanish dungeon, says he left his
money—will certainly be a very long search.
Just about the time that Victoria’s
muddled railways were slaughtering people
wholesale, State Premier Bent was in the midst
of a State’s Frights frenzy, and was pouring forth
a wordy cataract about the alleged mismanage-
ment of the Commonwealth’s Postal Department.
Postmaster-General Mauger resolved the vitu-
peratory waterspout into its essential elements
by heaving at it a hard clod of history. Some
time before Fedev ition, electrical engineer
Howard reported to the Victorian P.O. bosses
that the State’s telephones were just anyhow,
and that it would take £250,000 to make
them eVen fairly shipshape. State Postmaster-
General Watt recommended that the work should
be done, but the slothful State Government just
let things slide ; and if the Commonwealth hadn’t
been forthcoming to undertake the woeful and
expensive job of making them efficient, the Vic-
torian telephones would probably have slid into
the nether scrap heap before this.
From a Fat Man’s diary :
I have always endeavored to instil into my employees
a proper sense of economy to be observed, even in the
smallest matters about the household, as well as in the
warehouse. Consequently, when Birdie —my wife —drew
my attention to the fact that the consumption of soap in
the servants’ bathroom had recently increased from one
bar at s£d., to one bar and three-eights per week, I, with
natural business instincts, determined to investigate the
cause. After making a few resultless inquiries amongst
the servants—by the way, these persons have peculiar
ideas of honor, and of what is due to their masters —I
made up my mind to catch the culprit red-handed. I might
say that my suspicion naturally rested on a youth who
had recently taken the place of another house-boy at a
reduced rate, as his mother was an invalid. This youth
I had actually observed to pluck a flower from
my garden for his buttonhole, and on another
occasion I distinctly saw him light a cigarette
with a match from a box intended for use in
the kitchen—acts which, of course, received the
reprimand they merited. However, I determined to
make sure, and with that object concealed myself in a
cupboard in the corner of the bathroom. I did this with
some little inconvenience, as I have put on considerable
flesh in recent years, especially in the region of the lower
waistcoat. I had not to wait long before a footstep
sounded at the door. I peeped out, expecting to see the
suspect enter. Judge of my surprise, however, when,
instead, our youngest housemaid, a girl of comely
features, made her appearance. Before f could
realise what was about to occur, the girl
had disrobed and proceeded to bathe. Wedged
in an uncomfortable position, my breath was completely
taken away by this unexpected development, and I
became conscious of the extreme awkwardness of the
situation. My desire was to remain quiet and undis-
covered. I will mention that I could not help making a
mental note to the effect that the girl’s figure compared
more than favorably with Birdie’s, though, of course, I
took but little notice. The whole incident would cer-
tainly have been trivial enough, and no one would have
been the wiser, but for the consistent pressure of the cup-
board on my person. Beads of perspiration stood out
on my brow, but I suffered in silence till nature
could hold out no longer. Then a sudden gasp escaped
me. Simultaneously the girl sprang from the bath with
a piercing scream and fell in a fainting-fit on the floor.
Birdie, who was in the vicinity, rushed into the room as
I extricated myself with no little exertion from my con-
finement. She, too, screamed as women do, and of
course misunderstood the whole situation. Explanation
was of no avail. Soon the whole household was at the
door, and James, the coachman (I afterwards discovered
he was an admirer of the girl), so far forgot his position
as to threaten to strike me actually to lay his
hands on my person! A common working man to
address me—a Captain of Industry—with so little respect!
I shall discharge him at the first opportunity I have of
securing a cheaper substitute. The £2 I intended giving
as an advertisement to the hospital had to be diverted to
appeasing the housemaid, and a judicious distribution of
presents, which might have been used to better advan-
tage, was necessary in order to convince the servants
that there was really nothing’ in the incident. Birdie
will not even yet listen to reason. And my intentions
were of the best.
The other day, Greid had put in one of his
very rare appearances at the House —he does
sometimes attend now—and, as usual, announced
his presence by purposeless interruptions. Jabez
Coon was talkin*g about the cap industry in Eng-
land, and produced a cap that was made in Man-
chester for 3s. per dozen. “ That shows the con-
ditions of workers in Great Britain,” said Jabez.
“ Persons engaged in making these caps don’t get
sufficient to live on, and have to be relieved by the
parish rate.” Thereupon :
. Greid : That is one of your wild Collingwood state
ments.
Coon : No, it is not. It is the sworn evidence before an
English royal commission.
Dead silence from Reid. Then :
Johnson : Perhaps the hon. member would be surprised
to learn that the caps were not manufactured in England
at all.
Coon : I have the invoices here, and will show them to
the hon. member if he wishes to see them.
Deader silence from Johnson, who probably felt
like he felt once before when he was fool enough
to sit down on a fallen tree and found that the
fallen tree was an alligator.
The early-rising habit has got the women
of southern Melbourne in an aggravated form just
at present. The vast cow-pasture known as
Albert Park was recently reported to have pro-
duced a mushroom. The news quickly spread, and
now weary pressmen, wandering home in the
small hours, bump up against thin girls, stout
girls, pretty girls, and ugly girls, all
bent on snaring the unsuspecting fungus.
Edith, Florence, and Lily arrange the expedition
days beforehand. They talk nothing else. They
stay awake sat night thinking about it. They
sleep together (Florence in the middle) so that
they can make an early start. The alarm is set
for 4 o’clock. They talk all night, so as to be sure
of getting up. At breakfast time three weary
girls with wet feet, sleepy eyes, straggling hair,
and bad tempers put in an appearance. They
must have struck a bad morning. Nobody seemed
to get any. They’d had a nice walk. Anyway,
they are not much gone on mushrooms after all.
Queensland Lands Department is making
an effort to keep Gent One out of such Land
Court cases as the assessment of rents. It has
suggested to the lessees in some pending cases that
they should conduct their own eases, and if they
consent the department will also do without Shark-
One.

BEWARE OF CHEAP IMITATIONS.
By Royal Warrant to
His Majesty the King.
On which Side of the Line
Do You Buy
r
iBM
Credit is a Commodity—just the same
as goods, and a very expensive Com-
modity. A Merchant who buys credit
as well as goods, sells you his expen=
sive Commodity of credit, goods,
and profit combined.
You too may buy credit, or you may
be paying Cash at a credit-buying or
credit-giving establishment, but in
either case you are paying the credit
high price. The point is, if you can
pay Cash do you prefer to pay for these
credits, and for those losses of non-
payers, for that is what it means, or j ust
to buy the goods without these extra
charges attached. If you do, come over
the line!
Stewart Dawson & Co. manufacture or
buy from the actual Makers, Jewellery,
Precious Stones, Silverware, and fine
Leather Goods for Cash and sell them
to you for Cash . If you buy at Stewart
Dawson’s you will not have to pay
inflated credit prices, nor will you have
to pay the bad debts of others, but you
will purchase at prices 10 per cent, to
20 per cent, lower than at an average
shop—whose business and selling prices
are built up, not on a foundation of
value, but on credits and losses thereon.
Call at the Stewart Dawson Showrooms,
or write us, and realise the revelation
in price-saving.

The immense number of orders for
Frootoids, sent by post direct to the Pro-
prietor, is convincing proof that the
Public appreciate their splendid curing
power over the above-named complaints.
They are elegant in appearance, pleasant
to take, and, what is of the. utmost
importance, are thoroughly reliable in
affording quick relief.
Frootoids are immensely more valu-
able than an ordinary aperient, in so far
that they not only act as an aperient,
but do remove from the blood, tissues, and
internal organs, waste poisonous matter
that is clogging them and choking the
channels that lead to and from them.
The beneficial effects of Frootoids are
evident at once by the disappearance of
headache, the head becoming clear, and
a bright, cheery sense of perfect health
taking the place of sluggish, depressed
feelings, by the liver acting properly, and
by the food being properly digested.
Frootoids are the proper aperient
medicine to take when any Congestion
or Blood Poison is present, or when Con-
gestion of the Brain or Apoplexy is pre-
sent or threatening. They have been
tested, and have been proved to afford
quick relief in such cases when other
aperients have not done any good at all.
It is of the utmost importance that this
should be borne in mind, for in such
cases to take an ordinary aperient is to
waste time and permit of a serious ill-
ness becoming fatal.
Frootoids act splendidly on the liver,
and quickly cure bilious attacks that
“antibilious pills” make worse. Many
people have been made sick and ill by
“antibilious pills” who could have been
cured at once by Frootoids. People
should not allow themselves to be duped
into contracting a medicine-taking habit
by being persuaded to take daily doses
with each meal of so-called indigestion
cures that do NOT cure. Frootoids
have been subjected to extensive tests,
and have in every case proved success-
ful in completely curing the complaints
named.
The ordinary adult dose of Frootoids,
of which there are 72 in a bottle, is 2 to
4 —more or less as required—taken, pre-
ferably at bedtime, when constipated,
or at the commencement of any other
disease requiring an aperient, as an
auxilliary with the special medicine
necessary for the case. A constipated
habit of body will be completely cured
if the patient will on each occasion,
when suffering, take a dose of Frootoids,
instead of an ordinary aperient; making
the interval between the taking of each
dose longer and the dose smaller. The
patient thus gradually becomes indepen-
dent of Aperient Medicines.

A Vic. country storekeeper recently re-
ceived this letter from a man who was late :
Wuk Wuk,
?/4/’OB.
Dear S'r,—T made a start for Bullumwall last week,
and got as far as Bairnsdale, but as I was just recoverin'/
from an attack of influenza, I tried to cure it witii
whisky, the result being that I left Bairnsdale in a state
bordering on the horrors, minus the cash I went in
with. Such being the case, it was impossible to go am
further, and as 1 can get credit in “ Wuk Wuk,” thither
I returned to recover, accompanied by a legion of blue
devil. Since' then I have disposed of all the “Blue
Devils "and other fiery and inartistic forms and phan-
toms which forced their rather too sociable but gruesome
company upon me. lam now painting a cart, and have
the iron here tq make a tank afterwards, but will await,
the receipt of your answer before going any further, as
to whether you would sooner I came up, or, if you have
anyone else handy who would suit you better than my-
self for the job. As I make it my rule in life to keep my
word sooner or later, under any circumstances, from a
moral point of view, apart from the legal aspects of the
case.
I have enough work of various kinds on hand to last,
me for six months, but if you decide on my “ coming up,"
I will cut across the bush so as to avoid the rocks and
shoals on the route via Bairnsdale.
Apologising for any inconvenience T may have caused,
I can only say in conclusion, the spirit was willing but
the flesh was weak.
I remain,
■Yours respectfully,
Johnson Tank.
The storekeeper replied to Mr. Tank, bidding
hint tie up his blue devils in the public-house
yard at Wuk Wuk and come along.

The Man Who Slammed the
Door
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
We have slammed, and boltr d, and barred the door in
the face of the Colonies !—W. Churchill.
rriHERE’S a sound of jubilation on a budly-
' I sweated shore—
_L From all the mills of Manchester ascends
a joyous roar,
For the “ Freetrade ” superstition
Is just slipping to Perdition—
It is rolling in the gutter with The Man Who
Slammed The Door !
His name was Winston Churchill— now it’s
ordinary Mud,
In spite of his “ connections,” and his cer-u-le-an
blood !
He's The Man Who Shimmed the Door—
He won’t do it any more,
For Manchester has dropped him with a heavy,
sickening thud !
Yes; the mighty politician who thought Chamber-
lain a bore,
And on the beastly colonies set very little store,
Is standing in the street
With unpleasantly cold feet—
Feeling like a shipwrecked mariner upon a barren
shore.
But the Sydney Morning Herald and the Tele-
graph both swore
That “the Freetrade cause 7 aust triumph—it is
solid to the core ! ”
Now they rise up to declare
That the “ cause” was never there
When the sweated cotton-spinners “chucked”
The Man Who Slammed the Door !
One great, SOLID FACT emerges from this
subdititious lore ;
Like a ton of granite rock it stands upon the fiscal
floor ;
It’s a stone that marks the death
Of their drear old Shibboleth—
The remarkable admission of The Man Who
Slammed The Door !
All the signs are plainly written —you can read
them bv the score—
Hip and thigh is Freetrade smitten from the
Stietlands to the Nore ;
England’s hacking down her Joss—
And it won’t be any loss !
And with mighty slams is slamming all The Men
Who Slammed The Door !
N.S.W.
O. C. CABOT.
A Son of Ishmael.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
THE little crowd which had gathered
about the window of the chemist’s shop,
flattening noses against the grimy glass,
gave way grudgingly before the Hibernian per-
suasiveness of a top-heavy police constable, and
reluctantly dispersed.
The Brobdingnagian and inflammatory bowls
generally associated with the rites of the humbler
disciples of Galen threw broad splashes of
amber, green, and crimson on the glistening pave-
ments. The rain pattered persistently, with the
disagreeable fluency of a street orator. Five
minutes more dragged slowly by, and Mr. Samuel
Sharp, his coat collar turned up about his throat,
his whole attire soddenly suggestive of a recently-
resuscitated suicide, sidled to the door of the
chemist’s shop and darted an impatient and
inquisitive glance at the dilapidated figure of
Mr. Jeremiah Maunder, who was directing a
steady, vituperative stream of eloquence into the
ears of an unsympathetic dispenser of healing
drugs.
Presently, Mr. Maunder, wearying of his un-
appreciative audience, turned slowly op a worn-
out heel, and passed into the cheerless night.
“Blirne, Jerry, wotever ’ave you bin a-doin’
of? inquired the exasperated Mr. Sharp. “Blowed
if I weren’t beginnin’ to think as ’ow you really
wos the missin’ link, as I’ve so orfen told you
afore, an’ they’d took an’ put you inter meth’lated
spernts fer a curiosity. ’Ave they bin di-sectin’
of you, Jerry ? ”
Mr. Maunder shuddered.
That ’ere’s the larst fit I’m goin’ ter ’ave, even
of you wos ter go down on yer bended knees an’
oogan pray of me, Sam Sharp,” he said, with
ocision. “The soap’s bad enuff —I don’t get rid
o the taste fur days an’ days —an’ when it comes
or the pizen them murderers give a pore sick
(oan, Lor’ lumine ! ” He paused for a moment,
nen wandered off into the flowery fields of
metaphor, culling with a loving care the choicest
)ectives that bloom as he went upon his way.
• U IS . yus; I know orl about that,” said Sam,
mpatiently. “But wot did the old lady an’
Vf' k lan Ve ou * UI y er suff’rin’s ?”

om his lurid ramblings, and laughed the hard,
i ter laugh of the disillusioned and misanthropic
Superior Person.

ea Jerry that some scheme of iniquity was
evolving itself in the shadowy recesses of his
comrade’s brain.
I here was a moment’s poignant silence. Then
Mr. Sharp resumed. ‘ I’ve got it—a fust-class
lay ! Jerry, you know old Mr. Ebenezer Manuel,
the rich Jew wot lives in Collins-street ? ”
Mr. Maunder nodded.
“I’ve ’eard of ’im,” he responded, with a strong
suspicion of anti-Semiticism in his tones. “It
ain tno use, Sam ; ’e won’t do nothink fur pore,
ard-workin’ Christians. If me an’ you wos Yids
it would be different.”
“ Necessity’s the mother o’ inventions, Jerry,”
said Sam, reprovingly. “ Orl ’er kids might be
still-born fur the good they does you. Listen : I
want you to be a Jew. Your conk’s jest O.K.
It’s a pity you ain’t got more sense, but that don’t
matter, as it ’appens, ’cos you’ve lost yer mem’ry
—dunno who you are, or why you’re alive an’
walkin’about like a warmed-up corpse so as ter
save diggin’ expenses. Orl you want is to git
back to Jerusalem, where the artichokes come
from —twig ? ”
Mr. Maunder came to a dead stop, breathing
vengefully.
“Ho, yus; you’re mighty clever, Sam Sharp,”
he began, with biting emphasis.
‘I know I am—don’t you wish you wos me?”
interrupted Sam. “Don’t try the idea
in too sudden-like, Jerry, or p’raps it might bring
on another o’ them attacks you’re so subject to.
Come along an’ ’ave a drop o’ beer. I’ll explain
things to you as we go. Worked properly, this
oughter mean a fi’pund-note between the pair o’
us.”
Jerry’s strained dignity relaxed at the mention
of refreshment. He suffered Sam to take him by
the arm and lead him gently in the direction of an
adjacent hostelry.
About ten o’clock in the forenoon of the follow-
ing day, Mr. Ebenezer Manuel, a fresh-colored,
portly old gentleman of benevolent aspect, quitted
his house and walked leisurely down Collins-
street in the direction of the Treasury Gardens.
He had not gone far before he was accosted by the
shorter of two men who, for the past hour, had
been lingering in the roadway directly opposite
his residence.
“ Beggin' your pardon, sir,” said this individual,
whose foxy face looked out from beneath the brim
of a battered hat, “but might I make so bold
as ter arst if your name ain’t Mr. Ebenezer
Manuel?”
The old gentleman fixed a pair of gold-rimmed
glasses precariously on the bridge of his nose, and
beamed upon his interlocutor.
“ Why, yes,” he replied, “I am he. Is there
anything I can do for you ?”
Mr. Sharp indicated the drooping, disreputable
form of his grey-bearded companion with a back-
ward jerk of the thumb.
“ It’s ’im I’ve come to tork to you about, sir,”
he said, in a hoarse whisper.
“ I’m a lumper wot lives at Port Melbourne
(Emerald-street), an’ crool ’ard work it is. Three
times I’ve broke me leg, an’ the larst time the
doctor sez as ’ow ’e wondered it weren’t me ’eart.
Well, sir, two days ago I ’ad jist knocked off
slavin’ aboard the steamer London, an’ wos goin’
’ome, when I see this pore old man standin’ there
on the quay, sobbin’somethin’bitterly. ‘ Wot’s
wrong, matey?’ -I sez, fur I couldn’t bear ter see
’im, ’e looked that sorrerful. Fur a minute ’e
couldn’t answer me back, but at larst ’e sez
through ’is tears, ‘ Jerusalem’, an’points at the
steamer as I’ad jist come off of. ‘No, mate,’ sez
I, not understandin’ ’im— ‘ that ’ere’s the London,
mixed cargo, from Durban.’ ’E shakes ’is pore
’ead at me. ‘Jerusalem,’ ’e sez, impatient-like ;
then again, ‘Jerusalem ! my ’appy ’ome ! ’ 1
“Well, sir, I stood there not knowin’ wot ter
do, when up walks Cholly White, a friend o’
mine wot works in the Customs. ‘ Hullo ! ’ ’e
sez, ‘ so you’ve struck the Wandering Jew, Sam ?
Rum old boy, ain’t ’e. Every day fur the larst
week ’e’s bin down ’ere arstin’ everybody the way
ter Jerusalem.’ ‘ Who is he, Cholly ?’I sez. ‘I
dunno,’ sez ’e; ‘wot’s more, ’e dunno ’isself.
Told me ’e’d bin livin’ in a grotto, an’ wanted ter
end ’is un’appy days in the ’appy land, or some
sech a place. Said as ’ow ’e ’oped ter git there
fur the farst o’ kippers.’ ”
“Yom Kippur,” corrected Mr. Manuel, who
had been listening open-mouthed to Sam’s veracious
narrative. “ Dear me, this is really a most extra-
ordinary case. From what you tell me I should
gather that he is like so many of us whose fondest
wish is to repossess the country from which we
have been outcast and exiled for so many cen-
turies. He has the true Semitic features ; but I
wonder if, peradventure. he is mentally afflicted
—he has a strange, wild look. Let me speak a
few words to him.”
He addressed the uneasy Mr. Maunder in a
language which that terrified person presumed to
be Hebrew.
“It ain’t no good, sir,” said Sam, sadly. “The
pore old man’s deaf. Unless you ’oilers at ’im
with two-’undered ’orse power, ’e won’t ’ear you.
I’ve got clergyman’s sore throat meself with bel-
lerin at ’im like a Bull o’ Basin.”
Mr. Manuel shook his head. It was quite clear
he couldn’t hold an animated conversation with
Mr. Maunder in broad daylight and in a public
thoroughfare.
“You see, sir,” resumed Sam, “not likin’to
leave ’im where them imps o’ boys might make a
mock of ’is grey ’airs, I took ’im ’ome with me. A
kind action, I sez ter meself, is its own reward,
Sam Sharp, so ’ang the expense. I ’ad a knuckle
o’ cold pork fur tea that night, an’, silly-like, furst
thing I done wos ter arst ’im if ’e wouldn’t ’ave
none, an’ the langwidge ’e see fit ter make use of
would ’ave done credit to a born Christian—the
on’y time I’ve ’eard ’im tork really free an’
familiar, so to speak.”
“You interest me more than I can tell you,”
said old Mr. Manuel, polishing his glasses with a
silk handkerchief. “It is a most remarkable
story. I should like to speak to the Rabbi about
him.”
“Beggin’ your pardon, sir,” interjected Sam,
with commendable promptness, “but the furst
person I went to wos the Rabbit, an’ ’e says, ‘ My
’eart bleeds fur ’im, but I’m a pore man meself,
an’ wot kin I do ? Take ’im to Mr. Ebenezer
Manuel, a true-’earted, noble-sperrited gen’leman
wot won’t see a feller-creetur suffer without re-
lievin’ of ’im.’ Them wos ’is very words.”
Mr. ManSel looked gratified.
“That was an extremely kind thing to say. Of
course I shall be only too glad to help the poor
fellow. Now, unfortunately, I have an important
engagement I must keep. Both of you come to my
house to-morrow morning at eleven o’clock, and in
the meantime I will consult with an excellent
friend of mine, a poor man as far as this world’s
goods go, but one who does an exemplary work
among the necessitous in the slum quarters of this
great city. What is your name again, my friend ? ”
“Sam Sharp, sir, Emerald-street, Fort Mel-
bourne,” replied that altruistic lumper.
“ Well,” continued the old gentleman, pulling
■out a knitted purse; “here is a sovereign ; see
that your charge lacks for nothing. I shall hope
to more than indemnify you at no distant date
