Sam’s eyes glittered greedily.
“ Never mind me, sir,” he said, with self-
denial. “ Live on others an’ you’ll live well an’
be ’appy, as my pore old father used to say.
There ain’t nothin’ I wouldn’t do furaYid—Jew,
I meantersay. Why, even when I wos a nipper,
so ’igh, I wos never so ’appy as when I wos
a-playin’ on the Jew-’arp’ ——
Mr. Manuel cut the reminiscences short:
“ I can’t stop any longer. Mind, eleven o’clock
to-morrow morning sharp.”
“Yus, sharp’s the word,” muttered Sam,
exultantly, as he watched the old gentleman’s
retreating form. He fell into an ecstatic reverie,
from which he was rudely aroused by the voice of
his afflicted comrade.
“’Ere, ’and over that thick-un wot’smine, Sam,
d’ye ’ear me?”
“ ’Opes to identify me by me kind-’earted sym-
pathy. ’As a strange, wild look. True emetic
features,” murmured the dreamy Mr. Sharp,
speaking in sections, his eyes fixed in far-away
fashion on a little drift of fleecy cloud. “ Blime,
Jerry, ’aven’t I orfen told you as ’ow it alius did
make me feel ill on’y to look at that phiz o’ yers?”
“ Are you a-goin’ ter fork out that ’ere suv’rin
wot wos give me, or are you not ? ” persisted Mr.
Maunder, with ominous emphasis.
“I wonder at you, Jerry,” said Sam, reproach-
fully. “’Ow should I ever be able to look that
good old man in the face agin, arter ’im trustin’
of me, too? Didn’t you ’ear ’im say as you wos
meant ter be inflicted ? No, no ; a bit o’ Kosher
beef or a short-bread I don’t mind buyin’ you,
but ”
Here Mr. Maunder interrupted the speaker
with many and profanely-worded doubts as to the
legitimacy of the Sharp pedigree from time im-
memorial.
“Now then, not so much o’ that kind o’ tork.
Move yerselves along,” said a leisurely policeman.
They moved themselves along—and instinctively
they did it towards a pub.
• • * *
The chiefest of Jerry’s afflictions was a
perennial and feverish thirst, and to this Sam
ministered at intervals all the afternoon. Un-
fortunately he caught the complaint himself.
Towards’nightfall the conspirators-lurched with
devious tread into a cheap eating-house in
Lonsdale-street. They took their seats oppo-
site an elderly man with venerable grey beard
and nose modelled on the familiar Ghetto lines.
Him Mr. Maunder fixed with a glassy and male-
volent eye.
“ Sooner than be a Yid,” he began, irrelevantly
addressing the circumambient air, “ I’d be a Sal-
vation Sal, or a —or a ’eathen ’lndu.”
He smote the table with a broken-hafted knife.
Sam Sharp remonstrated.
“Shut yer fat’ead, Jerry,” he said thickly. “If
y’ are tight y’ needn’t let everybody know it.”
Mr. Maunder disdainfully ignored this asper-
sion on his spotted character.
“Sooner than be a Jew, I’d be a—a anythink,”
he announced oratorically to all and sundry ; then,
with.a sudden lapse into rudimentary verse, “Git
a lump o’ pork, stick it on a fork, an’ give ”
“Gif orders, blease, shentlemans,”said the per-
suasive voice of the Dago waiter, who was buzzing
from customer to customer like an enormous and
bloated blue-bottle.
Mr. Maunder regarded him sternly ; then his
lustreless eye wandered across to the patient old
man, busy with his humble meal of bread and
butter and fried fish.
“Sausages an’ mashed,” he demanded, with
needless ferocity ; “ an’ porky uns, mind.”
The old man gave him a quick, keen glance,
half-pitying, half-contemptuous, then he rose,
and, cup in one hand, plate in the other, sought a
vacant table at the remote end of the room.
Mr. Maunder laughed a sneering, raucous
laugh.
****
Precisely at eleven o’clock the next morning,
Sam and Jerry presented themselves at the trades-
men’s entrance of Mr. Manuel’s house.
“Boss in, miss?” inquired the former ingratia-
tingly of the buxom, dark-eyed damsel who
attended the bell.
“This way, please,” she rejoined, and led the
amateur philanthropist and the object of his
solicitude up a flight of stairs, down a short
passage, and into a room where Mr. Manuel was
seated at an escritoire immersed in correspondence.
“ Ah,” he said, looking up and flicking the
glasses from his nose, “It’s you, is it? Good;
sit down, please. Salome, ask Mr. Moss to be so
kind as to spare me a few minutes, will you ? ”
The girl left the room, and the old gentleman
again became engrossed in his letters. No sound
broke the silence save Mr. Maunder’s troublesome
breathing. Presently the door opened, and——
Sam did not stand on the order of his going.
He just went. He made swift time through the
passage, and took most of the stairs at a leap.
For a brief moment Mr. Maunder’s petrified gaze
rested on the placid, venerable face of the new-
comer, and through a haze he remembered that he
had seen that face before—the evening before, in
fact. He remembered, too, that Sam carried the
remnants of the trust money. Breathing heavily
he upheaved himself, and with all the grace of an
elephant he also fled in broken boots through the
portal.
That night he made fid. by having a fit in
Collingwood.
LAWRENCE B. JUPP.
“ Nym ” : The despised Nanny and Wil-
liam goat are coming into fashion again. Those
who have partaken of goats’ flesh speak highly in
its favor, while Nanny’s milk, for richness and
flavor (not to say purity), shames Poley’s contribu-
tion to the milk-pail every time. Also, the
quantity Nanny is capable of giving would sur-
prise those who scorn the object of many a comic
sketch. Round Bendigo (Vic.) and other large
centres, the goat population is now affectionately
preserved, thanks to the lesson taught last drought
time, when the cows failed to come home, and
only tinned milk was available. Then tea-drinkers
bethought them of Nanny, grazing on jam-tins
and old paper wrappers op the rubbish shrines
around, and went forth to collect the lost animal.
Peaceable church folks who had suffered
adult immersion fought over the ownership of the
whiskered rovers hitherto looked upon as mere van-
dals. Now Nanny and thekids hold prideof place in
many a householder’s heart. It is a noticeable
fact that tuberculosis is rarely seen in children
brought up on goats’ milk, while those reared on
Strawberry’s doubtful dole show the disease fre-
quently. Although it is suspected that goats’
flesh is used in Bendigo, and other places, in
making up sausages, and occasionally as joints,
few folks would deign to dine on Nanny’s limbs,
however tastefully cooked, if they knew what
they were eating. But that is mere prejudice.
During the. drought, when butchers’ meat was
scant and dear, certain pastrycooks used rabbits’
flesh, run through a mincing machine, instead of
the unprocurable, and often doubtful, cow
sausage-meat. The ructions certain saucy Satur-
day-night patrons created when they learnt of the
change are remembersome. There were even
fights, and nose-blood was shed. Despite all
critics, I say that Nanny and Bunny are deserv-
ing of most kindly cultivation for culinary
purposes.
